Summary 0:
Collective investment undertakings and marketing communications to investors — common rules for investors
Collective investments in collective investments in social entrepreneurship
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — common requirements for managers of collective investments and on marketing communications addressed to investors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
    б  б  г  
It sets out common rules to ensure that investors receive information on collective investments* and marketing communication to them.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
alternative investment fund managers (AIFMs, EuVECA managers and EuSEF managers)
UCITS management companies
collective investment funds (UCITS), including any UCITS which has not designated a UCITS management company
EuVEC managers
EUSEF (European Union Social Entrepreneurship Investment Fund) managers
Mutual fund managers
Directive 2014/65/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 15 May 2014 on markets in financial instruments and amending Directive 2002/92/EC and Directive 2011/61/EU on markets for collective investment in social enterprises
Directives 2014/64/EU and 2014/66/EU concerning the supervision of cross-border activities of managers of investment funds
Regulatory framework for the management of collective investment funds and managers of UCITS
Regulations (EU, Euratom and Euratom)
Regulator (EU), Euratom (EU).
Regulating framework for marketing communications for investors in collective investment firms
Regulators must ensure that:
investors can obtain information on the investment strategies presented to them in marketing communications, including information on their rights and information on investor rights;
the marketing communications must specify where, how and in which language investors can obtain summarised information on investors’ rights and they must clearly state that investors have the right to terminate the arrangements made for marketing.
The European Commission must adopt implementing standards by 2 February 2021.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  — collective investments
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 852/2019 of 12 January 2019 laying down common rules on collective investment undertaking and on communication to investors (OJ L 183, 14.1.2019, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS

Commission Implementing Regulation (EU-EU) 2018/856 of 2 February 2018 on market monitoring of managers' fees and charge levied by the competent authorities for supervision of mutual fund managers and mutual fund investors (Commission Regulation Euratom 2018/1856 of 3 February 2018)

Summary 1:
Counterfeiting of goods — EU rules on market surveillance and enforcement of penalties
Counterfeitting of goods – EU rules to combat counterfeiting
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/426 — EU measures to prevent counterfeit products from entering the EU market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries take effective and proportionate measures to combat counterfeit products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products that are intended to be sold in the EU.
It does not apply to:
electronic products;
electromagnetic devices;
machinery;
vehicle parts and components;
appliances;
foodstuffs;
medical devices.
National market surveillance authorities
Each EU country must set up a national market surveillance authority to carry out market surveillance.
The authority must:
identify products that do not comply with the EU harmonisation legislation;
establish a national surveillance strategy;
conduct checks and controls on products entering the market;
assess the impact of non-compliance on the EU’s economic and social cohesion;
ensure that the measures taken are proportionate, effective and dissuasive;
provide information to the European Commission, other EU countries and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) on the results of market surveillance;
coordinate with EU countries’ competent authorities.
EU countries must also ensure that the competent authorities have the necessary resources to implement their national surveillance strategies.
Procedure
The competent authorities of an EU country may request assistance from the competent authority of another EU country if they believe that a product does not meet the requirements of the legislation.
If an authority in one EU country receives a request for assistance, it must inform the applicant authority about the measures that have been taken or which are planned to be taken.
Mutual assistance
A mutual assistance request may be made by an authority of a non-EU country to a competent authority in another country.
Regulatory cooperation
The European Commission has set up an administrative cooperation group to assist EU countries in the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/65/EU on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States and repealing Directive 2009/142/EC (see summary) has applied since 1 January 2014.

Summary 2:
Retransmission of television and radio programmes — rights in retransmission and authorisation of retransmissions of programmes (Directive 2019/24/EU)
Retransmitting of television or radio programmes is one of the most important means of promoting cultural and linguistic diversity and social cohesion, and of increasing access to information. However, the rights in these activities are subject to the exclusive right of communication to the public. This directive aims to ensure that these rights are respected.
ACT
Directive 2011/23/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 March 2011 on the rights of the holders of copyright and related rights in broadcasting and retransmitting programmes (recast) and repealing Directive 93/83/EEC
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
   ˆ ˙ ˘ ˌ ˗ ː ˅  
It aims to improve the protection of the rights held by the rights holders of works and other protected subject matter (i.e. authors, producers and other rightholders) in the exercise of their rights in the reproduction of their works and related subject matter.
KEY POINTS
The directive lays down rules on the right of the right holders of the exclusive rights of communication of works to the general public to retransmit their works on television and to transmit their works to other organisations.
The rights in works are harmonised, inter alia, through Directive 2001/29/EC and Directive 2006/115/EC (see summary), which provide for a high level of protection for rights holders.
Rights holders must obtain authorisation from the rights holder before they can use their works for the purpose of broadcasting.
These rights must be granted by the holder of exclusive rights to the right to the transmission of a work or subject matter of their choice.
In addition, rights holders must receive appropriate remuneration for their rights.
This directive allows rights holders to transfer their rights either to a broadcasting organisation or to a collective management organisation, thereby allowing them to have a direct share in the remunerity paid by the operator of a retransmitted service.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE ENTER INTO FORCE?
It entered into force on 7 June 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 7 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
 ‘Directive 2013/13/EU’ is the first EU directive to introduce a new framework for the protection and licensing of rights in online services that are ancillary to broadcasts.
For more information, see:
‘Rights of rights holders’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 3:
The.eu country code top-level domain (ccTLD) and its available variants in other scripts (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011). The.eu TLD aims to support the digital single market, to build an online Union identity and to encourage cross-border online activities.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 184/2011 of 19 October 2011 implementing the.eu Country Code Top-Level Domain (‘CCTLD’).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a registry for domain names in the European Union (EU).
It allows EU citizens to register a domain name, regardless of their place of residence, from 19 October 2019.
KEY POINTS
Registry
The registry is managed by the European Commission.
The Commission designates an entity as the registry following the completion of the procedure referred to in paragraphs 1 and 2 of the regulation.
A multistakeholder advisory group is appointed by the Commission on the basis of an open, non-discriminatory and transparent procedure. The group may include one representative of stakeholders established outside the EU.
In accordance with its supervisory activities, the Commission may convey specific instructions to the registry for correcting or improving the organisation, administration and management of the.EU TLD.
Registration and revocation of domain names
The registrar is responsible for revoking domain names belonging to non-EU citizens who are not citizens of the EU and who do not live in an EU country.
Disputes between domain name holders are settled by bodies located in the EU applying the relevant national law, without prejudice to rights and obligations recognised by the EU countries or by international instruments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/2021 has applied since 19 October 2018.
Regulatory (EU, Euratom) 2019/2022 has applied from 19 November 2019. It repeals Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratl and Euratom.
BACKGROUND
   
The.EU country code TLD is one of a number of sub-TLDs that are part of the hierarchical structure of the domain name system (DNS) which ensure an interoperable system of unique identifiers, available throughout the world, on any application and any network.
For more information, see:
‘‘.eu’’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Domain name: the name of a geographical or geographical area which affects the EU's political or territorial organisation.

Summary 4:
Cohesion Fund for Europe (CEF) 2018-2025 — funding for cross-border projects in the transport, energy and digital sectors (TEN-T) and the digital single market (TECHNIC)
The European Commission sets out strategic objectives for 2025 with a view to optimising investment in digital connectivity infrastructure. The European Union (EU) Cohesion Fund provides the European Commission with the financial resources to achieve these objectives.
ACT
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/2025 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2018 on the European Union Cohesion fund for Europe
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the eligibility and award criteria for the European Regional Development Fund (ERCF) for projects to be funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) in the field of transport and energy.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 182/2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The CEF supports the development of infrastructure in the following sectors:
transport,
energy,
digital single market,
information and communication technologies (ICTs),
information society,
science and technology,
health and social policy,
education and training,
environmental and rural development.
Eligible actions
EU countries are eligible to apply for funding if they:
have a national multiannual investment strategy in the fields covered by the CEF;
have achieved a significant improvement in their economic, social and environmental performance;
make a significant contribution to the EU’s cohesion policy.
The Commission determines the number of projects eligible for funding based on the criteria set out in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 20 October 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU Cohesion funds are the main source of funding for EU countries’ efforts to improve their economic and social cohesion.
For more information, see:
‘Cohesion funds’ on the EIB's website.
* KEY TERMS
Cohesion fund: the European Fund for Regional Development is the EU's main instrument for providing financial support to EU countries' efforts to develop their economies.

Summary 5:
Hosting service providers and users’ trust in the online environment — transparency and mutual recognition of information
Hosting services providers and customers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 on the transparency of information on the identification and removal of terrorist content
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that hosting service providers (HSPs) and users have the same level of transparency when identifying and removing terrorist content on the internet.
It also aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s digital single market.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all hosting services in the EU. It does not apply to content provided by third parties, such as the internet hosting service provider (HSP).
It does not affect the right of users to access the content they wish to see or to have access to.
To identify and remove terrorist content, the competent authorities of an EU country must:
identify the content as terrorist material;
decide whether the material constitutes ‘terrorist content’;
take the necessary measures to remove the content;
establish a contact point in the country where the content provider has their main establishment or where their legal representative resides.
The competent authorities must notify the service provider of the reason for the decision and the time of the removal or disabling of the content.
If the competent authority considers that the content does not constitute terrorist material, it may issue a ‘removal order’, which is issued by the EU country in which the content is hosted.
When a competent authority issues a removal order, it must inform the service providers of the reasons for the order. The reasons provided should not imply the disclosure of sensitive information which could jeopardise ongoing investigations.
Service providers that have taken action or were required to take action pursuant to this regulation in a given calendar year should make publicly available annual transparency reports containing information about action taken in relation to the identification or removal of terrorism content. These reports should be made available to the public, including the names and contact details of competent authorities.
In the event that a service provider fails to comply with a competent authorities’ request, it has the right to an effective remedy, which may include:
the removal of the service or the disabling of access to that service or access to which has been disabled;
the refund of the cost of the services or the loss of the revenue from the service;
a ban on the use of that service.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2013/13/EU introduced a new framework for the transparency and communication of information concerning the identification of terrorist material and the removal of such material. It was amended by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 on the protection of personal data.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘Transparency of information’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Terrorist content: material that is intended to cause death, serious injury or damage to the physical, mental or moral integrity of an individual or group.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 of 13 April 2017 on the Transparency of Information on the Identification and Removal of Terrorist Content (OJ L 157, 14.4.2017, pp. 1-2)
RELATED ACTS
Directives 2013/23/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 March 2013 on the Protection of Personal Data on the Internet and repealing Directives 2002/36/EC and 2002/37/EC (Directive 2002/35/EC) (Directives 2003/36 /EC, 2003/37 /EC and 2003/38/EC, Directives 2001/33/EC), 2001/34/EC of the Federal Republic of Germany and 2001/35 /EC (Regulation 2001/32/EC). See consolidated version.
Communication from the Commission to the EU Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions — Towards a European Digital Single Market (COM(2018) 609 final, 15.5.2018)

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 (FPRI 2021-2020)
Framework Programmes for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2027 (FRR/2027)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — framework programme for research and innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the framework for the EU’s Horizon Europe programme, which aims to invest in R&I to boost jobs, growth, and investment in the European Union (EU).
It establishes the European Innovation Council (EIC), the European Commission and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The FPRI covers research, innovation and technological development (R&I) in the areas of democracy and governance, culture, cultural heritage and creativity, social and economic development and the environment.
The programme is divided into 4 pillars:
Global Challenges and European Industrial Competitiveness (GCT);
Widening participation and strengthening the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF);
InvestEU.
Funding
The European Commission, the EIC and the EIB are responsible for the implementation of the programme.
Eligible actions and ethical principles
The EIC, the Commission and EIB must ensure that beneficiaries:
provide open access to peer-reviewed scientific publications;
promote participation of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in research;
encourage the development of innovative products and services;
ensure that beneficiaries have the right to participate in the research and development activities of the EIT.
Procedures for awarding grants
The Commission, EIC or EIB may award grants in the form of grants, co-financing or other forms of financial support to eligible actions.
They may also award grants to non-profit legal entities or to legal entities established in specified associated or other non-EU countries in addition to EU countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The framework programme entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
Horizon Europe (European Commission).

Summary 7:
The European Space Agency (ESA) (European Union Agency for Space Technology (EUASAT)).
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — the establishment of ESA
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The decision establishes ESA as the European Union agency for space technology (ESA).
The agreement sets out ESA’s tasks, including:
assessing the space weather risks and corresponding user needs;
ensuring the provision of user-driven space weather services;
improving EU countries’ capabilities to provide the SWE services.
KEY POINTS
ESA is responsible for:
monitoring space weather;
providing SST services to EU countries;
developing the technical specifications for GOVSATCOM;
advising the European Commission on the use of SST;
supporting the development of the EGNOS satellite communication system.
ESA has a number of tasks, such as:
examining the geostationary satellite system (GSS), the European satellite navigation system (ESN) and the European Earth observation satellite (EEO), as well as the EEO satellite, the European Space Communications System (ESCS) and EEO satellites.
It is also responsible for providing the Commission with information on the activities of ESA, ESA and ESA countries.
EU countries are responsible for ensuring that ESA has sufficient resources to carry out its tasks.
EASA has a budget of €2.5 billion for the period 2017-2021.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND THE REGULATION APPLY?
Directive (EC) No 852/2017 of the European Parliament and of the Council has applied since 1 January 2017.
Directives (EU, EU, EU countries and the Commission) have to incorporate the decision and the decision into national law by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
European Space Agency
European Commission website
ESA (European Commission).

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Hubs (EDI) 2021-2027 — cybersecurity, trust and digital innovation — European Cybersecurity and Trust
European digital innovation hubs (EDI) 2021–2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 on the European Digital Innovation Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European digital innovation programme to support digital innovation in the European Union (EU).
It aims to tackle the challenges posed by the digital transformation and review policies affected by it.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EDI programme is designed to support the development of a digital society.
It is a multiannual programme for a period of 7 years to align its duration with that of the Multiannual financial framework laid down in Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
ensure that the EU’s financial interests are protected against fraud, corruption and any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the EU;
promote cybersecurity and trust in the digital economy;
encourage the use of new technologies to improve the security and integrity of the information and communication technologies (ICTs) used by businesses and consumers;
support the development and use of innovative products and services in the information society;
provide information, communication, publicity, policy support and dissemination to the public and the media;
facilitate the exchange of best practices and best practices in the IT sector;
contribute to the fight against fraud and corruption and other illegal activities affecting the EU's financial interests.
Support for digital innovation
The programme is funded by the European Social Fund for Social Innovation (ESI), the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the Erasmus+ programme.
Eligible actions
Actions financed under the programme must meet the following criteria:
they must be user-friendly, should comply with the law by default and should provide consumers with more choice and more information, in particular regarding the quality of products, services and products.
They must also:
be based on the best available technology;
have a clear and measurable objective;
notify the public of the results of their work;
be coordinated with the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) and other relevant national authorities;
inform the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the measures taken by the EDI.
The actions supported by EDI are complementary to those supported by the Social Fund Plus (ESF+), the EU Social Fund, the European Rural Development Fund and the Horizon Europe programme, and Horizon Europe.
Implementing acts
The regulation sets out the implementing acts for the programme. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 31(2) of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has set up a digital innovation strategy for the EU.
For further information, see:
Digital innovation (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
    И  барскиганска на грашка 2021/694
  Егода 2021-2020 (OJ L 183, 1.1.2021, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 9:
EU support for the European Economic and Social Network (EEN)
EU support towards the European European Network (ESN)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/515 on the European Union’s investment in services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the objectives of the European Investment Bank (EIB) for the period 2019-2021.
It aims to support the development of European Union (EU, Euratom) services by providing financial support to small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The objective of the EIB is to help EU businesses create jobs and grow across borders, offer a wider choice of services at better prices, and maintain high standards for consumers and workers.
The EIB supports the European Commission, the European Parliament and the Council in implementing the EEN.
In order to achieve this objective, the EBI supports SMEs in the following sectors:
services:
small and medium sized enterprises in the services sector;
information and communication technology (ICT) sectors;
agriculture, forestry, fisheries and aquaculture;
biotechnology;
telecommunications and information and communication technologies (ICT);
health and safety;
food and consumer protection;
education and training;
transport;
science and technology;
the environment;
energy and transport;
digital innovation;
other sectors.
EIB supports these sectors by:
providing financial support;
facilitating access to financial instruments and budgetary guarantees;
supporting research and development;
encouraging cross-border cooperation between EU countries;
promoting the use of information and communications technologies;
improving cooperation with non-EU countries in the information society;
developing the capacity of EU countries’ authorities to exchange information and information on EU services;
ensuring the protection of intellectual property rights;
enhancing cooperation between national authorities in the fields such as agriculture, forestry and fisheries;
strengthening the role of the Commission in the fight against organised crime;
helping EU citizens and businesses to improve their access to the EU services market;
raising awareness of the importance of EU services to consumers, workers and consumers in the EU;
increasing cooperation between the Commission and EU countries to improve the quality of services offered by EU businesses;
boosting the competitiveness of EU businesses in the digital economy;
creating jobs and growth across borders;
fostering innovation and promoting innovation in the sectors covered by the European Digital Innovation Hubs under the Digital Europe Programme established by Regulation (EU), and by the InvestEU Advisory Hub.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 19 March 2019 and had to become law in the Member States by 19 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world's largest single market for goods and services.
For more information, see:
The European Commission's website on the EU Investment Bank.

Summary 10:
Assessment of the most dangerous firearms and ammunition in the European Union (EU) — rules on their import, export, importation and trade in small arms and ammunition (SAP)
Assessment and control of the sale, import and export of small arms, ammunition and accessories
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the classification of firearms, essential components and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s laws and regulations are applied uniformly throughout the EU.
It codifies and repeals Directive 93/13/EEC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 13 June 2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all types of firearms and essential components, including handguns, rifles, shotguns, assault rifles, pistols, revolvers, shotguns and assault rifles. It does not apply to certain types of ammunition, such as live ammunition, which are intended for hunting or target shooting.
The legislation applies to the following categories of firearms:
automatic firearms;
machineguns;
automatic pistols and revolvers;
battery guns;
pistol and revolver ammunition with expanding projectiles and the projectiles for expanding projectiles;
muzzleloading ammunition.
Exceptions
Certain persons may be authorised to acquire and possess certain firearms. These include:
armourers, proof houses, manufacturers, certified experts, forensic scientists and, in certain cases, those involved in film or television recordings;
certified experts;
prohibited persons;
those involved in the manufacture, import or export of firearms or essential components.
dealers may grant dealers the right to effect transfers of firearms from their territory to a dealer established in another EU country without the prior authorisation.
EU countries may also authorise museums to acquire, possess and dispose of firearms.
Importers must ensure that:
the firearms they sell or transfer are registered in accordance with national law;
they are accompanied by a licence issued by the competent authority of the EU country in which they are sold or transferred;
the dealer is authorised to carry out the sale or transfer of the firearm.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 13 June 2014.
REFERENCES

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Regulations (EU), Euratom and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) have adopted a number of measures to ensure the safety and security of the environment, including:
measures to protect the environment and the public against the threat posed by the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs) and their parts and components;
measures for the protection of human health and the environment against the risks posed by WMDs; and
measures aimed at preventing, detecting and deterring the illicit manufacture and trafficking of weapons and ammunition, as well as the illegal transfer of WMD parts and accessories.

Summary 11:
European Maritime Single Window (EMSWe) — information exchange on port calls
European MaritimeSingle Window (EMSW) – information sharing on ports calls
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of the European Maritime Single-Window environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a single EU-wide information exchange system (EMSW) for port calls.
It aims to improve the efficiency of maritime transport and to limit the duplication of information which must be provided for operational purposes when a ship makes a port call.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU-registered ships, whether or not they are registered in the EU or not.
EU countries must designate a maritime single window for each of the port services listed in Article 1(2) of the EU’s common maritime transport policy (see summary).
The national single window is responsible for the operation of the EMSWe.
National single windows are managed by the national authorities of the country in which the ship is registered.
The European Commission has delegated powers to the EU countries to establish the national single windows.
Implementing acts
The EU countries must adopt implementing acts by 15 August 2024. The first implementing act must be adopted by 15 July 2024 and the second implementing act by 15 September 2024.
Delegated acts
EU country authorities must adopt delegated acts to set up the maritime national single-window. The delegated acts are adopted by the European Parliament and the Council on the basis of a proposal from the EU country in question.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    и  
It has applied since 15 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Maritime transport: the transport of goods by sea, air, rail, road, inland waterways and inland waterways.
* Port: the place where goods are loaded or unloaded by ships.

Summary 12:
Electronic road toll systems (EETS) — rules on user rights and rights of toll chargers and EETS service providers (OJ L 183, 27.4.2016, pp. 1-8)
Electronic roads tolls (EETS) – rules on users rights and obligations
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/679 on electronic road tolls
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that road users have the right to pay a road toll within the European Union (EU).
It repeals Directive 1999/98/EC, which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all road users in the EU, irrespective of where they live or work.
It does not apply to:
road users in non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland;
travelers on the European Economic Area (EEA) and the Baltic States;
people on the territory of the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) or the European Atomic Energy Community (EEC);
individuals who do not live in an EU country.
Rights and obligations of EETS users
EETS users have a number of rights, including:
the right to be identified and to be compensated for any loss or damage caused by their use of the EETS system;
the rights to have their data collected, processed and used by EETS providers;
to have their rights respected in the event of a breach of their EETS rights.
The rights of EETS users are set out in the directive’s annex to the directive.
Toll chargers
EETs providers must ensure that:
they have the necessary authorisation from the EU country in which they provide EETS services. The initial period of such authorisation must not exceed 3 years.
They must also ensure that EETs users have access to the relevant information, including their names, contact details and contact details.
EU countries must set up a Conciliation Body to deal with disputes between EET providers and EET users. The body has the power to request relevant information from EET service providers and any third parties active in the provision of EATS within the EU countries concerned.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives (EC) No 552/2008 and (EU/2016/679) have applied since 27 April 2016 and had to be transposed into national law by 27 April 2017 respectively.
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EETS is a new form of transport that allows users to pay tolls on their way to and from their destination.
For more information, see:
‘EETS’ on the website of the Commission.
* KEY TERMS
* Electronic road toll system: a system that allows road users to charge a toll on their road using a mobile phone or electronic device.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 13:
European Union (EU) immigration liaison officers — cooperation between EU countries and non-EU countries on migration policy (revision and detention centres for use in the context of the Schengen acquis) (recast)
European Union migration policy — cooperation among the EU countries, the European Commission and the European External Action Service (EEAS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — the European Union’s migration policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a network of EU countries’ competent authorities responsible for the deployment of EU-level immigration officers to non-European Union countries to support the implementation, application and development of the EU migration policy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the scope and assignment of tasks and reporting lines of the respective EU country authorities, the Commission and EU agencies for defining the scope, assignment and reporting of the tasks and the tasks of their respective immigration liaisons.
It also sets up an EU-wide information exchange platform to exchange information on the migration situation in the EU.
The European Commission is responsible for setting up a steering board to coordinate the activities of the network. The steering board is composed of representatives of the countries concerned, including the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission, and is chaired by the Commission.
EU countries are required to designate an immigration liaison officer for each of their territory.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
The EU countries covered by the regulation are:
Switzerland,
Iceland,
the Republic of Iceland and
the Kingdom of Norway.
BACKGROUND
The Commission has been deploying EU country-by-country immigration officers for more than a decade.
For more information, see:
‘EU migration policy’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Immigration liaison officer: an EU country official responsible for ensuring that the migration policy is implemented in accordance with EU and national law, policies or procedures.
* Reception and detention centre: a detention centre is a facility that is used either at operational level, strategic level, or both.
REFERENCOURAGING ACTS
Commission Regulation (EU), Euratom and the EU agencies set up the European Migration and Integration Steering Board to:
coordinate the activities and priorities of the Union in the area of migration;
make recommendations to the Commission on how to achieve the objectives of the European migration policy;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to carry out its work;
organise meetings of the steering board;
convene meetings of other organisations;
draw up a list of the relevant EU instruments and structures to be used for the implementation of the migration policies of the Member States;
review the work of the Steering board every 5 years;
set up an annual report on the activities carried out by the EU country concerned;
take due account of the views expressed by the representatives of those countries.

Summary 14:
Biometric identity cards and residence cards
Biometric identities and residence documents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — biometric identity card and residence card security standards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the security of the EU’s identity card system by introducing biometric identifiers* and a highly secure storage medium.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the security standards and technical specifications for biometric identification and residence certificates (i.e. cards issued by EU countries to citizens of the European Union (EU).
It also sets out a contact point for issuing identity cards or residence cards.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1030/2002.
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 2 years on the implementation of the regulation.
Scope
It applies to all EU citizens who are citizens of an EU country, regardless of their nationality. It does not apply to non-EU citizens who have the right to live in the EU.
National IDENTITY CARDS
National identity cards must contain a facial image of the holder of the card and two fingerprints in interoperable digital formats.
EU countries must use the standardised code ‘Family Member EU Art 10 DIR 2004/38/EC’ in the data field in data field, as referred to in the annex to the regulation, as well as in the Annex to Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/1954.
Family members of EU citizens must have their family members’ identity card or residence card issued by the EU country in which they live in.
They must have a minimum period of validity of 5 years and a maximum period of 10 years.
A contact point must be set up in each EU country to issue identity cards. The contact points must be staffed by qualified and duly authorised staff designated by the authorities responsible for issuing the identity card.
Collection of biometric data
The data must be collected solely by qualified authorised staff, for the purpose of being integrated into the highly secure data storage medium provided for in Article 3(5) for identity cards, and in Article 7(1) for residence cards, which must be erased or destroyed within 3 years of the end of the 5-year validity period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/65/EU on the electronic identification and trust services for the internal market (see summary) and Directive 2014/64/EU (see amending act(s) below) has applied since 1 July 2014.
Regulations (EU), Euratom and the Commission have to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Biometric Identity Cards’ on the European Commission's website
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 752/2019 of 29 June 2019 on biometric information and residence document security standards for the electronic identity card (OJ L 183, 30.6.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation(Euratam) No 77/2018 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems for security, border and migration management (MID)
Interoperabilisation of EU data systems for border, transit and immigration management (IDM)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 on interoperability of the European information system (EES)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve interoperability between the EU’s information systems to ensure that border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the necessary information at their disposal.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the EES, VIS, ETIAS, CIR and Eurodac.
It does not apply to:
the European Union External Action Service (EEAS) or the European Union Customs and Border Control Agency (CFTA);
the EU countries’ competent national authorities;
the national authorities of the EU countries that have access to at least one EU information system, including the CIR or the SIS;
EU countries that do not have the right to access the information systems.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
Eligibility
The EES and VIS are open to all EU countries, including non-EU countries, for the purpose of:
searching for one's private life and to the protection of personal data;
collecting, processing and storing data for the purposes of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences;
identifying persons who are unable to identify themselves or unidentified human remains;
assessing the different identities without delay;
checking that the data are stored in the right place;
ensuring that the relevant national authorities and EU agencies have the information they need to carry out their duties;
providing the competent authorities with the necessary technical assistance.
Access to the data
The EU countries must ensure that the supervisory authorities independently monitor the lawfulness of the processing of data under this regulation by the EU country concerned, including their transmission to and from the interoperability and any assistance, including from the national competent authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/34/EU has applied since 1 January 2015.
Directives 2014/35/EU and 2014/36/EU have applied since 31 December 2014.
Decision 2014/33/EU had to become law in the Member States by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
The aim of the directive is to ensure interoperability in the EU information and communication systems (ICTs).
For more information, see:
‘Information and communication system interoperability’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 16:
Sustainable and greenhouse gas emissions saving criteria for biomass fuels used in the electricity sector and in the heating and cooling sector
Sustainability and greenhouse gaseous fuels used on biomass farms
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2010/31/EU on renewable energy
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (EU) 2018/1999
1.6.2018

OJ L 183 of 12.6.-
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2019/1, 2019/2 and 2019/3

1-(1) of the European Parliament and of the Council
1 (1) amending Regulation (EU), Euratom and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom)
1(1) repealing Regulation (EC) No 552/2008
1) repeals Regulation (CFSP) No 652/2009
2-4 amending Regulations (EC, EFTA and Euratom).
3-5 amending Directives (EC and EFTA) No 469/2008 and (EC).
4-6 amending Directive (EU).
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVES?
The renewable energy directive aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from the production, transport, storage and use of renewable energy.
It aims to ensure that renewable energy is used in all sectors of the economy, including:
energy production and distribution;
energy distribution and consumption;
transport and storage;
air transport;
aeronautics;
agriculture and forestry.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all renewable energy sources, including wind, solar, geothermal and geothermal energy, biomass, biofuels, bio-fuels and bio-energy storage.
The legislation does not apply to renewable energy generated in the power sector.
EU countries must set up national support schemes for renewable energy from renewable sources.
Support schemes are designed to:
provide financial support to producers, distributors and end-users of renewable electricity;
support the development and operation of renewable power stations;
encourage the development of new renewable energy infrastructure;
provides financial support for the development, operation and maintenance of existing renewable energy installations;
contribute to the sustainable development of the EU’s renewable energy sector;
ensure that renewable electricity is used for the generation, transmission and distribution of energy, distribution and storage of electricity, and for the supply of electricity to final customers.
In addition, the directive provides for the creation of guarantees of origin for other renewable energy, such as hydrogen.
Guarantees of origin
The EU countries must ensure that the renewable energy they provide to producers and distributors is not used for other purposes other than those for which it was originally intended.
Regulatory (EU)-level rules
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The energy directive is one of the most ambitious environmental legislation in the world.
For more information, see:
‘Renewable energy’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 17:
Competition in the wholesale market for fixed and wireless communications networks and publicly available electronic communications services (Directive (EU) 2016/1148) and its subsequent amendments
Competition on the European Commission’s website
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2016/2011/EU — wholesale market rules for the wholesale provision of fixed- and wireless-based communications networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that consumers have access to a wide range of wholesale telecommunications services across the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals Directives 2011/1149/EEC and 2011/2011 (EU), which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all fixed-and-wireless communications networks (fixed and wireless) and publicly accessible electronic communications (e.g. mobile phones, tablets, computers, etc.).
It does not apply to the provision of publicly available e-mail services.
It sets out the rights and obligations of internet service providers (i.e. companies that provide internet services).
The legislation does not affect the right of consumers to choose between different types of telecommunications services, such as telephone, cable television, internet radio, satellite television, fixed-line telephony and satellite internet access.
Rights and obligations
Internet service providers must:
provide consumers with:
access to a network, irrespective of their geographical location;
have the right to contract with a service provider;
be able to negotiate interconnection;
with a network termination point;
make a minimum price for the same service;
ensure that the service is available to all end-users in the same way.
They must also provide consumers with a right to:
use a number;
use the internet for the purposes of making a payment;
establish a database on the numbering resources with an extraterritorial use within the EU.
Regulatory authorities
The European Commission must ensure that:
the directive does not infringe on consumers’ fundamental rights;
it does not undermine competition in the retail market;
the regulation does not prevent competition in wholesale markets.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The new directive entered into force on 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
For more information, see:
‘The wholesale market’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption of heavy-duty vehicles (Regulation (EU) 2017/2400)
Monitoring, reporting and data collection of greenhouse gases emitted by heavy-duty vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 595/2009 on a regulated procedure for monitoring and reporting greenhouse gas emissions and diesel fuel consumption
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE IMPLEMENTING REGULATION?
It sets up a system to monitor and report greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and motor vehicle fuel consumption to the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes a central database for data on GHG emissions and consumption by heavy duty vehicles.
The data are collected by the European Environment Agency (EEA), which manages the database on behalf of the Commission. The data are transmitted to the competent authorities of the EU countries and to the EEA’s central database.
EU countries are responsible for collecting the data and imposing administrative fines where the manufacturer fails to deliver the required data within the applicable deadline.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATING PROCEDURE APPLY?
Regulating Regulation (European Parliament and of the Council) 2018/2401 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to reduce greenhouse gas emission from heavy duty vehicle production.
For more information, see:
Heavy-duty vehicle emissions (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation of 20 December 2014 setting up a regulatory procedure for the monitoring, reporting, collection and data dissemination of greenhouse gasses emitted by vehicles and motor vehicles and amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/2301 (OJ L 183, 21.12.2014, pp. 1-8)
Regulation of the European Parliament and the Council of 21 May 2013 on a mechanism for monitoring greenhouse gas emitted by motor vehicles, motor vehicles or other vehicles and repealing Regulation (EEC) 2004/2004/2004 (OJR L 165, 18.6.2013, pp.)

Summary 19:
Safeguarding the security of gas supply in the European Union (EU) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — the obligation of EU countries to provide solidarity for the protection of their electricity and gas systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries provide each other with adequate and fair compensation for the loss of electricity or gas supplied to their territory.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all electricity and natural gas customers in the EU, regardless of their nationality.
It does not apply to:
the gas delivered into the territory of the requesting EU country;
all other relevant and reasonable costs incurred when providing solidarity, including, where appropriate, costs of such measures that may have been established in advance;
reimbursement for any compensation resulting from judicial proceedings, arbitration proceedings or similar proceedings and settlements and related costs of any proceedings involving the EU country providing solidarity.
EU countries must establish preventive action plans and emergency plans.
The measures, actions and procedures contained in the emergency plan must be tested at least once between its four-year updates.
Each EU country must designate a competent authority to carry out the risk assessment and the preventive action plan.
A national risk assessment must be carried out every 4 years. The risk assessments must take into account the economic impact of meeting the infrastructure standard, the gas market development and gas infrastructure projects in the risk group.
An EU country may introduce non-market-based measures in order to comply with the obligations laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    банска ганца  года  
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
Electricity or natural gas system: a system that supplies electricity to the end user of the electricity supply.
Natural gas: a natural gas network that supplies natural gas to end users of natural gas.

Summary 20:
Ecodesign: energy labelling and labelling of energy-related products (E-LabEL) (European Commission Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Ecodesigned: energy-labelling and laboratoryelling of products (European Union Commission Regulation(EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council on market surveillance and control of products entering the Union market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to ensure that products on the European Union market are labelled in a way that ensures that consumers can easily identify them.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2009/125/EC on ecodesign.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products on sale in the EU, including:
electrical products;
electromagnetic products (e.g. heaters, refrigerators, air conditioners, washing machines, etc.).
It does not apply to:
gas appliances;
water heaters and hot water heaters;
air conditioners and boilers.
Labels
Products must be labelled in the following ways:
in a colour that corresponds to the energy efficiency of the product;
in the format set out in Annex I to the regulation.
The label should be supplemented by a product information sheet.
If a label for a product group is rescaled, confusion on the part of customers should be avoided by replacing the labels on the affected products displayed in shops within a short timeframe, and by organising adequate consumer information campaigns clearly indicating that a new version of the label has been introduced.
EU countries must ensure that suppliers and dealers keep a product database of all the models of which were exclusively placed on the market before 1 August 2017.
Suppliers must keep the information in the product database for all the units of which they sold before that date.
Distributors must keep a separate database of models that were exclusively sold on the EU market before the regulation came into force.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2008/65/EC has applied since 1 August 2008 and had to become law in the Member States by 1 August 2009.
Regulations (EC, EEC and EFTA) have applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce energy consumption in the energy sector.
For more information, see:
‘Energy labelling’ on European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy labelling: the label of an energy-efficient product, such as a heating or cooling unit, which indicates its energy efficiency.
ACT

Summary 21:
Protecting the rights and freedoms of natural persons in the processing of their personal data (Regulation (EU) No 1215/2012)
Protecting human rights and the freedoms of non-EU citizens in the process of processing personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the protection of the rights of natural people in the European Union (the ‘Data Protection Regulation’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that natural persons who are in the EU by a controller or a processor who are not established in the Union have the same level of protection as EU citizens.
It sets out rules for the processing and management of their data.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the processing, management and control of personal data by:
a controller or processor established in an EU country;
a processor established outside the EU;
an independent body entrusted by the EU country to manage the processing;
third parties, such as the European Commission or the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom).
It does not apply to:
the processing of data by a third party for the purpose of providing goods or services to a natural person;
processing of data for scientific research purposes;
the use of third-party data for commercial purposes.
EU countries must ensure that:
data protection officers are appointed by means of a transparent procedure by the competent national authority responsible for data protection;
natural persons have the right to have their data processed in a way that respects their rights and their privacy;
personal data can be deleted if they do not wish to do so.
The regulation lays down a number of measures to protect natural persons’ rights, including:
right to object and automated individual decision-making;
right of access to personal data and processing operations;
protection of the personal data of children and other vulnerable groups;
privacy and security of data;
access to the internet;
data security;
security of data and data protection measures;
transparency and security measures.
A data protection officer is appointed by each EU country and is responsible for:
ensuring that the processing is lawful and fair;
assessing the risks involved by processing the data and ensuring that the data are protected;
protecting the privacy of the data subject;
monitoring the processing activities of the controller and processor;
providing the competent authority with information on the data processing and the data protection officers;
establishing an independent body to manage data protection.
Each EU country must set up a supervisory authority to supervise the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
The Directive entered into force on 1 May 2014.
For more information, see:
The Data Protection Regulation (European Commission).

Summary 22:
Harmonised indices of consumer prices (HICP, HICP-CT, OOH price index) and the house price index (HPI) at EU and national level
harmonized indices of consumers’ prices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 on the development, production and dissemination of harmonised indices on consumer prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down a common framework for developing, producing and disseminating harmonised consumer prices.
It aims to ensure that the indices are comparable and relevant.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the harmonised index for consumer prices, the harmonised price index for goods and services, the national price index and the price of housing.
The harmonised prices are based on observed prices, which include taxes on products.
They are divided into 4 sub-indices:
price of goods,
the price of services and
the prices of capital goods.
National authorities must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with the national data on the prices of products and capital goods at the end of each calendar year.
Each year, the Commission publishes a report on the national results of the national authorities’ data collection and on the harmonisation of the indices. The report is published in the Official Journal of the European Union (FISIM).
The Commission publishes the results of national data collection every 3 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?

It has applied since 21 May 2013.

  * KEY TERMS

Summary 23:
Anti-dumping and countervailing duties — rules on imports and exports of certain products from non-EU countries (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011) (see summary)
Anti-Dumping and anti-countervailing duty — rules for importing and exporting certain products and certain products of non-European Union countries
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 on the application of the rules of the 1994 Anti-Doping Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the application by the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) on the protection of intellectual property rights (IPR).
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules for applying the GATT’s rules on trade in goods and measures to protect the intellectual property of EU countries.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 181/2008.
Importers must notify the Commission of any imports they consider to be being dumped on the EU market.
The Commission decides whether to impose an anti-defection duty on the imports of a product or a product of an EU country.
If the Commission suspects that the imports are being dumped, it may impose a duty on them. It may also impose a provisional duty on imports if there is reason to believe that an undertaking is being breached, or in the case of breach or withdrawal of an undertaking, where the investigation has not been concluded.
An EU countries may request the Commission to carry out a verification visit to check whether the dumping is being carried out.
EU countries must inform the Commission within 3 months of the start of the verification visit and within 3 years of the conclusion of the investigation.
In the event of a breach, the Commission may decide to impose provisional or definitive duties on the import of the product or product of the exporting country if:
the dumping is directly or indirectly controlled by a third person;
the dumped imports are causing injury to the producers of all or almost all of the production within such a market;
there is a risk that the dumped imports will adversely affect the production of a third party.
A provisional duty may be imposed if the Commission considers that the dumping practices are being committed by:
a third party;
an EU country has a dominant position in the market; or
the third party has a significant market share in the EU.
Provisional duties may not be imposed for a period longer than 9 months and may be extended for a further 9 months or they may be suspended for 9 months if they are found to have been breached.
Duties may also be imposed on imports of certain goods if they:
have a significant adverse effect on the production or export price of the importing country;
are not being produced in the country concerned;
have been produced in a way that is contrary to the interests of the producer of the imported product;
contribute to the development of a competitive market or to the growth of the economy of the country in question;
do not constitute a threat to public health or the safety of the environment;
provide a substantial amount of value added tax (VAT) or indirect taxes on the export of the exported product and the materials physically incorporated therein, when intended for consumption in the exporting countries and not collected or refunded in respect of the products exported to the Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    данска ванка  гара бародна и года гракова прагандрациальнога, гораста ковского глакса нашка,  бодски гходцабаннока обланцкой бхаркодралкома манта and борсконна  хорговной  гчановка
It has applied since 1 July 2018.

Summary 24:
Countervailing duties on imports of certain products — EU countries and exporters — rules on how they are applied
Combating unfair competition — EU governments’ subsidies on imports — rules for applying them
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the application of the principle of countervailing duty on imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the application by EU countries of a principle of anti-subsidisation (countervailing* duties) to certain products imported from non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Direct subsidies
The principle of subsidisation is the principle that a government or an industry receiving a subsidy is entitled to receive a countervailable amount of money from the government of an EU country or an exporter.
The amount of the subsidy must be calculated by spreading the subsidy across a period which reflects the normal depreciation of such assets in the industry concerned.
It does not apply to indirect subsidies (i.e. subsidies that are not directly linked to the acquisition or future acquisition of fixed assets).
The regulation lays down the conditions for determining whether a company or industry receives a subsidy.
In the event of a decision to grant a subsidy, EU countries must notify the European Commission within 3 weeks of receiving the request. The Commission then carries out a verification visit to the company or exporter to check that it has not received a subsidy or that it is not receiving one.
If a company does not comply with the EU country’s request, it must inform EU countries or exporters of the reasons for the decision.
EU countries or companies that have received the subsidy
The Commission then decides whether to grant the subsidy or to suspend it.
Importers and importers must inform the Commission of the reason for the suspension or suspension of a subsidy and provide the Commission with a copy of the decision or the reasons why.
A decision to suspend a subsidy may be taken by the Commission on the basis of information provided by the companies or the exporters.
Disclosure of the essential facts and considerations to parties which qualify for such treatment
The European Commission must make a decision on which companies or companies do not qualify for a subsidy within a time limit which allows them to defend their interests within the EU. The decision may be made by the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC).
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    даганска баркова гаршка  года ванка, горкона кодна  ходка and конша доблакка are repealed.
BACKGROUND
Direct and indirect subsidies
Direct government subsidies are the main source of subsidies in the EU for the production of goods and services of an industry. They are known as ‘subsidies’.
Direct subsidy is a form of indirect government support for a particular sector of the economy, such as agriculture, forestry, fisheries, forestry and aquaculture, which is intended to reduce the price of those products to a significant degree or prevent price increases, which would otherwise have occurred.
Indirect subsidies are a type of direct government support to a sector of an economy that is not directly related to the production or export of goods or services of that industry.
For more information, see:
Direct Government subsidies (European Commission).
Direct Direct Subsidies (European External Action Service).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  бодскиг  прабандка — Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of the Council and of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States of the European Union, meeting within the Council, amending and repealing Regulation (EC) No 597/2009 (OJ L 347, 20.12.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 181/2012 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 25:
Recovery and resilience plans for the EU’s 2020-2021 climate change recovery and resilience strategies (EU-2020/2020/2021)
Recovery & Resilience Plans for the European Union's 2020–2021 Climate Change Facility
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1939 on the recovery & resilience plans of the Member States
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the framework for EU countries to develop and implement recovery and resiliency plans to tackle climate change and the challenges resulting from climate change.
It aims to support the EU countries’ efforts to recover from the effects of the CO2 emissions crisis and to tackle the challenges associated with climate change, including biodiversity, and to reduce disparities in income, wealth and social mobility.
KEY POINTS
The EU-2020–2022 Climate Change Fund supports the development and implementation of national recovery and recovery plans (R&R plans) for the period 2020–2020.
The R&R plan must be submitted to the European Commission by each Member State by 30 June 2020.
Each Member State must submit a recovery and resilient plan to the Commission by 31 December 2020. The plan must include the following elements:
a framework for the implementation of the R & R plan;
a breakdown of the costs and benefits of climate change;
measures to reduce inequalities in income and wealth in the Member State;
aims to reduce poverty and inequality in the region;
the development of renewable energy resources;
rehabilitation of degraded natural resources, such as forests and wetlands;
investment in renewable energy sources;
environmental measures to reduce air pollution;
sustainable urban development;
climate action to reduce the impact of CO2 emission emissions;
economic and social policies to reduce social inequalities and inequalities in the regions affected by climate change
The Commission must transmit the recovery and sustainability plans to the Council of the EU and the European Parliament by 31 June 2021. The Commission must then transmit the plans and the Council implementing decisions to the EU Member States by 31 August 2021.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE?
   
 
It has applied since 19 February 2017.

Summary 26:
Structural Reform Support Programme (SRSP) — financial support for EU countries’ efforts to implement structural reforms
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/241 on the European Structural Reform support programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out how the European Commission provides technical support to EU countries in order to support them in their efforts to achieve structural reforms.
It aims to promote the EU’s economic, social and territorial cohesion by supporting the efforts of EU countries to implement reforms and to prepare, amend, implement and revise recovery and resilience plans.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The SRSP supports EU countries on the basis of a request for technical support from the Commission.
The support can cover a wide range of policy areas, including:
reforms in the context of economic governance processes;
the implementation of reforms undertaken at the initiative of the EU countries;
regional integration;
economic and social cohesion;
transformation of the labour market;
education and training;
health and social inclusion;
environmental protection;
digital transitions;
reintegration of EU citizens into society;
employment policies;
social cohesion.
Funding
The European Commission is responsible for setting out the work programmes for the support provided by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC).
The work programmes set out:
the allocation for the instrument, in accordance with the general objective and specific objectives referred to in Articles 3 and 4 of this regulation, respectively, and within the scope of the ERSP;
eligible actions;
selection and award criteria for grants and all the elements required by the Financial Regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a Structural and Investment Funds (SIFs).
For more information, see:
‘The Structural & Investment Funds’ on the EESC website.
* KEY TERMS
* Structural reform: reforms undertaken by EU countries aimed at improving the quality of life of citizens, improving the competitiveness of the economy and reducing social inequalities.

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights to rail services — common rules for carriage and carriage of passengers
Rail passengers' rights to railway services — Common rules for carrying passengers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on rail passenger rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that rail passengers have equal rights when travelling by rail in the European Union (EU).
It lays down uniform rules for the carriage of rail passengers and their luggage.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all rail passenger services throughout the EU, with the exception of certain services offered for historic or touristic use.
Passengers have the right to:
to purchase rail tickets without undue difficulty;
to have their travel pass or season ticket validated;
have access to traffic and travel information both before and during the journey; and
to be compensated for any delays of less than 60 minutes during the period of validity of the travel pass and season ticket.
It does not apply to the operation of reservation systems and to the operations on reservation systems referred to in Annex II, Parts I and II of the regulation.
The rights of passengers include:
the right to purchase a rail ticket without any undue difficulty and to have their ticket validated by the railway undertakings;
access to information regarding the service both before the journey and during it;
the possibility to submit their requests by using a form;
a right to make a request for compensation within 1 month after the submission of the ticket price;
right to dispose of their luggage;
in the event of death or personal injury;
rights to have been infringed.
EU countries must lay down penalties applicable to infringements of this regulation and ensure that these penalties are applied.
Railway undertakings and station managers must keep the data necessary to assess the complaint for the duration of the entire complaint-handling procedure and make that data available to national enforcement bodies upon request.
National enforcement bodies are designated by EU countries to deal with complaints made by passengers. They must:
deal with all infringements by rail undertakings or by station or infrastructure managers;
identify the national enforcement body or any other body designated by the EU country on whose territory the incident took place;
take all necessary measures to ensure the protection of passengers;
ensure that passengers have access to the information they need to make their requests for compensation;
assist passengers with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility with a comparable level of assistance and accessibility when offering an alternative service.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
railways must provide persons with disabilities with mobility equipment or assistive devices lost or damaged with equivalent levels of assistance;
re-routing transport service providers must provide people with disabilities, persons with a reduced mobility or persons with limited mobility with equivalent level of access to an alternative transport service;
transport service providers are required to provide passengers with access to information on alternative transport services.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2019/882 repeals and replaces Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1300/2014.
Regulations (EC), Euratom and Euratom have been amended several times, most recently in 2018.
BACKGROUND
Railways are one of the main means of transport in the EU and are a key component of the EU’s single market.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EEC) no 1370/2007 of 23 February 2007 on rail passengers‘rights to rail passenger carriage’ (OJ L 136, 29.3.2007, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Eurat) No 1307/2007 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 28:
External assistance for the Western Balkans (IPA III) 2021-2027
External aid for the western Balkans (IPSA III 2021 - 2027)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/941 — framework for planning and delivering external assistance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a framework for the EU’s external assistance to the Western Balkan countries for the period between 2021 and 2027.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the framework for EU funding to the beneficiaries of the pre-accession financial assistance (pre-IPA II) and the rural development assistance (RDA) programmes.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the IPA III programme and the RDA programmes, which are managed by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The IPA III is the third phase of the IPA programme, which began in 2014 and runs until 2027, and aims to improve the quality of life of the beneficiaries and to strengthen democracy and the rule of law in the countries covered by the IPA II programme.
Actions under IPA III must be consistent with the principle of ‘do no harm’ and should comply with EU taxonomy to the extent possible, in particular to ensure the sustainability of investments in the areas covered by IPA III.
In order to achieve these objectives, the EU provides financial support to beneficiaries in the following areas:
promoting democracy and human rights;
supporting the fight against organised crime and corruption;
strengthening civil society and cooperation with local authorities;
improving the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms, including the independence of the judiciary;
protecting the integrity of the judicial system;
encouraging the development of the private sector;
developing the capacity of local authorities to tackle corruption and abuse of power;
facilitating the exchange of information;
providing technical assistance;
contributing to research and development;
exchange of information on the beneficiaries’ progress;
assisting in the implementation of projects, programmes and initiatives.
Implementation
The EU provides the beneficiaries with a strategic board to advise the Commission on the strategic and budgetary commitments to the programme. The strategic board is composed of representatives of beneficiaries, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union and is chaired by the Commission. Participation in the meetings of the strategic board must be voluntary.
Each beneficiary is assessed on its own merits. In the case of a significant regression or persistent lack of progress by a beneficiary listed in Annex I, the scope and intensity of assistance may be modulated accordingly, including by reducing the funds proportionally and redirecting them in ways that avoid compromising support for improving fundamental rights, democracy and rule of the law, including support to civil society.
Annex II of the regulation provides for the establishment of a programming framework and general principles for IPA III, as well as financial instruments and guarantees for external actions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The implementing regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
    *  *  
For more information, see:
‘The Western Balkans’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Regulation of 7 January 2021 laying down the Framework for Planning and Providing External Assistance for the Period 2021 -2027 (OJ L 183, 7.1.2019, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Implementing Resolution of 7 February 2021 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

European Union foreign action – migration and Development cooperation
SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU supports partner countries to manage migration more effectively towards sustainable development.
KEY POINTS
The European Union (EU), through its external action instruments, provides financial support to countries experiencing fragility or conflict, small island developing states (LDCs), landlocked developing countries and heavily indebted poor countries.

They aim to:

encourage the development of sustainable development policies and programmes;

migration,
development,
human rights and democracy,
civil society,
economic development,
environmental protection,
health and safety,
education and training,
transport,
food security,
energy,
sustainable rural development, and
education, childcare and training.
