Summary 30:
Reporting of breaches of EU law
Reporting breaches of Union law
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/679 on the protection of whistleblowers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect whistle-blowers* by ensuring that they have the right to report breaches of the law of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens and non-EU nationals, irrespective of whether they are EU citizens or third-country nationals, who, by virtue of their work-related activities, have privileged access to information on breaches that it would be in the public interest to report and who may suffer retaliation if they report them.
It does not apply to EU citizens who are not EU citizens.
The protection granted to whistleblowers is based on Article 21(7) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU) and Article 114 TFEU (see summary).
It applies to persons who:
have a legitimate interest in protecting the interests of the public;
have been victims of a serious breach of the laws of an EU country;
are victims of an act that is harmful to the public’s health, safety, security or the environment;
may be victims of fraud, abuse of power or abuse of any other form of unlawful activity.
EU countries must ensure that this protection is effective, including, where necessary, by restricting, by legislative measures, the exercise of certain data protection rights of persons concerned in line with points (e) and (i) of Article 23(1) and(2) of Regulation (EC) No 596/2014 and (ii) of Directive 2013/30/EU.
Reporting channels
The legislation provides for the establishment of internal and external reporting channels.
An internal reporting channel may be set up by an information centre or by a single and independent administrative authority.
External reporting channels may be established by third parties, at local, regional and national level, whilst being commensurate with their size.
Protection
Reporting persons must be protected against retaliatory measures taken directly vis-à-vis them, including those that can be taken indirectly, including by facilitators, colleagues or relatives of the reporting person who are also in a work related connection with the reporting persons’ employer or customer or recipient of services.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Whistleblowers’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Whistleblower: a person who is a victim of a criminal offence or who has been the victim of an unlawful act.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management
European integration of border control
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1624 on the European integrated border and coast guard
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Integrated Border and Coast Guard (EIBG), the EU’s border control agency.
It aims to improve border management in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The EIBG comprises the national authorities of EU countries responsible for border management, including coast guards to the extent that they carry out border control tasks, the EU countries’ national authorities responsible for return and the European Border & Coast Guard Agency (EBCG).
It covers land, sea and air border surveillance and border checks.
The regulation lays down the general rules for the EBIG, including the protection of fundamental rights and a fundamental rights strategy.
EBIG is responsible for the return of illegal immigrants and their return to their country of origin or destination.

Summary 32:

Information and communication technology
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1584 — establishing the European Network and Information Security Agency (ENISA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European network and information security agency to support EU countries’ efforts to protect their networks and information systems against cyberattacks.
It aims to improve the protection of the EU’s information and communication technologies (ICTs) against cyber threats.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the tasks of ENISA, which is responsible for providing technical advice and assistance to EU countries and EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies on cybersecurity issues.
ENISA is a non-governmental organisation (NGO) established under Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001.
Its main tasks are to:

support the European Cybersecurity Agency (ECA) in its activities;
facilitate the exchange of information and expertise between ENISA and the EU countries;
ensure that EU countries share information on incidents and crises related to cybersecurity.

Summary 33:
Gross national income at market prices (GNI) and gross domestic product at market price (GDP) — definitions, verification and reporting
Gross gross national income* and Gross domestic product* at market markets (GND) – definitions and verification
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 608/2014 on the definition, verification, reporting and reporting of gross national incomes (GNIs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the definitions and methods for the calculation of Gross National Income* (GNI)* and Gross Domestic Product* (GDDP*) at market.
It aims to ensure that GNI data are comparable, reliable and exhaustive.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation defines the categories of GNI* and GDP* and defines the methods for their calculation.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with GNI and GDP data for each year.
The Commission must verify the sources, their uses and the methods in the inventory.
Eurostat must transmit the data to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) by 18 April 2019.
Transmission
The data must be transmitted for the preceding year and any changes made to the data for previous years must be communicated at the same time.
National accounts experts
The national accounts experts must be registered on a list constituted on the basis of voluntary proposals sent to the Commission by the national authorities responsible for the reporting of national accounts.
Duties
The European Commission has delegated the power to adopt delegated acts to the ESSC for a period of 5 years.
Delegated acts must be drawn up within 9 months of the end of the 5-year period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 89/130/EEC, Euratom and Regulation (EC) No 1287/2003 had to become law in the EU countries by 20 April 2003.
Directives 89/131/EC and 89/132/EC had to be repealed by 20 June 2004.
For more information, see:
‘Gross National Income at Market Prices’ on Eurostat’s website.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units — withdrawal of the UK from the European Union (EU) and the transition period from 30 March 2019 to 31 December 2020
EU type–approval
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 — type–applicability of the EU type‑approval system for vehicles, system, components, separate technical unit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for granting EU type-applicable type-approved vehicles and systems to manufacturers in the EU.
It aims to ensure a level playing field for manufacturers by ensuring that the same type of vehicle and system is approved in all EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles and systems covered by Directive 2007/46/EC (see summary);
components covered by Regulation (EC) No 852/2013 (see overview);
separate technical units covered by Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 1052/2014 and (EC).
It does not apply to certain types of motor vehicles, such as light commercial vehicles (LCVs) and vans.
Manufacturers must apply to the competent EU type‐approval authority for the approval of their vehicle or system.
The competent EU authority must grant the type of approval within 2 years of receiving a request for approval.
If the competent authority has granted the type‐approved vehicle, it must issue a type‐certificate of conformity and a certificate of conformity to the vehicle and its components.
A manufacturer may apply for a type‑certificate for the same vehicle or component in another EU country.
An EU type–certificate must contain a number indicating the EU country in which the vehicle, system or component was approved and the date on which it was approved.
Vehicle manufacturers must submit a request to the relevant competent authority in the relevant EU country, which must grant them the type–car type–technological unit and a type–equipment certificate for each vehicle and component covered by this regulation.
This type–vehicle type must be approved in accordance with Article 1 of the Treaty on European Union.
When a vehicle is approved, the manufacturer must apply for an EU–equivalent type–technical unit certificate for the vehicle or components covered by the relevant regulatory act of that EU country and submit the request to that competent authority. The competent authority may reject the request if it considers that the vehicle does not meet the requirements of the regulation or that it does not fulfil the requirements for the type‑equipment* certificate.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulatory (EU), Euratom has applied since 30 March 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to ensure that the EU’s type–manufacturer–type–equivalence system is applied throughout the EU, including:
Directive 2007/43/EC on the harmonisation of technical specifications for vehicles and components and Regulation (EEC, EEC, EFTA, EC and EFTA) No 1169/2014 on the conformity assessment procedure for motor vehicles;
Regulations (EC and EEC), EEC and EC of the European Parliament and of the Council on type–compatibility procedures for vehicle systems and components (Regulation(EU) 2016/1628) on type‐equivalency procedures for motor vehicle components and related technical units, and Directive (EC), EC and EC on the equivalence of technical certificates for vehicle system and components, and Directives 2008/65/EC and 2009/54/EC, and Council Directive 2009/53/EC of the same name (see consolidated version).
For more information, see:
‘Vehicle and system safety’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules for the use of personal data by Europol (European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation (Eurojust) and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF) (European External Action Service (EEAS) and Eurojust).
Europol is the European Union’s (EU) law enforcement office. It is responsible for preventing and combating crime in the EU.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom, ECSC) No 1271/2013 of 20 July 2013 laying down the rules for Europol
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out rules for using personal data to prevent and combat crime.
KEY POINTS
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure that personal data are used only for the purposes for which they are collected or processed.
The regulation sets out a number of rights and responsibilities of Europol, including:
the right to rectification, erasure and restriction of the personal data held by the agency;
the rights to have their personal data deleted or to have them further processed;
right to be informed of the results of the searches carried out by EU countries’ law enforcement authorities;
rights to have the information they have collected used for the purpose of preventing or combating crime used for other purposes.
EU countries must ensure that Europol has a copy of bilateral and multilateral exchanges of information with other EU countries on crime falling within Europol's objectives.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?

BACKGROUND
The European Union has a wide range of law enforcement instruments to combat crime, including the European Criminal Justice Network (ECJN), Eurojust and the EEA.
For more information, see:
‘Europol’ on the Europol website.
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: data on a person, such as their name, address, social security number, date of birth, gender, nationality, place of residence and place of employment.

Summary 36:
Financial assistance to EU countries affected by the COVID-19 outbreak (see summary)
Financial assistance in the event of an outbreak of the CoVID‐19 virus
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1046 on the use of the European Union’s financial assistance instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It allows the EU to provide financial assistance to affected EU countries by the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) to help them deal with the effects of the COVIV-19 virus on their labour markets.
It sets out the conditions and procedures for using the financial assistance.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have been affected by COVID‑19. It does not apply to the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (UK), which withdrew from the EU and the Euratom on 1 January 2019.
Beneficiaries of the assistance are the EU countries which have been most severely affected by this outbreak.
The financial assistance is provided in the form of a loan granted by the Union to the EU country concerned.
Borrowing and lending operations
The EU provides financial assistance by means of a borrowing and lending operation (borrowing and lending) to the beneficiary EU country. The amount of the loan is calculated according to the gross national income (GNI) of the beneficiary country.
EU countries must open a special account with its national central bank for the management of their financial assistance received.
Contributions to the instrument are provided by EU countries in the following ways:
the EU countries provide the European Commission with the necessary financial resources to cover the cost of the short-term work schemes or similar measures, as well as, where appropriate, of the relevant health-related measures, that may be financed;
the Commission provides the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee on Employment and Social Affairs with a report on the application of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 220(1) on the European Framework for Financial Assistance (TFEU)
the European Parliament and the Council provide the Commission with a call on guarantees for the loan.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 8 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
The COVID–19 outbreak is one of a series of highly contagious viruses that have affected the labour market in the EU over the past 20 years.
For more information, see:
‘CoVID-20’ on the EU's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1046/2018 of 7 May 2018 on the utilisation of the Financial Assistance Instrument (TFU) (OJ L 136, 8.5.2018, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
   
Regulations of the Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2017 on the common framework for financial assistance for Member States affected by natural disasters and on the establishment of a multiannual financial framework (MFF) and on own-resources ceilings and repealing Regulations (EC, EC, EC and EFTA) No 552/2008, (EC), (EC).
Regulatory (EU), Euratom No 220/2010 of the EU Parliament and Council of 12 December 2010 on the framework for economic assistance to Member States and repealing Directives (EC and EC) No 550/2008 and 2009/852/EEC (Directive 2009/1052/EC) and Directives 2009/11/EC, (EU and Euratom).
Directive 2010/1053/EC of the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe on the prevention and control of the spread of communicable diseases and the elimination of the threat to human health and the environment caused by the proliferation of microorganism, the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD) and the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) and of its Member States, and repealing Directive 2009/946/EC.
Commission Recommendation of 8 May 2020 on the extension of the financial support provided by the EU under Regulation (CFSP) No 2023/2010 to Member Countries in the case of the outbreak of COVID‐20 and repealing Decision 2009/12/EC (Commission).

Summary 37:
Financial distress or failure in a clearing house (CCP): resolution of financial distress and resolution of CCPs (Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 on the resolution of the financial distress of a clearinghouse (CCPs)
Financial distress/failure in a CCP: resolution of its financial distress
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 — rules and tools for resolving financial distress or failing in a regulated clearing house
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures to be followed by national authorities in the event of a financial crisis or failure of a regulated financial services provider (CPS) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation aims to ensure that:
the resolution of a CCP is as simple as possible;
the loss allocation tool allows the resolution authority to reduce the value of any gains payable by the CCP to non-defaulting clearing members in recovery;
clearing members and clients that provide them with CCPs with clearing services have the right to receive the full allocation of any uncovered losses to them.
It also sets out a framework of measures to be taken when a CCP fails or is considered to be failing or likely to fail, including early intervention measures.
The resolution authority is responsible for:
establishing a recovery plan for the CCP;
ensuring the re-establishment of a matched book;
assessing the risk profile of the CCP and its clients;
setting up a dispute resolution procedure;
implementing the ‘no creditor worse off’ principle;
procedures for the transfer of assets and liabilities;
suspending the payment or delivery obligations of both counterparties to any contract entered into by a CCP under resolution from the publication of the notice of suspension.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The implementing regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  
The European Commission has been working to modernise and streamline the EU’s rules on financial distress, failure and resolution.
For more information, see:
‘Financial distress and failure’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Financial distress: when a financial service provider fails or fails to meet its obligations.
* Resolution authority: the competent national authority that resolves a failing or failing financial institution.

Summary 38:
European Year of Rail 2021 - promoting sustainable and smart rail transport (European Year 2021) - encouraging and supporting the efforts of the EU, Member States, regional and local authorities, and other organisations to increase the share of passengers and freight moving by rail.
The European Year 2021 is the European Union’s (EU) initiative to encourage and support the efforts by Member States and Member States’ authorities to increase passenger and freight movements by rail in order to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions in the transport sector.
ACT
Council Decision (Euratom) 2021/2021 of 20 November 2021 on the ‘European year of rail’ (the European Year’).
SUMMARY
The general objective of the European Year is to encourage, support and provide technical assistance to Member States to increase rail passenger traffic and freight movement in the EU by 2023.
In particular, the objective is to:
encourage and support Member States in their efforts to open up the market of domestic passenger services, to reduce costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the EU and to reduce technical barriers to the development of the rail sector;
support the European Railways Agency for Railways (ERA) with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
By 31 March 2021, the Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of its plans.
National contact persons are appointed by the Commission to coordinate the running of the year at EU level. The Commission may, if the national contact persons do not fulfil the objectives set out by the European Commission.
At EU level, the EU countries’ contact persons meet at least once a year.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
The decision has applied since 20 November 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU’S climate neutrality strategy aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society, with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use.

Summary 40:
Reclaimed water: minimum requirements for urban waste water reuse
REclaimed water : minimum requirements to be met by EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — minimum requirements on the reuse of treated urban wastewater
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements that EU countries must meet in order to re-use treated municipal waste water from urban treatment plants for agricultural purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to treated municipal wastewater from urban waste treatment plants.
It does not apply to treated urban waste from industrial wastewater treatment plants or treated municipal water used for drinking water.
The minimum requirements are set out in Annex I of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that, at the point of compliance, reclaimed water intended for agricultural irrigation complies with the following requirements:
minimum requirements for water quality laid down in Section 2 of Annex I;
any additional conditions set by the competent authority in the relevant permit pursuant to points (c) and (d) of Article 6(3), as regards water quality.
Reclamation facility operators must:
ensure that the reclamation facility operator meets the minimum requirements;
provide the relevant authorities with information on the water reclamation process and the results of the process;
have the capacity to plan the supply of reclaimed water in line with the demand from end-users;
use the water in accordance with the water management plans established by the EU countries.

Summary 41:
Beneficiaries of international protection — access to the EU blue card for highly qualified third-country nationals who are beneficiaries of the right to free movement (Directive 2011/95/EU)
Beneficiés of International Protection — Access to the European Union blue card
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/852 — granting highly qualified non-EU nationals the right of free movement within the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that highly qualified nationals who have benefited from international protection in another EU country have the same rights as other EU citizens.
It also aims to improve the integration of highly qualified migrants into the labour market in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
non-EU citizens who have been granted international protection by an EU country and who are highly qualified;
third-country national who have acquired EU long-term resident status in a EU country;
highly qualified workers who have left their country of origin.
The legislation does not apply to highly qualified citizens of the EU countries concerned.
EU countries must ensure that the competent authorities of the country in which the beneficiary is resident have the necessary information to determine whether the beneficiary has benefited from the protection granted by the EU country in question.
If the beneficiary does not meet the criteria for granting the protection, EU countries must issue a blue card to him or her. The card must be valid for a period of at least 24 months. It must be at least 1,0 times, but not higher than 1,6 times, the average gross annual salary in the country concerned. EU countries may refuse to issue the card if:
the documents presented have been fraudulently acquired, falsified or tampered with;
the beneficiary is considered to pose a threat to public policy, public security or public health; or
the employer’s business was established or operates for the main purpose of facilitating the entry of the beneficiary.
An EU country may issue a new blue card if it considers that the beneficiary no longer meets the criteria.
A beneficiary may be expelled from the EU if he or she does not fulfil the conditions of the blue card.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directives 2011/96/EU and 2011/98/EU have applied since 1 July 2011.
Directions 2011/97/EU has applied since 31 December 2011 and Directive 2011/99/EU had to become law in the Member States by 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a comprehensive labour migration policy and for better integration of migrants.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and integration’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Highly qualified: a person with a high level of education, training or experience who is highly qualified in a specific field.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU), of 20 June 2019, concerning the granting of a right of freedom of movement by Member States to highly-qualified non-European Union citizens and repealing Directive 2003/109/EC (OJ L 347, 21.6.2019, pp. 1-5)

Summary 42:
Integrated Border Management Fund (IBMF)
Integrated border management fund (IBFM)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/523 on the implementation of the integrated border management Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE IMPLEMENTING REGULATION?
It sets out the principles and procedures for the management of the EU’s external border management (IBM) fund.
It aims to support the Member States’ and the European Union (EU), including the European Commission, in their efforts to tackle the trafficking of migrants and to encourage compliance with legal migration rules, thereby safeguarding the integrity of EU countries’ immigration systems.
KEY POINTS
The IBMF is managed by the Commission, which has the powers to:
programme, monitor and evaluate, manage and control the IBMF;
provide financial support to Member States and the EU, in particular the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund (EMF), the Just Transition Fund, the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund (AMIF), the Internal Security Fund and the Instrument for Financial Support for Border Management and Visa Policy (IFSP).
The regulation lays down the common principles for the implementation, monitoring and evaluation, management and control for IBMF.
The objectives of IBMF are:
to combat trafficking in human beings and the exploitation of vulnerable persons;
to promote the integration of migrants into the labour market and the social cohesion of the Member State in which they are resident;
support the efforts of Member States to integrate migrants into their society;
promote the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
encourage cooperation between Member States in the field of border management and visa policy;
facilitate the exchange of information and information on the situation at the external border;
contribute to the development of national border management strategies;
ensure the efficient use of the internal border management system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPJECTIVE APPLY?
The implementing regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The IMBMF is part of the Integrated Border Management Facility (IBMF).
For more information, see:
IMBMF (European External Action Service).
KEY TERMS
* Trafficking of migrants: a person who is trafficked for the purpose of exploiting, exploiting or exploiting human beings.
* Migrant exploitation: the exploitation, exploitation or exploitation of human beings for the purposes of sexual exploitation or sexual exploitation.

Summary 43:
Parental leave: equal treatment for fathers and mothers
Parental leaving: equal treatments for fathers AND mothers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/22/EU on parental leave
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that men and women have equal treatment when it comes to parental leave.
It repeals and replaces Directive 2010/18/EU, which provided for a minimum period of parental leave for both mothers and fathers.
KEY POINTS
The directive sets out the minimum amount of paid parental leave that each parent has the right to take on the occasion of the birth of their child.
EU countries must ensure that fathers or, where and insofar as recognised by national law, equivalent second parents, have the same amount of leave as mothers.
The legislation does not apply to criminal proceedings, unless otherwise provided by the EU country in which the child was born.
Employers must provide flexible working arrangements for parents and carers.
Workers must be informed of promotion processes and internal vacancies and should be able to participate in such processes and to apply for such vacancies.
Flexible working arrangements can change depending on the needs of both the employer and the worker.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 20 April 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive on its website.
For more information, see:
‘Paternity leave’ on the European Commission's website

Summary 44:
Fraud affecting the EU’s financial interests — definition of criminal offences
Fraud impacting the EU's financial interests – definitions and sanctions
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 — definition and sanctions for fraud affecting the European Union’S financial interests
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out a common definition of fraud falling within the scope of this directive, which should cover fraudulent conduct with respect to revenues, expenditure and assets at the expense of the general budget of the EU (the ‘EU budget’), including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all offences that affect the EU budget, including:
the EU budget;
the budgets of the European institutions, bodies, offices and agencies established pursuant to the Treaties or budgets directly or indirectly managed and monitored by them;
legal persons;
public officials;
natural persons.
Sanctions
EU countries may impose sanctions on natural persons who commit or are suspected of committing or have committed any of the offences referred to in the directive.
The penalties may include:
a maximum sanction of at least 4 years of imprisonment;
a limitation period of 5 years from the time when the criminal offence was committed.
Penalties may also be imposed on legal persons who are perpetrators of the criminal offences or who are criminally liable under Article 5 of the Convention on the Protection of the Union's Financial Interests of 26 July 1995.
In addition to criminal sanctions, EU countries may also provide for sanctions other than criminal sanctions in the following cases:
fraud against the common VAT system;
defrauding the European Central Bank for International Settlements (CBIS);
falsifying or falsifying the accounts of EU countries’ national central banks;
infringing on the integrity of the internal market of a national central bank;
spreading false information to a third party;
misappropriating the assets of a public official;
obstructing the work of a central bank or of an EU institution;
using public funds for personal gain;
taking advantage of public contracts, contracts or other forms of public procurement;
other types of fraud.
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 allows EU countries to impose sanctions for the following offences:
spam, fraud or falsification of accounts;
abuse of the power of the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC);
abuse or misuse of public funds;
corruption and misappropriation of public office;
criminal offences of passive corruption;
passive corruption; and
corruption of public officials, whether holding a formal office in the EU, in the Member States or in third countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
    данскагандранна барка грашка, годна  гласнога варака and гълакова кражакка are the legal names used by the European Commission in implementing the Directive.
BACKGROUND
The EU budget is the main source of revenue and expenditure for the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Fraud’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fraud: the intentional or unintentional loss of revenue or expenditure by a person or entity to a person, entity or body, which is intended or intended to cause a loss to the EU or to the Union.

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 45:
Eliminating direct and indirect obstacles to the proper functioning and completion of an integrated market for electronic payments
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 laying down uniform requirements for card-based payment transactions and internet and mobile payments based on cards
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down uniform rules for the interchange fees charged by payment card schemes and payment service providers for the processing of debit and credit card transactions in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all debit card transactions within a payment card scheme or to transactions with payment cards issued by three parties.
It does not apply to the processing fees charged for the payment of credit cards by card-issuing banks.
The interchange fee is calculated by the European Banking Authority (EBA) on behalf of the European Union (EU).
The EBA may, after consulting an advisory panel, adopt measures to reduce or eliminate the interchange fee.
EU countries must ensure that the EBA adopts measures that are equivalent or equivalent to those of the other EU countries.
In order to ensure that all EU countries comply with the regulation, the European Commission must publish a list of the national competent authorities responsible for collecting data on the volume and value of debit card and payment card transactions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    иганска барка  года гарша  
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report on the implementation of the regulation in December 2016.
For more information, see:
‘Direct and indirect barriers to the functioning of the internal market for e-payments’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Interchange fee: the fee charged by a payment service provider for processing a debit or credit card transaction. It is calculated on the basis of the total value of the transaction, including the cost of the merchant service charge and the fee for service paid to the acquiring bank for each payment.

Summary 48:
Statistics on the transport of goods and passengers by rail (Regulation (EU) 2017/852)
Regulations (EU, EU, EU countries and the European Commission on the statistics on rail transport
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 — statistics on transport by rail
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of statistics on goods and passenger by rail in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries to report to the European Statistical System (Eurostat) every 2 years on the number of passengers and goods transported by rail across the EU.
Each EU country must send the data to Eurostat by 31 December each year.
The data must cover the following types of data:
annual statistics on passenger transport — detailed reporting (Annex I);
year-by-year statistics on traffic flows on the rail network;
quarterly statistics on freight transport;
regional statistics on railway traffic flows;
statistics on rail network.
EU countries must ensure that the data are collected and transmitted in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
National authorities must take measures for the coordination of the data sources used and to ensure the quality of the statistics transmitted to the Eurostat.
Eurostat must assess the quality and the data transmitted, including at expert level, the data collection methods used and the quality results produced.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 ?
Regulating (EU countries had to incorporate the regulation into national law by 1 January 2018.
Regulation(EU countries have to incorporate it into national legislation by 1 February 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for implementing the regulation since 1 January 2017.

Rail transport statistics (European Commission).

Summary 49:
Suspension of the visa requirement for nationals of non-EU countries (Schengen acquis)
Temporary suspension of the visas requirement for non-European Union citizens
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 on the temporary suspension of visa requirements for nationals from non-european countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for suspending the visas of nationals of third countries who are not nationals of an EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to nationals of at least one EU country who do not have a valid visa.
It does not apply to:
non-EU citizens who are resident in the Schengen area of the European Economic Area (EEA).
It applies to the following countries:
Belgium,
Côte d'Ivoire,
Denmark,
Estonia,
Finland,
Greece,
Guatemala,
Honduras,
Jamaica,
Kosovo,
Lithuania,
Latvia,
Mongolia,
Morocco,
Nigeria,
Norway,
Seychelles,
Saint Vincent and the Grenadines,
Serbia,
Slovakia,
Sweden,
Tonga,
Vietnam,
Yugoslavia and
Slovenia.
Annex I to the regulation lays down the procedures to be followed when suspending a visa requirement:
the EU country concerned must notify the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission in writing within 30 days of the implementation by the third country of the requirement;
the European Commission must adopt an implementing act suspending the requirement within 90 days of receiving the notification.
The Commission must communicate the measures it has taken to the EU country which notified it of the suspension to the other EU countries.
If a country does not comply with the directive, it must suspend the exemption for no more than 90 days in any 180-day period, notwithstanding the possibility of having country-specific amendments to those annexes in particular circumstances, for instance as a result of a visa liberalisation process or as the ultimate consequence of a temporary suspension.
A country which does not respect the directive may suspend the visa exemption for a limited period of time, provided that it takes into account the situation of human rights in that third country and the possible consequences of a suspension for that situation.
EU countries must inform the Commission of the measures they have taken and the reasons for them.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2017/852 has applied since 1 January 2018.
Directives (EU, EU, EU countries) 2016/856 and (EU countries) 2018/854 have applied since 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
The visa requirement is one of the main obstacles to entry into the EU and is the main reason why the EU is a transit area for illegal immigrants.
For more information, see:
‘Non-EU nationals’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Temporary suspension: when a country temporarily suspends the visa requirements of its nationals for a period of 90 days.

Summary 50:
Eurojust — EU’s external criminal justice system (Eurojust)
Eurojust is the European Union (EU) institution responsible for investigating and prosecuting cross-border criminal offences.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2014 of 5 March 2014 establishing the European External Criminal Justice System (EuroJust)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the legal framework for Eurojust, an EU institution that investigates and prosecutes criminal offences across the EU.
It aims to improve cooperation between EU countries’ law enforcement authorities in criminal matters.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EU institution is responsible for investigations and prosecutions affecting only one EU country and a third country, where an agreement has been concluded with that third country or where there may be a specific need to involve Eurojust in criminal proceedings.
The European Union institution (EU institution or body) is the EU institution which investigates, prosecutes and investigates criminal offences in all EU countries.
Its tasks include:
sharing information on its cases with other EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies;
collecting, analysing and analysing operational data;
investigating and prosecuting criminal offences;
assisting the European Commission in its work in the field of law enforcement;
supporting the International Criminal Police Organisation (Interpol) in its activities relating to international criminal matters;
facilitating cooperation between Eurojust and non-EU countries;
providing technical assistance to Eurojust.
Evaluation of Eurojust
The regulation sets up a European Parliament and Council committee to assess the effectiveness of EuroJustices of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions (ESCR) and the Commission.

Summary 51:
Fighting money laundering by means of criminal law — effective, proportionate and dissuasive penalties for EU countries’ nationals and companies
Fighting euro-country corruption
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/849 on combating money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering in all EU countries by ensuring more efficient and swifter cross-border cooperation between competent authorities.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to the following types of money laundering:
money laundering involving property derived from criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests, which is subject to specific rules as laid down in Directive 2017/1371 (see summary).
It does not apply to money laundering involving the proceeds of criminal activity.
Penalties
EU countries must ensure the freezing and confiscation of the instrumentalities and proceeds of crime in all cases.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and to the Council by 3 December 2023 assessing the added value of this directive and its provisions necessary to comply with it.
It also requires EU countries to ensure that sufficient personnel and targeted training, resources and up-to-date technological capacity are available.
In addition, EU countries should encourage and support the establishment of effective measures and mechanisms to combat the spread of illicit financial flows.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 3 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The fight against corruption is one of the main priorities of the European Union (EU).

Summary 53:
Injunctive pricing of vessels — EU countries’ obligations to ensure fair competition
In injurious pricing of ships — EU governments’ responsibilities to ensure fairer competition
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — injurious prices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the principles and procedures for determining whether a vessel is being sold at a lower price than the normal value compared with the price of similar vessels in the EU’s shipbuilding industry.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the sale of vessels of the EU industry to a non-EU country.
It does not apply to:
the sale of a vessel to an EU country for the purpose of the construction of a passenger vessel or a cargo vessel;
the supply of vessels for the purposes of the transport of goods between EU countries; or
the export of goods for the use of EU countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Injurious pricing (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Imputative price: the difference between the export price and the normal price of a product or service for which the buyer is entitled to receive compensation.
* Expiry date: the date on which the vessel is due to be delivered.
Expiry period: the period from the date of delivery of the vessel to the date at which it is due for delivery to the vessel for which it will be delivered to the buyer.
Shipyard: the company that builds the vessel.
MAINJoint venture: a joint venture between a shipyard and a shipbuilding company.

Summary 54:
Cableway installations — conformity assessment
Cableways — conformity assessments
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the conformity assessment of cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the safety of cableways in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the procedures for authorising the construction and the entry into service of installations which are located within the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to installations located on the territory of an EU country.
It does not apply to cableways located in non-EU countries.
EU countries must notify the competent national authority responsible for notifying and market surveillance of installations located within their territory.
The competent authority must ensure that the installations comply with the requirements of the regulation and that the competent authority has carried out a conformity assessment before authorising them to be put into service.
Implementation
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. It has delegated the powers to adopt implementing acts to the European Parliament and the Council to ensure that it is implemented correctly.
In addition, the Commission has the power to adopt an implementing act to ensure uniform conditions for the application of the implementing act.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 21 March 2011 and had to become law in the EU countries by 21 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
Directive 2000/9/EC is based on the ‘new approach’ principles, as set out in the Council Resolution of 7 May 1985 on a new approach to technical harmonisation and standards.
For more information, see:
‘New approach to technological harmonisation’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Conformity assessment: the process by which manufacturers, importers and distributors check whether a product complies with the relevant technical specifications.
Safety assessment: a procedure by which a manufacturer, importer or distributor checks that a product meets the relevant safety requirements.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for people with disabilities (Directive 2017/1564/EU)
Assistance of persons with disabilities with regard to the sale, putting on the market or putting into service of products, services and information
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/65/EC on the accessibility of goods and services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to increase the availability of accessible products* on the European Union (EU) market and to improve access to these products by improving their accessibility.
It sets out the responsibilities of manufacturers, importers, distributors and service providers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
electronic products, including desktops, notebooks, smartphones and tablets;
services providing access to audiovisual media services, including websites, online applications, set-top boxes and video conferencing;
computer hardware systems for the processing and storage of data;
electrical equipment for the storage and transmission of data.
Products
Manufacturers are responsible for ensuring that their products comply with the accessibility requirements set out in Annex I of the directive.
Importers must indicate their name, registered trade name or registered trade mark and the address at which they can be contacted on the product or, where that is not possible, on its packaging or in a document accompanying the product.
Distributors must indicate the name of the service provider or the address of that service provider on their products or on their services.
EU countries must ensure, in accordance with the directive’s annexes, that economic operators only place on the EU market products and only provide services that comply with accessibility requirements.
Disproportionate burden
If a product or service imposes a disproportionate burden on a consumer with a disability, the EU country concerned must take the necessary measures to ensure that the non-compliant product is withdrawn from their market and inform the European Commission accordingly.
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the progress made in implementing the directive and on the measures to be taken to eliminate the disproportionate burden.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
    данска ванта ганда банкова  
It has applied since 20 June 2009 and had to become law in the EU countries by 20 June 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
Accessibility — EU rules
Accessible products
Accessive services
KEY TERMS
* Accessible products: products that allow users with disabilities to use their assistive technologies.
* Electronic equipment: a device that can be put into use by a user, such as a computer, notebook, smartphone or tablet.

Summary 56:
EU system for supply chain due diligence for metals and minerals related to the conflict in the African Great Lakes region (AGLR)
European Union system for supplier-holder relationship (SDR)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — EU system for suppliers-holder relation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union system to ensure that the supply chain of minerals and metals related to conflict in Africa and the Great Lakes Region (GLR) is traceable.
It aims to reduce the opportunities for armed groups and security forces to trade in tin, tantalum and tungsten, their ores, and gold.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the supply of minerals, including:
tint and tantalum;
tungsten;
gold;
mineral-related products such as copper, cobalt, zinc, lead, nickel, and cobalt.
Importers are responsible for ensuring that:
the minerals they buy are traceable;
they carry out independent third-party audits of the entire supply chain;
the smelters and refiners in their supply chain comply with the regulation’s requirements.
EU importers of metals are exempted from the obligation to carry out third party audits provided they make available substantive evidence, including third- party audit reports, demonstrating that all smelter and refining facilities in the EU have complied with this regulation.
Ex-post checks on EU importers
EU countries must carry out an ex-post check on each EU importer of metals within the scope of the regulation every 3 years.
The Commission must make publicly available, including on the internet, a list of competent authorities using the template in Annex III to the regulation. The Commission must keep that list up-to-date.
In addition, EU countries must set up a delegation to assess the impact of this regulation on EU economic operators including SMEs, and to report to the European Parliament and to the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
    данска грациальна баркова  года
It has applied since 1 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Supply chain: the process by which minerals are sourced, processed, refined and sold to end-users.
* Traceability: tracing back the origins of minerals to trace them to their final destination.
For more information, see:
‘Supply chain traceability’ on the European Commission's website
* Mineral traceability: traceability of minerals (European Commission).
* Tint: traceable traceability is the process through which minerals can be traced back to their source. It is the method used to trace the minerals used in the manufacture of tin.
• Tungsten: trace tracing is the procedure by which the minerals are traced to the source of their production.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ибрагов галки горски  бодск  
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017 / 852 of 20 July 2017 establishing a Union system of suppliers-holders relation (OJ L 168, 20.7.2017, pp. 1-2)

Summary 57:
Customs debt on import and export — rules for managing the EU’s implementing powers
Customs debts on import — rules governing the management of the EU's implementing powers (recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — rules on managing the implementing powers of the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the management by the Commission of the powers delegated to it by the EU countries to manage the implementation powers of Regulation (EU, Euratom).
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 181/2011.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods imported into the EU and exported from the EU.
It does not apply to goods imported from non-EU countries.
Importers must submit a customs declaration for release for free circulation, temporary admission or temporary admission with partial relief.
The declaration must contain the following information:
the name of the person who brought the goods into the customs territory of the Union;
the person in whose name or on whose behalf the goods were brought into that territory and who assumed responsibility for carriage of the goods after they were brought.
If the declaration is not verified, the goods must be placed under a storage procedure or re-exported in accordance with the procedures laid down in the regulation.
A temporary storage declaration must be kept by the customs authorities for the purpose of verifying that the goods to which it relates are subsequently placed under an import or export procedure.
Storage in customs warehouses must be accompanied by the competent authorities in the EU country where the goods are to be placed.
Temporary storage declarations may be kept for up to 5 years and may be revoked where no longer justified.
An authorisation must be granted to an authorised economic operator (i.e. a person who is authorised to carry out activities in the free zone of an EU country).
The authorisation is valid for 5 years from the date of entry into force of the decision to grant the authorisation. The authorisation may be suspended or revoked where the decision was taken or was applied for.
EU countries have the power to adopt delegated acts, in order to determine the conditions for granting authorisation to an economic operator.
Duty on import
The Commission has the powers to adopt implementing acts to:
reduce the amount of import duty already paid by the importer;
suspend or reduce the import duty on goods that have not been paid;
restrict or suspend the export of goods which have not yet been paid.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    Вода баркова гаранска ванка  бъланда нашка, карша данна коганца,  харна  баснагача и годнака and горхнала мананта подка
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  Изгонацка is one of the most important legal instruments used by the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Customs’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Importers’: any person or business that brings goods into or leaves the EU, or has the right to bring them into or leave the EU by sea or air.
* Authorised economic operator: a person, business or organisation that is authorised by a national authority to carry goods into, or leave them in, the EU or to carry them out.

Summary 58:
Ensuring the enforcement of intellectual property rights in the European Union
Ensuing the enforceability of intellectualproperty rights in EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — enforcing intellectual property right in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ customs authorities are able to enforce intellectual property (IPR) rights when importing goods from non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
goods imported into the EU infringe IPR and that they are seized by the customs authorities;
the competent customs authorities carry out adequate controls to detect and control infringements of IPR.
The regulation sets out the procedures for applying for an application to enforce IPR, as well as the information to be provided by the holder of the application.
It also lays down the conditions under which an application may be refused or amended.
Customs authorities must:
ensure that goods suspected of infringing an IPR are seized and detained;
take proportionate identification measures to identify the goods;
carry out an inspection and sampling of the goods before they are released or detained.
Inspection and sampling
The competent customs department of the EU country in which the application has been submitted must forward the following decisions to the competent customs departments of the other EU countries indicated in the application, immediately after their adoption:
decisions granting the application;
deciding revoking decisions granting the decision;
Decisions extending the period during which the authorities are to take action.
If an application for compensation is refused or amending, the holder must return the samples to the authorities on completion of the analysis, at the latest before the goods are released.
An EU country’s customs authorities must also:
assess whether the goods infringe intellectual property and determine whether they are liable to be seized;
make a decision on whether to seize the goods if they are found to have infringed IPR;
inform the European Commission of the decision to compensate in the event that goods are not detained or released or if no action is taken to detain them.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Ensourcing intellectual property’ on the European commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Im implementing Regulation (EC) No 1752/2017 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2017 on enforcing intellectual Property rights in accordance with Union customs legislation (OJ L 347, 12.12.2017, pp. 1-8)

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/240 establishing a customs programme at EU level
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Customs programme (ECP) to support the European Union (EU), the EU countries and the European Commission by providing a framework for actions that aim to strengthen cooperation between the EU and its customs authorities.

KEY POINTS
Scope
The ECP is a multiannual programme, which runs from 2021 to 2027.

administrative actions, such as on-the-spot checks and inspections, with a view to establishing whether there has been fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of EU countries.
Implementation
Implementing actions are carried out by EU countries, the Commission and EU countries’ competent authorities. The ECP may be terminated or adjusted in order to make them more effective or relevant if they prove to be inadequate at any point in time.

provide young people in the EU with opportunities to participate in solidarity activities;

The regulation sets out the rules for the organisation of the ESC.

voluntary activities in priority areas identified at EU level and solidarity activities in support of humanitarian aid operations in non-EU countries;
participation of individuals;
organisations;
social enterprises;
non-profit organisations.
Participation of NGOs
EU countries may also apply for funding on behalf of informal groups of participants.

Summary 63:
Transparency of interest representation in the EU — transparency of the EU institutions’ decision-making processes
Transparency on interest representation
SUMMARY OF:
Framework Agreement on Transparency of the Decision-Making Processes of the European Union institutions, bodies, offices and agencies
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — conclusion of the Framework Agreement on transparency of decision-Making processes of the Union institutions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that the decision-makers of the signatory institutions (the EU institutions) are fully informed of the activities carried out by interest representatives* with the objective of influencing the formulation or implementation of policy or legislation.
The decision concludes the framework agreement on behalf of EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The framework agreement applies to all EU institutions and bodies, bodies and agencies that have a role in the formulation, implementation or evaluation of EU policies, programmes and policies.
It does not apply to non-governmental organisations (NGOs) that are part of the public sector.
Eligibility and code of conduct
An applicant for a registration in the transparency register must meet the following criteria:
be an EU citizen;
be a national or subnational resident of an EU country;
have a minimum age of at least 18 years;
meet at least one EU institution, body, office or agency;
represent the interests of a group of EU citizens, bodies or agencies, including the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Regions, the Commission and the Commission;
not be a member of a non-profit-making body, such as the European Atomic Energy Community or the Committee for the Protection of the Rights of the Child.
Registrants may not:
promote their own interests or the collective interests of their members vis-à-vis any EU institution or body;
provide an up-to-date estimate of the annual costs related to covered activities according to the grid below.
Registration
The register is operated by the European Commission, which is responsible for the management and operation of the register.
Annex I of the agreement sets out the information that can be entered in the register, including:
the name of the applicant;
the applicant’s contact details;
contact details of the registrant;
a description of the type of activities covered by the agreement.
Access to the register
The European Commission publishes on its website a list of the institutions that are signatories to the agreement, as well as the information they make available to the public on their websites.
FROM WHEN DOES THE AGEMENT APPLY?
    иганска грациальна баркова  года  шанда варскога коглашин гарнов горакод гендов  
It entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Transparency’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 65:
Economic and social support for COVID-19 countries
Economic and Social support for CoVID - EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — an exceptional and coordinated programme of economic and social assistance for countries affected by the COVID crisis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to provide financial support to EU countries that have been particularly hard hit by the crisis caused by the measures taken by the EU countries in response to the crisis in the former Yugoslavia.
KEY POINTS
The measures are part of the EU’s ‘CoVID’ programme, which aims to boost the recovery of the economy and jobs in the EU by providing financial support for EU countries which have been hit particularly hard by COVID.
The programme is designed to support EU countries with the necessary resources to recover their economies and jobs.
It is financed by EU countries’ own own resources.
In order to prevent further deterioration in the economy, employment and social cohesion and to boost a sustainable and resilient recovery of economic activity, the measures are designed to:
provide financial support in the form of commitment appropriations (i.e. money that can be used to cover the costs of implementing the measures);
support for the development of sustainable, resilient and inclusive economic growth in EU countries;
encourage investment in the sectors that have suffered the most from COVID, such as tourism, agriculture and rural areas;
support social cohesion in the areas of education, health and social protection, social protection and the environment.
support measures for social cohesion
Support measures
The EU countries must provide the necessary financial resources for the implementation of the measures.
To achieve this, the Commission must draw up a programme of measures to support the economic recovery of COVID countries.

Summary 66:
Sanctions under the euro area’s Stability and Growth Pact (SGP) and Regulation (EC) No 1466/97 on excessive government deficits
Sanctions within the euro zone
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — fines for excessive government deficit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedure for imposing fines on euro area countries that fail to comply with the rules of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ governments are held to account for their excessive public deficit and debt.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to non-euro area countries.
It applies to countries that do not use the euro and which have not adopted the euro by 1 January 2018.
The fines are calculated according to the gross domestic product (GDP) of the EU country concerned. The amount of the fine must not exceed 0.2 % of GDP of the Member State concerned.
Imposing the fine
The fine must be lodged with the European Commission within 10 days of a Council decision to impose the fine. The fine may be reduced or increased by the Council on grounds of exceptional economic circumstances or following a reasoned request by the EU countries. The Commission may also recommend that the Council reduce or cancel the fine or cancel it.
A non-interest-bearing deposit is required to lodge the fine with the Commission. The deposit.
If the non-laying deposit exceeds the amount of a fine, the country must make up the shortfall when it lodges the deposit. If the deposit is made up of the difference between the interest paid and the amount to be paid by the country that lodges it. The EU country must notify the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee of the interest it has paid on the deposit and the interest accrued on the interest.
EU countries that have not complied with the fines may be subject to a fine of up to 0.5 % of their GDP if they fail to meet the medium-term budgetary objective and do not meet the minimum requirements for national budgetary frameworks.
Penalties
The European Commission may impose fines on EU countries which do not comply with this regulation. The penalties may include:
a fine of €1,000,000 for each EU country that fails to comply;
a penalty of €500,000 if the country does not meet these minimum requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Вганска барка  года гарца ванка, гостранна какова данцка and гхарна  ходкабака are repealed and replaced by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/853 (see summary).
BACKGROUND
The TFEU allows the adoption of specific measures in the EU which go beyond the provisions applicable to all Member States, for the purpose of ensuring the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union.
For more information, see:
‘Sanctions’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Excessive government deficit: the deficit of a country which exceeds the medium term budgetary objective by more than 2 % of its gross national product (GNDP) and which is not in a position to meet its medium term budget objective.
* Gross domestic product: the sum of the gross national income of an EU country divided by the total value of the goods and services it produces and imports.

Summary 67:
Population and housing statistics — data on the population and the main family, social, economic, economic and housing characteristics of persons
Statistics on population and housing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 176/2008 on the production and dissemination of Community statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and use of European Union (EU) data on population, social and economic characteristics of people.
It aims to ensure the comparability of the data provided by the EU countries and for reliable overviews to be drawn up at EU level.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
The European Commission (Eurostat) is responsible for collecting and disseminating the data.
EU countries must transmit the data to Eurostat in electronic form in accordance with the technical specifications of the topics covered by the regulation.
Data sources and methodology
Each EU country must select a data source and methodology for each of the following categories of data:
the national level, NUTS 1 and 2;
the geographical level;
regional level, MUTS 3;
local level, LUTS 4;
other geographical levels;
non-derived topics (e.g. place of usual residence, place of work, location of place of residence, etc.).
The national and EU statistical authorities must ensure that the chosen data sources and methods are compatible with the essential features of the relevant data sources.
they must also take into account of the essential characteristics of the population* and housing* characteristics.
Quality of data transmitted
The data transmitted by EU countries must meet the requirements of data protection.
National and EU countries are responsible for ensuring that the data transmitted are of a high quality and that are comparable to the data collected by the national and European statistical authorities and that they meet the essential requirements of the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESDP).
Quality reports
Each year, EU countries send Eurostat a quality report on the quality of their data transmitted to the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Population and Housing Statistics’ on Eurostat’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Population and housing: statistics on the total population of an EU country, the total number of people living in that country and the number of persons living in its territory.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 178/2008 of 20 December 2008 on the collection and transmission of European statistics (OJ L 61, 5.1.2008, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Euratom) no 178/08 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 July 2019
Rules on the Rules of Procedure and the procedure for the adoption of acts
SUMMARY OF:
RULES OF PROCEDURE
Decision (EU) 2019/302 on the rules of procedure of the EU Parliament
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE RULES?
The decision and the rules set out the procedures for the EU’s Parliament and the Council.
The decisions and rules lay down the procedures and competences of the Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
KEY POINTS
The rules of Procedure
The European Parliament has the power to adopt, adopt, amend and adopt acts and measures in the interest of the Union and the EU countries.
Membership
The members of Parliament are elected for a term of 5 years. They represent the European Union (EU), the European Community (now the EU) countries and the Member States of the Schengen area.
They elect a president and a vice-president for each parliamentary term. The president is assisted by a quaestor, who is responsible for administrative and financial matters directly concerning Members, as well as for other tasks entrusted to them.
Duties of the Quaestors
The president and vice-presidents are appointed by the president of the Conference of Presidents. They are responsible for appointing a quorum of representatives from each EU country. The quorum is set out in the following ways:
the president is elected by the quorum, which is set by the President of the Commission, the president is chosen by the Vice-Presidents of the countries of the EEC,
the President is nominated by the Council and the President appoints a vice president, and
the Vice-president is chosen for a 5-year term by the High Level Group on Gender Equality and Diversity (HLGD).
The President and Vice Presidents are assisted by the Ombudsman, who assists the President in the case of death, resignation or dismissal of the Ombudsmen.
Eligibility of Members
Members of Parliament must respect the dignity of the institution and its members. They must also respect the rights of the public and the fundamental rights of EU citizens. Members must respect Parliament's dignity and must not harm its reputation.
Access to documents and provision of information to Parliament
Members have the right to access the documents and information they need for their work in the institution.
Procedure to be applied when drawing up Parliament's estimates
The estimates are drawn up by the Committee of the Regions and the Committee for the Protection of the Fundamental Rights of EU Citizens.
When drawing up the estimates, the committee of the regions and the committee responsible for the protection of fundamental rights draw up draft recommendations. The committee must then draw up a report on the results of the estimates. The report must be published in the Official Journal.
In the event of differences between the estimates and the conclusions of the committees, the conclusions must be taken into account in drawing up an estimate.
Political parties
The parties are represented in Parliament by a political group. The parties are divided into 3 groups:
Political groups are represented by a Conference of Representatives, which meets at least once a year.
Each party is represented by one representative of each of the 3 political groups. Representatives of the parties are elected by a majority of the members of the House of Representatives.
There is no fixed number of members for each political group and the number of Members of each party is determined by a secret ballot. The number of seats allocated to each party in each parliamentary session is fixed by a procedure known as the ‘selection procedure’.
Rules of procedure
The procedure for adopting and amending the agenda
The Parliament adopts and amends the agenda on the basis of a proposal for a legally binding act, in whole or in part, by means of a vote. The proposal must be put to a single vote, unless, at the request of a political party or Members reaching at least the low threshold, Parliament decides to proceed immediately with the vote on amendments in accordance with the procedure laid down in Article 3 of the Treaty on European Union.
A motion to amend the agenda may not:
contain any decision on matters for which other specific procedures or competences are laid down;
deal with the subject of ongoing proceedings in Parliament.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/2195 has applied since 1 July 2018.
Decisions (EU and EEC) 2017/2193 have applied since 31 December 2017.
Directives (EU/EEC) 2018 /2195 and (Euratom) 2016/1125 have applied from 1 January 2019.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition into force

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) — European Union institutions, bodies, offices and agencies — data protection and data protection policy
European data protection supervisor (EDPB)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/1725 — establishing an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of natural persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes an independent European Union institution, body, office or agency responsible for the protection of the rights and fundamental freedoms of the natural persons of the European Union (EU).
The regulation sets out the role and responsibilities of the EDPB, which is responsible for:
ensuring the protection and integrity of the personal data of EU citizens and of EU institutions and bodies;
protecting the privacy and security of the data of the EU citizens, of EU bodies, bodies and offices;
assisting the European Commission in its work in the field of data protection;
providing the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions with information on data protection issues.
KEY POINTS
The EPDB is composed of three members and three deputies:
the Director;
the Head of Unit or Head of Sector with the highest grade or, in the event of equal grade, by the head of unit or head of sector with the lowest seniority within the grade or by the eldest.
The director is assisted by the Director and the Appointing Authority, who are appointed for a period of 2 years by the EDPS.
A Management Meeting is held once per week. The management meeting ensures strategic oversight of the work of the eDPB and ensures that the strategic priorities set by the director and the director are implemented.
Each year, a risk management process and the annual activity report are published on the ePDB’s website.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Decisions (EC) No 1725/2018 and (EU/2017/1939) have applied since 1 March 2018.
Regulation (EU, EU) 2017/2039 has applied since 31 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for implementing the European data protection directive since 1 January 2018. It has delegated responsibility for the implementation of the directive to the European Directorate-General for the Protection of the Fundamental Rights and Freedoms of the Union.
For more information, see:
‘Data Protection Supervisor’ on the European Agency for Fundamental Rights’‘EU institutions’ website
* KEY TERMS
* Data protection supervisor: an independent EU institution or body, body or office responsible for safeguarding the rights of individuals and their rights to data.
* EU institution: a body or body responsible for protecting the fundamental freedoms and fundamental rights of the individuals concerned.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EU), of 27 February 2018, establishing an Independent European Data Protection Supervisory Supervisor (EPDB) (OJ L 347, 28.2.2018, pp. 1-8)
