Summary 70:
The EU’s financial framework for the period 2021-2027 (MFF 2021-2020)
The European Union's multiannual financial framework will be extended until 31 December 2023.
ACT
Decision (EU) 2020/852/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 17 July 2020 on the system of own resources
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DECISION DO?
It sets out the rules for the use of EU countries’ own resources (known as ‘own resources’) to finance the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out how EU countries will be able to use their own resources to finance their own budget.
The EU will use its own resources for a number of purposes, including:
to finance the general budget of the EU (‘the EU budget’);
to provide short-term financing on capital markets to the European Commission for the sole and exclusive purpose of financing measures to address the consequences of the COVID-19 crisis;
to support the European Stability Mechanism (ESM) and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The new own resources will be used for the following purposes:
the general budget;
the EIB and the EIB;
short-term loans to the Commission;
long-term loan to EU countries;
other EU countries.
WHEN DOES THE DECISION APPLY?
From 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
In order to better align the EU budget with its policy priorities, to better reflect the role of the general EU budget in the functioning of the single market and to better support the objectives of EU policies, and to reduce Member States’ contributions based on gross national income (GNI) to the EU's annual budget, the European Council concluded in July 2020 that over the coming years the EU would work towards reforming the system for own resources.
For more information, see:
‘The EU budget and its own resource system’ on the European External Action Service's website.

Summary 71:
Tyre labelling of tyres — rolling resistance, wet grip, external rolling noise and other essential parameters
Tyre label of cars, vans and heavy-duty vehicles
Tire labels of cars and vans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009 — tyre labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to provide consumers with information on the rolling resistance (RRC) and wet grip (WG) characteristics of tyres to enable them to make an informed choice when buying tyres.
It also aims to reduce the environmental impact of road transport by promoting fuel‐efficient, long‐lasting and safe tyres with low noise levels.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the requirements for tyres to be labelled on the European Union (EU) market. It applies to tyres for cars (C1 tyres) and vans (C2 tyres) but not for heavy‐duty vehicles (C3 tyres).
It does not apply to re‐treaded tyres or second‐hand tyres, unless such tyres are imported from a non-EU country.
Tyres must bear the following information:
the fuel efficiency class;
the rolling resistance coefficient (RC);
the wet grip coefficient (Wg);
external rolling noise (dB);
a description of the type of tyre;
a list of tyres available for sale in the EU.
The information on tyres must be provided in technical promotional material in the following order:
in the form of a list of fuel efficiency classes;
instructions on the tyre’s characteristics;
information on tyres’ fuel efficiency;
an indication of the wet grip class and the RRC of the tyre.
inform consumers of the characteristics of the tyres available on the EU market.
Manufacturers must ensure that tyres are labelled in accordance with the specifications set out in the regulation. They must also ensure that the tyres are accompanied by a description of their fuel efficiency and their wet grip coefficients.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULAMENDMENT APPLY?
   
  да   иг  б  г  б 
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
Fuel efficiency — tyres (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Treaded tyre: a type of rubber used for road transport.
* Wet grip coefficient: the coefficient of friction between the tyre and the road surface.
ROLLING RACING CROSSING CRAP: the resistance of a rubber surface to the pressure applied by the wheels of a vehicle to the road.

Summary 72:
Integrated Border Management Fund (IBMF)
IntegratedBorder Management Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/444 — Integrated Border Management Instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an integrated border management fund for the period 2021-2027 to support the EU’s external borders.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the scope of the IBMF, including:
the purchase or upgrade of customs control equipment;
measures to prevent, detect, correction and investigate fraud;
the recovery of funds lost, wrongly paid or incorrectly used;
costs relating to personal protective equipment.
Implementation
The IBMF is managed by the European Commission.
It is funded by the EU budget.
Funding
The EU budget is divided into 3 categories:
EU funding for the purchase and upgrade of EU programmes and instruments;
EU funds for the maintenance and repair of customs controls equipment; and
EU grants for the repair and maintenance of customs checks.
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the IIMF.
Duties of the Commission
The European Commission has delegated the power to adopt delegated acts to the EU countries’ competent authorities. These delegated acts are used to ensure that EU countries carry out their duties effectively and efficiently.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 5 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Integrated border management’ is a term used in the EU to refer to a set of rules designed to improve the control of the external borders of the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Customs’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Integrated border management: an instrument designed to assist EU countries in their external borders in order to ensure both safety and security.
* Customs: a system of border controls that allows EU countries to control goods entering and leaving the EU by means of a single set of controls.

Summary 73:
Electricity market rules — balancing, metering and settlement of electricity and gas
Electricity markets — balancing and metering of electrical energy and natural gas
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/943 — balancing electricity market rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that electricity consumers have access to affordable, reliable and competitive electricity in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The directive aims to:
ensure a level playing field for consumers in the electricity market;
improve the transparency of electricity markets;
provide consumers with the information they need to make informed choices and to participate in the competitive market.
It repeals and replaces Directive 2012/27/EU (see summary).
Scope
The legislation applies to all electricity distribution systems in the EU. It does not apply to gas distribution systems.
The main objectives of the legislation are to ensure:
a high level of security of supply for consumers;
fair competition in the market for electricity;
the protection of consumers’ interests;
protection of the environment;
harmonisation of energy markets.
Regulatory framework
EU countries must ensure that:
the European Commission has the power to draw up a regulatory framework for electricity transmission system operators;
transmission system operators are able to procure flexibility services, including congestion management in their areas, in order to improve efficiencies in the operation and development of the distribution system;
market participants have the right to meet their needs;
to participate in market-based electricity price contracts;
demand response through aggregated loads;
consumer participation is encouraged;
tariff-related measures are in place to encourage investment in the transmission system operator’s internal accounts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘Electricity’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Electricity market: a system where electricity is supplied to consumers by a single distribution system.

Summary 74:
European High Performance Computing Joint Undertaking (EuroHPCJoint Undertaking)
European high performance computing Joint Undering (EurohPCJ)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 establishing the European High Performance Computers Joint Underlying
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European high-performance computing joint under the European Commission’s Horizon Europe programme.
It aims to provide the European Union with world-class supercomputing, quantum computing and data capabilities according to its economic potential, matching the needs of European users.
KEY POINTS
The European High-Performance Computing Joint undertaking is a public-private partnership (PPP) set up by the Commission under the Horizon Europe initiative.
The EU is a member of the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA).
The joint undertaking was established for a period until 31 December 2033 for the implementation of the initiative on European High performance computing, a joint initiative within the meaning of Article 187 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).
It is responsible for the management of the EuroHPC supercomputers and for the operation of the hosting consortium.
In addition, it is responsible to:
provide technical support to the participating countries in the initiative;
facilitate the use of the supercomputer infrastructure by participating countries;
support the development of new supercomputer technologies, such as nanotechnologies, to meet the requirements of the users;
ensure the availability of supercomputer resources to the public and private sectors;
contribute to the research, development and testing of new technologies in the field of high-performing computing;
promote research and innovation in the fields of high performance and quantum computing, including through the development and demonstration of new products, technologies and services;
assist in the management and coordination of the activities of participating countries, including by providing technical support and technical expertise;
encourage the participation of the private sector in the project, including the establishment of user-friendly user interfaces for the use and maintenance of the high-powered supercomputer equipment;
make it possible for participating countries to share information on the activities being carried out under the joint undertaking;
take part in the joint undertakings’ activities, in particular by:
participating in the research and development activities of participants;
providing technical support in the activities carried out by participants under the Joint undertakings;
carrying out the administrative work of the Joint Underakings.
Each participating country may entrust a portion of its contribution to the Joint undertaking for the purpose of:
acquisition and owning high-end supercomputer and quantum simulators;
upgrading of the computers;
procurement of new high-speed supercomputer hardware and software;
implementing research and technological development activities;
exchange of technical information and best practices;
the provision of technical assistance to participating countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘European High-performance Computing’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* High-speed computing: a technology that allows computers to perform complex calculations in a very short time.
* Quantum computing: the ability to process data in a way that is impossible to do by conventional computers.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/695 of 11 December 2019 establishing a European Highperformance Computing Jointundertaking (OJ L 183, 12.12.2019, pp. 1-2)
last update 14.02.2020



Summary 75:
Climate-neutrality objective: 2030-2040 targets for EU greenhouse gas emission reductions and measures to tackle climate change
Climate - neutrality objectives: 2030 and 2040
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 401/2009 on the EU’s 2030 climate policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the objectives for the EU to achieve a climate-neutral future by 2030.
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions by 40 % below 1990 levels by the end of the century.
KEY POINTS
The EU is a global leader in the transition towards climate neutrality, and it is determined to help raise global ambition and to strengthen the global response to climate change, using all tools at its disposal, including climate diplomacy.
To achieve this objective, the EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle the effects of climate change and to delivering on the implementation of the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’) in December 2015.
The 2030 target for greenhouse gas emissions is set out in the European Green Deal (see summary).
The objective is to reduce GHG emissions by 30 % by 2040 compared to 1990 levels.
In order to achieve the 2030 target, EU countries and the EU institutions must take the necessary measures at EU and national level to enable the collective achievement of the objective, taking into account the importance of promoting both fairness and solidarity among Member States and cost-effectiveness in achieving this objective.
National measures
The Commission must assess the progress made by EU countries towards achieving the EU 2030 target by 2030 and take appropriate national measures to ensure that the target is met.
A Climate-adopting Advisory Board is set up to advise the Commission on the measures to be taken by the EU countries, the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on the national measures needed to achieve this 2030 target.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    ибаковска башка  бхакахкаккагакцафакта  ххраки  
It has applied since 17 September 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Climate-adAPT’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Carbon capture and storage (CCS): the process of removing carbon dioxide (CO2) from the atmosphere and storing it in the form of heat-trapping gases (e.g. carbon dioxide, methane and nitrous oxide).
* GHG: the greenhouse gas emitted by the burning of fossil fuels (i.e. the CO2 emitted by burning fossil fuels).

Summary 77:
Creative Europe programme for the 2014-2020 period
Creative European programme forthe 2014 - 2020 period
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1295/2013 establishing the Creative Europe programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s Creative Europe Programme (CEP) which aims to support the activities of the cultural and creative sectors in the EU.
It sets out the priorities of the programme and the measures to be taken to achieve them.
KEY POINTS
The CEP covers the following strands:
the Culture strand, which covers cultural and the creative sectors with the exception of the audiovisual sector;
the Media strand which covers the media sector; and
the Cross-sectoral strand (which covers actions across all the sectors) which covers actions in all sectors.
The objectives of the CEP are to:
promote cultural diversity in Europe;
support the EU commitment relating to the UN Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) in particular its cultural dimension;
contribute to the sustainable development of the EU;
foster social inclusion;
encourage the participation of young people in the creative sector in society;
improve the quality of education and training;
increase participation of women in the labour market;
facilitate the exchange of information and culture;
provide a forum for dialogue between the creative and cultural sectors;
raise public awareness of the importance of the work of artists and their work;
ensure the protection of intellectual property rights;
protect the environment and the environment;
strengthen the rights of artists, producers and distributors of works of art and culture.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU’Sustainable development strategy for the EU is the European Green Deal.
For more information, see:
European Green Deal (European Commission).

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?

Scope

Geographical indications

They must be registered by the European Commission and must be accompanied by a description of the specific characteristics of the product and a sales denomination (e.g. the name of the country, region or place of origin of the producer or the place of production of the wine.

a description
the quantity of alcohol per litre
the number of litres of wine in the bottle
the wine’s alcoholic strength
the percentage of alcohol by volume in the volume of wine and the alcohol content in the litre of wine by volume.
Awards of GIs
The European Commission has the power to grant or deny GIs to producers, importers and distributors.

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework (MFF) for the period 2014-2020
Multiannational financial framework for the years 2014-20
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 on the duration of the Multiannual Financial Framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the annual appropriations for the European Union (EU)’s (now the EU) multiannual budgetary framework for a period of 7 years starting on 1 January 2014.
KEY POINTS
The MFF is designed to provide the EU with the financial resources it needs to prepare and implement medium-term investment programmes.
The European Commission sets the MFF ceilings for each of the years covered by the framework. These ceilings are set out in the regulation.
Each year, the Commission must submit a proposal to the European Parliament and the Council on the annual MFF appropriations to be made. The proposals must be submitted by 1 May of the following year. The Commission then decides on the total appropriations for payments for the year.
In the case of a reduction in the ceiling, the European Commission may propose that the difference be financed through the annual amounts made available for the previous year. If the Commission does not propose this, the Parliament and Council must decide on the amount to be financed by the Commission.
Every 3 years, the EU must submit its proposals to the Commission for the annual allocations for the following years. These proposals must include the total amounts for the contributions from the general budget of the EU to those projects and, in particular, their orderly progression in relation to the appropriations for commitments.
These proposals are submitted to the Council and the Parliament for approval.
If the Commission decides that the annual budgetary allocations for a given year do not meet the ceilings set by the EU, it must propose a technical adjustment to the EU budget. This technical adjustment is known as the ‘adjustment of cohesion policy envelopes’.
This regulation sets out how the EU finances its own resources. The EU must respect the own resources ceiling by ensuring that it does not exceed the amount of own resources it receives from the EU.
A Contingency Margin of up to 0.03 % of the Gross National Income of the Union must be constituted outside the ceilings of the framework, as a last-resort instrument to react to unforeseen circumstances.
An Emergency Aid Reserve is set up to provide financial assistance in the event of major disasters occurring on the territory of an EU Member State or of a candidate country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
    Заганска граца баркова васта  
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND

Summary 84:
The European Maritime Fund for Fisheries (EMFAF)
The European Monetary and Fisheries Fund (EMF) is the European Union’s (EU) fisheries fund. It aims to support the sustainable use of the oceans, seas and marine resources for sustainable development.
ACT
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2013 establishing the European Maritime and Fisheries Facility (EMFF) and repealing Regulation (EU/1939)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
    *  *  
It sets out the rules for the EU fisheries fund and the specific rules for providing EU funding, complementing the general rules applicable to the EMFAF.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EMFF is a multiannual fund that provides EU countries with up to €5.5 billion for the sustainable management of their fisheries.
The EU fisheries budget is divided between the EU countries and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
Funding
Each EU country must submit a national work plan to the European Commission every 2 years. The Commission then decides on the amount of EU funding to be provided.
EU countries must ensure that the work plan is in line with the objectives of the EMFF and with the national plans.
Eligible activities
The work plan must include the following:
measures to reduce the impact of fishing on the marine ecosystem;
measures aimed at reducing the negative effects of fishing activities on the environment and human health and welfare;
projects to improve the management of the marine environment, including the protection of marine protected areas (MPAAs) and the conservation of marine ecosystems;
fisheries projects aimed at improving the management and management of marine resources;
programming for support under shared management (e.g. the sharing of fishing opportunities between EU countries);
research and development projects;
environmental, cultural, social and human resources projects.
Programming
Each year, EU countries must submit their work plans to the Commission.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* MPAAs: an international agreement that aims to protect and manage marine resources.
* SHARE: an agreement between an EU country and a non-EU country to manage the resources of the other EU country.

Summary 85:
Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish Stock (ICMST)
Convention of the International Association for the Protection of Stradling Fish Species (IAPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — Convention on the conservation and management of species covered by the International Agreement on the International Convention on Species Conservation and the International Dolphin Conservation Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE AGREEMENT?
The regulation aims to ensure the protection of species of fish stocks and highly migratory fish stocks in the Pacific Ocean.
The IAPS is a multilateral agreement between the United Nations Convention of 10 December 1982 on the Law of the Sea and the Agreement for the Implementation of that Convention relating to the conservation of species listed in the ICP.
It aims to conserve and manage these stocks and to ensure their sustainable management.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This regulation applies to all fishing vessels, including purse seine vessels, that use hydraulic, mechanical or electrical systems and that fish for species listed by the Convention in the area north of 23°N and south of 30°S, and the area bounded by the coastline at 2°N, west to 2°95°W, south to 15°S-85°W and south to 45°S.
Limits on fishing for certain species are set out in the annexes to the regulation. These include:
the closure of the fishery for tropical tunas from 9 October to 8 November of each year;
the restriction of the fishing of sharks by purse seines from 9 July to 8 October and from 9 November to 19 January
the prohibition of fishing on data buoys;
restrictions on the release of sharks into the sea;
preventing the capture, capture, transport and sale of sharks;
prohibition of the sale, transport, transport or sale of shark fins and fins;
protection of sharks from being released into the water;
safeguarding sharks by releasing them in the waterway;
protecting sharks by preventing their capture and killing;
ensuring that sharks are released in the sea in a manner that is safe for the welfare of the sharks and the sharks’ caretakers;
providing scientific and technical assistance to the International Commission for the Conservation of Tropical Tunas (IATTC) and the Commission’s Scientific and Technical Cooperation Centre (SCTC);
ensure that the IATTC has the necessary resources to carry out its work.
Implementation
Each EU country must notify the Commission of any modifications to the information concerning elements listed in paragraphs 1 and 2 of the regulation by 31 March each year. The Commission must forward that information to the IACTS Secretariat without delay.
In addition, each EU country is responsible for ensuring that the Commission receives all the information it needs to implement the regulation, including the results of scientific research programmes.
Monitoring
The Commission must send a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 5 years on the implementation of this regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The convention entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
International Agreement on species conservation and sustainable management (International Association for Species Conservation & the International Committee for the Prevention of the Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Animals).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 86:
Better law-making — tools for the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission
Better law - tools forthe European Parliament and the Council
SUMMARY OF:
Common Understanding on Better Law-Making
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The common understanding aims to improve the effectiveness of the European Union’s (EU) legislative and regulatory systems by providing the EU with tools for better decision-making.
The decision concludes the common understanding on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The agreement applies to all EU legislation, including delegated or implementing acts, within the limits of the Treaties.
Delegated acts are delegated by the European Commission to the EU countries’ competent authorities. They are used in an efficient, transparent manner and in justified cases, they are an integral tool for better law-Making, contributing to simple, up-to-date legislation and its efficient, swift implementation.
They are published in the L series of the Official Journal (OJ L 55 of 28.2.2016).
The European Commission may not use delegated acts for its own purposes, except in exceptional circumstances.
Implementation of delegated acts
Implementing acts are implemented by the Commission in accordance with its delegated powers.
Disputes between the Commission and EU countries over implementing acts are settled by a dispute resolution mechanism.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
The Common Understanding entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Better Law-making’ is a key principle of the Treaty on European Union (TEU).
For more information, see:
‘Better law making’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Delegated act: a delegated act is an act adopted by an EU country on the initiative of the Commission or by the EU country of the same name.

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiatives for the implementation of the EU’s Treaties on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and on the Council of the Regions
Citizens' initiatives for implementing the European treaties on the functioning of the Community
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2004/2003 — citizens’ initiative for the implementing of the Treaties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a procedure for citizens to approach the European Commission with a request inviting it to submit a proposal for a legal act of the Union for the purpose of implementing the treaties.
It aims to ensure that citizens have the right to participate in the decision-making process of the Commission.
KEY POINTS
Organisers
The regulation applies to all citizens who wish to take part in an initiative to implement the EU treaties, whether or not they are citizens of an EU country or not.
The initiative must be supported by a minimum number of signatories.
To be eligible to support a proposed initiative, signatories must be citizens of EU countries and be of the age to be entitled to vote in elections to the European Parliament.
Signatories may only support a given proposed initiative once.
Activities
The organisers of the initiative must set up an online system to collect statements of support from citizens. They must then send the statements to the competent authorities of the participating EU countries.
They must also send the organisers a copy of the statement of support form to verify that the organisers have complied with all the relevant procedures and conditions.
If the organisers do not have the necessary information to carry out the verification procedure, they must notify the competent national authorities of their failure to do so within 3 months of receiving the request.
National authorities must verify the statements by checking that they are genuine and that they have been sent by the organisers.
Organisations must also provide the national authorities with the information they need for the verification of the statements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2004.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Activities’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Citizens' initiative: a proposal to implement a treaty on the working of the Council or the Commission by means of a citizens' initiative.
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Commission Regulation (EU) No 434/2004
1.7.2004

Summary 88:
Statistical statistics on the placing on the market and use of plant protection products
Statistical analyses on the putting on the markets and the use of pesticides
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1107/2009 on the statistical production of Community statistics on pesticides
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the systematic production of EU-wide statistics on pesticide use.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers substances listed in Annex III contained in pesticides placed on the European Union (EU) market in each EU country.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with reports on the quality of the data transmitted as well as the methods used to collect the data.
Data collection, transmission and processing
Eurostat collects the data by means of a number of methods:
administrative sources, or,
a combination of these means, including statistical estimation procedures on the basis of expert judgements or models,
data collection and processing by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The data are transmitted to Eurostat by the EU countries within 15 months of the end of the reference year.
Quality report
Each EU country must supply Eurostat with a quality report indicating:
the methodology used for collecting the data,
relevant aspects of quality according to the methodology used,
the description of estimations, aggregations and exclusion methods used, and
the results of the quality checks carried out.
The Commission must submit a report on the implementation of the regulation to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years.

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — authorisation and safety
Planting protection products – authorisation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2002 on the authorisation of plant protection products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
    б  б  ганскакованнака банка — the European Union (EU) has introduced a system for authorising and supervising the manufacture, marketing and placing on the market of products designed to protect plants against the adverse effects of active substances, safeners or synergists.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products intended to protect plant health against the harmful effects of certain active substances (e.g. pesticides, herbicides, fungicides, insecticides, etc.).
It does not apply to products intended for use in the production, marketing or placing of foodstuffs.
It applies to active substances*, safeners*, synergists*, adjuvants*, plant protection product* and plant protection packaging.
Authorisation
The European Commission must grant authorisations to manufacturers of plants protection products to ensure that they comply with the requirements of this regulation.
The Commission must ensure that the products are safe and that they meet the safety requirements of the legislation.
EU countries may authorise products containing active substances only when it is necessary to control that serious danger to plant health in their territory.
An active substance is considered to be a ‘foodstuff’ if it fulfils the criteria of a foodstuff.
Active substances must meet the following criteria:
It must not be used for the purpose of killing, harming or destroying plants
It should not be administered to humans
It is not intended to be used to kill, injure or damage plants
The active substance must be used only for plant protection purposes
It may be substituted for an active substance if the safety of the active substance, safener, synergist or adjuvant does not justify the use of an inactive substance.
Derogations
An inactive substance may be derogated from the list of substances which are considered a food substance if:
it has been shown to pose a risk to human health;
it does not pose a danger to the health of plants or to the environment
it is not used in the manufacture of food or feedstuffs intended for human consumption.
A derogation may also be granted if the following conditions are met:
the active substance does not present a serious health or environmental danger to plants or the environment;
the safety of plants and the environment is not compromised;
there is no evidence that the product poses a serious risk to plants, the environment or human health.
Applying for an authorisation to manufacture, market or place a product
An applicant must submit an application to the Commission and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) for authorisation.
If the Commission considers that the applicant has met the criteria for the application, it grants authorisation within 3 months of receiving the application and must inform the EFSA of the reasons for the decision. If the EU country considers that an applicant has not complied with the conditions for granting authorisation, it may refuse to grant authorisation for a period of 3 months.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2002.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS

Summary 91:
Fisheries management system for the Mediterranean and Black Sea (GFCM Agreement)
Fishery management system to ensure compliance with the rules of the common fisheries policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2011 — implementation of the GFCM agreement on fisheries management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ fisheries management systems are in place to ensure the sustainable exploitation of living marine resources in the Mediterranean Sea and the Black Sea at levels which are considered sustainable and at low risk of collapse.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of an EU country in the waters of the Mediterranean, Black Sea and connecting waters, as defined in the preamble to the agreement.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EU) No 1005/2008 (see summary).
It sets out the rules for the operation of the EU fisheries management system (FMS) in the area covered by the agreement, as well as the measures to be taken by EU countries to prevent the overfishing of certain species of fish.
The EU countries must ensure that their national fisheries-related information systems allow for the direct electronic exchange of information on port state inspections between them and the Executive Secretary of the European Commission, taking due account of appropriate confidentiality requirements.
EU countries must also carry out inspections in their designated ports of vessels of at least 15 metres in overall length authorised to fish in the agreement area.
Port State inspections must be carried out in fisheries restricted areas;
Vessels flying EU flag FADs are prohibited from 1 January to 14 August of each year.
An EU countries may prohibit vessels flying their flag from fishing in these areas if they can demonstrate that:
the fishing vessel is not complying with the conditions of the agreement;
the vessel is carrying out an illegal fishing operation;
there is a risk that the vessel may be involved in illegal fishing in the restricted areas.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 20 October 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to implement the agreement since its entry into force.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing effort management’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fishing effort management system: a system that ensures that fishing vessels fishing in an area is managed according to the rules laid down by the EU countries.

Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection
Unitary Patent protection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2012 on the granting of unitary patent protection to patents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted with the same set of claims in respect of all the participating EU countries benefit from the same level of protection.
It establishes the European Patent Office (EPO) Register for unitary patents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to patents granted by the European Commission, the European Atomic Energy Community (EPC) and the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to patents issued in the EU countries of the European Economic Area (EEA), the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) or the EU’s single market.
The European patent with unitary effect applies to all patents granted in the EPC, the EEA, the EFTA and the EU as a whole.
A patent granted in a participating EU country has unitary protection in all EU countries, irrespective of whether it has been granted in that country or not.
EU countries must notify the EPO of the decision to grant the patent and the reasons for granting the patent.
In the post-grant phase, patent proprietors must pay a single annual renewal fee for a patent. The fee is progressive throughout the term of the patent protection and must be paid by the patent proprietor.
Penalties for infringements are based on the number of patents granted.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
The EU countries had to incorporate the regulation into national law by 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EPC and EFTA have been in force since 1 January 2013.
For more information, see:
‘Unitary patents’ on the European patent website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 95:
Protection of the EU’s banana sector against imports from Central American countries
Protection against the importation of bananas from Central America
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on the protection of the banana sector
Decision (EU) 2018/852 on the conclusion of the Agreement on the Protection of the Banana Sector
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to protect the EU banana sector from imports of bananas originating in Central America (known as the ‘banana sector’).
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope of the agreement
Under the agreement, the EU has the power to impose safeguard measures on imports of certain agricultural products originating in the Central American country of bananas. These safeguard measures are designed to prevent serious injury to the EU industry.
The measures may take one of the following forms:
a suspension of a further reduction of the rate of customs duty on the product concerned provided for in the Tariff Elimination Schedule;
an increase in the rate on the import of the product to a level which does not exceed the lesser of:
the most-favoured-nation (‘MFN’) applied rate of import duties; or
the MFN applied rate for the product in effect at the time the measure is taken.
Imposition of safeguard measures
The EU countries are allowed to apply safeguard measures in critical circumstances where there is sufficient prima facie evidence that imports of a product originating in a Central America have increased as a result of the reduction or elimination of a customs duty in accordance with the tariff elimination schedule and such imports cause serious injury, or threat thereof, to the Union industry. The measures may be applied for more than 200 calendar days.
Prior surveillance
EU countries must notify the European Commission and interested parties of any such extension and explain the reasons therefor. The Commission must also present an annual monitoring report to the European Parliament and to the Council on updated statistics on imports from the Central America countries of bananas and those sectors to which monitoring has been extended.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
  
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Agreements on protection against imports of agricultural products from the countries of Central America and on the stabilisation mechanism for bananas (European External Action Service) (European Food Safety Authority)
Agriculture and Rural Development (EFSA) Agreement (European Union).

Summary 96:
Preservation of a debtor’s bank account or accounts — judicial cooperation in civil matters in the area of freedom, security and justice (Protection of a bank account and accounts in the EU)
Preserving a debtor's bank account/accounts in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the protection of the bank account & accounts of the debtor
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that a debtor who has been unable to pay a creditor is able to obtain an order to preserve his or her bank account* or accounts.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the conditions for a court to issue a ‘preservation order’.
The creditor must have obtained a judgment, court settlement or authentic instrument in order to obtain a preservation order.
A creditor may also require the debtor to provide security in the form of a security deposit or an alternative assurance, such as a bank guarantee or a mortgage.
Before issuing the order, the creditor must provide the debtor with the following information:
a description of the subject matter of the case and the electronic version of the form to be used for the declaration pursuant to Article 25 of the regulation;
the bank or entity that the creditor is responsible for the preservation of the accounts;
an estimate of the amount of the funds in the account;
a list of the assets and liabilities of the creditor.
If the creditor does not provide the information, the court may refuse to issue the order on the grounds that it is inadmissible or unfounded.
An appeal against a refusal to issue an order may be brought by the debtor.
In the event of a refusal, the debtor may appeal to the competent court of the EU country in which has jurisdiction to rule on the substance of the matter in accordance with the relevant rules of jurisdiction.
Where the creditor has not yet received a judgment or court settlement, the decision concerning the security may be reviewed on the ground that the conditions or requirements of that Article were not met.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
 * Preservation order: a court order to prevent a debtor from losing their bank or account.
* Creditor: a person or entity who is a creditor of a person, entity or legal entity.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  бъбранска гарка  года   ванка
Council Regulation (EC, EC) No 550/2008 of 20 December 2008 on the Protection of a Bank Account & Accounts in the Community (Protective of a Banking Account and Accounts) (Codified version) (OJ L 183, 21.12.2008, pp. 1-8)

Summary 97:
Border surveillance at sea — preventing and combating illegal border crossings
Border surveillance on the external sea borders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 on border surveillance at the external borders of the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
It sets out the rules for border surveillance operations carried out by EU countries at their external seas borders in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the Member States of the European Union (Eurosur).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all measures taken by Member States or the Agency in accordance with this regulation. It does not apply to border surveillance carried out in the territorial sea or the contiguous zone of the Schengen area.
Objectives
The purpose of border surveillance is to prevent and combat illegal border crossing.
The objective of the regulation is to:
prevent and combat unlawful border crossings;
impose sanctions on persons who cross the border illegally;
take other measures to apprehend and prosecute those who cross illegally.
Implementing measures
EU countries must ensure that:
border surveillance is carried out rapidly and effectively;
the competent authorities of the participating EU countries have the necessary resources to carry out the operations;
measures are proportionate, proportionate and dissuasive;
there is a high level of protection for the safety and security of the persons concerned.
A coordination between EU countries and Eurosurveillance units is established within the EU.
Coordination
The European Border Surveillance System (EuroSur) is responsible for the coordination of the border surveillance activities of EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLYEARS OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Directive 2014/13/EU on the application of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2014 on the protection of fundamental rights and the principle of non-refoulement
 
REFERENCESCAPTS

Deadline for transposition in the Member State

29.4.2014

Amending act(s)
Entry to force
Entry and exit procedure
Entry procedure
Exchange of information
Exchanges of information between the host and participating EU country
Interception of a vessel suspected of being engaged in illegal activities at sea
Detection
The participating units must collect and report information about any vessel suspected to be engaged in unlawful activities at the sea, which are outside the scope of the sea operation, to the International Coordination Centre, which transmits that information to the national coordination centre of the host EU country.
Search and rescue
The competent authorities must provide assistance to the persons on board of the vessel in the event of a search and rescue situation.
If the vessel is found to be in distress, the vessel must be rescued and the persons rescued must be handed over to the host country or to a neighbouring EU country, provided that they are not in danger of being killed or seriously injured.
Rescue coordination
If a vessel is rescued, the competent authorities may take all appropriate measures to ensure the safety of the person on board, including:
restoring the vessel to its original position;
rescuing the vessel’s crew;
returning the vessel or persons to their country of destination;
recovery of the bodies of the rescued persons;
restraining the vessel;
disseminating the vessel and its crew to the authorities of a non-EU country;
exchanging information on the vessel, its crew and passengers;
assisting the rescue coordination centre with the relevant authorities of an EU country in the case of a rescue operation;
providing assistance to persons in distress;
taking all necessary measures to rescue the persons and their bodies to the rescue centre;
cooperating with other EU countries to ensure that the persons are rescued;
supporting the rescue and recovery operations of persons who are missing or presumed to be missing or feared missing;
ensuring that the rescue operations are carried out safely and effectively.
In addition, the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) has a role in the operation of Eurosur.
Amendments to the regulation
The Commission has adopted a number of amendments to the original regulation. These amendments have been incorporated into the original text. These include:
Amendment to the Regulation on the Protection of Fundamental Rights and the Principle of Non-Refoulement of the Persons on Board of a Vessel
Implementation of Decision 2014/15/EU of the Council of 26 May 2014 on the implementation of Decision 2013/852/EU (see summary)




Summary 98:
European Union external assistance instruments (EU-EASI) — principles and procedures
European Union (EU)-EasI) external assistance mechanisms
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 883/2013 on the principles of assistance and the procedures for implementing them
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the principles, procedures and procedures for the implementation of the European Union’s external assistance programmes.
It aims to reduce the administrative burden for beneficiaries, the EU countries and the European Commission, taking into account the principle of proportionality.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom) and non-EU countries. It does not apply to non-euro area countries.
EU countries are responsible for:
ensuring that beneficiaries receive the full amount due promptly;
providing them with information and publicity on the EU external assistance programme;
monitoring and evaluating the programmes;
assessing the effectiveness of the programmes and ensuring that beneficiaries have access to the information they need to make informed decisions.
The European External Action Service (EEAS) is responsible for administering the EU-EAs.
Responsible authorities
The EU country responsible for implementing an EU programme must designate a responsible authority. This authority must be independent of the EU country in which the programme is being implemented. The authority must have the powers to:
implement the programme,
monitor and evaluate the programmes,
assess the results of the monitoring and evaluation,
take decisions on whether to extend the programme or to suspend it;
ensure that beneficiaries are provided with information on the programmes they are receiving;
take appropriate action to prevent fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the EU’S financial interests.
Eligibility
The beneficiaries are eligible for EU assistance if they meet certain criteria, including:
being a national of an EU country;
being an EU citizen or permanent resident;
having a minimum level of education, training or vocational qualification.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EU external assistance (European Commission).

Summary 99:
European Asylum and Migration Fund (EMF) (2013-2020)
European asylum and migration fund (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 on the European Asylum Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide mechanism to provide financial support to EU countries to help them manage migration flows.
It aims to contribute to the efficient management of migration flows and to the implementation, strengthening and development of the common policy on asylum, subsidiary protection and temporary protection and the common immigration policy, while fully respecting the rights and principles enshrined in the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (CFR).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EMF supports EU countries’ efforts to:
contribute to migration management;
ensure a uniform and high-quality asylum policy and apply higher standards of international protection;
support the effective functioning of the Common European Asylum System (CES), which encompasses measures relating to policy, legislation, and capacity-building, while acting in cooperation with other EU countries, EU agencies and third countries.
Objectives
The objectives of the EMF are set out in the regulation’s annexes.
The EU countries must:
prepare and approve the draft work programme of activities, notably with regard to the objectives of financial support and the thematic priorities and indicative amounts of the budget for each action;
set up the general framework for the implementation of eligible actions;
assess the available financial resources and their distribution;
establish the principles and mechanism for the establishment of common EU resettlement priorities;
provide support for the European Migration Network.
Eligible actions
EU countries must take the necessary measures to resettle:
persons from a country or region which has been identified in the UNHCR resettlement forecast and where EU common action would have a significant impact on addressing the protection needs;
people belonging to a specific category falling within the UNHCR refugee resettlement criteria.
Resources
The amount of EUR 2 752 million is allocated to the EU countries for the period 2013-2020.
Each EU country must allocate at least 20 % of those resources to the specific objective referred to in the annexes of the regulation, and at least 80 % to the other objectives.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    бакованскабрашка грака  годна бъларскога варка, гласного крага  храски горовка and гшарнока данной ганкой  канца,  бчкома и гнаста правновна  and  търалномa гунканиг генника.
BACKGROUND
The EAF is part of the EU's common migration policy.

Summary 100:
The EU’s external borders and visa — EU-wide action to protect people and goods from increasingly transnational threats (2014-2020) (European External Action Service)
The European External Action Services (EEAS) is the European Union (EU) external action service (EAS) for the fight against cross-border, serious and organised crime and transnational organised crime.
ACT
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 515/2014 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 April 2014 establishing as part of the Internal Security Fund, the instrument for financial support for external borders.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out a framework for EU countries’ national programmes to combat transnational crime and terrorism.
It aims to support the work carried out by EU countries' competent law enforcement authorities to prevent, combat and eliminate transnational criminal activities.
KEY POINTS

promoting cooperation between judicial authorities and law enforcement;
encouraging the use of EU IT systems and national IT systems;
providing financial support to EU countries to implement their national programmes.

the fight against organised crime,
countering terrorism,
fighting organised crime in the context of the internal security strategy for the EU (‘Internal Security Strategy’) and
the prevention and combating of transnational crimes,
support for the protection of persons and goods against transnational terrorist activities,
the protection of EU citizens and the movement of goods within the EU.

Summary 101:
Joint management of stocks of common interest — measures to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal fishing (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Joint managing of stocks and fisheries — measures for preventing, deter, and eliminating illegal fishing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on joint management of stock and fisheries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fisheries are managed in a sustainable way.
It sets out the type of measures that EU countries may take to prevent and deter illegal fishing.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the management of fish stocks and fishery products by EU countries. It does not apply to fishing by EU vessels, or to production or consumption within the EU, of fish and fisheries products made of or containing such fish of the species for which the measures have been adopted.
The measures are based on objective criteria and are equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
The European Commission may adopt implementing acts determining whether the conditions laid down in the regulation have been complied with and, where necessary, providing that the measures adopted in respect of the country allowing non-sustainable fishing cease to apply.
EU countries must notify the Commission of the reasons for the identification of that country as a country allowing not sustainable fishing and the possible measures that may be taken in relation to it pursuant to this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements with non-EU countries — mutual recognition of the EU’s exclusive competence (TFEU)
Bilateral investments — mutual acknowledgement of the European Union's exclusive competence
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2011 — bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU has exclusive competence with respect to the negotiation and conclusion by the EU of bilateral investments with third countries with a view to the progressive replacement of the bilateral agreements notified under Article 2 of the TFEU.
It lays down the conditions for the conclusion of such agreements.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any bilateral investment agreement with a third country that contains provisions on investment protection. It does not apply to:
investment agreements concluded between the EU and its Member States or between the European Economic Area (EEA) countries or the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) countries;
agreements concluded between EU countries and the European Investment Bank (EIB) or the EEA countries.
EU countries must notify the European Commission within 90 days of receiving a notification of a request for an investment agreement. The notification must include a copy of the agreement and the name of the country concerned.
The Commission must take its decision within 90 months of receiving the notification.
Authorisation to open formal negotiations
The EU country must inform the Commission without undue delay of all meetings which will take place under the provisions of the investment agreement and must provide the Commission with the agenda and all relevant information concerning the topics to be discussed at those meetings. The Commission must also provide the EU country with the opportunity to present its views on the agenda of the meetings.
If the negotiations have resulted in an agreement, the Commission must make a decision on whether to sign and conclude the agreement.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The agreement entered into force on 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The TFEEU is the single market for goods, services and capital in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Bilateral Investment Agreements’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: any agreement between a European Union (EU) country and an EU country or an EEA country on the negotiation or conclusion of a bilateral investment contract.

Summary 103:
Generalised Scheme of Preferences (GSP) — rules for the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 732/2008 on the generalised scheme of preferences (SSP) (see summary).
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — generalised system of preferences
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for implementing the generalisation scheme of preferential trade measures (SOPs), known as the GSP.
The SOPs are designed to help developing countries to benefit from the benefits of the EU’s preferential trade policies.
It aims to promote sustainable development and good governance in developing countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to a number of categories of products, including:
cabbages, cauliflowers, kohlrabi, kale and similar edible brassicas;
onions and shallots, fresh or chilled;
chickpeas and other alliaceous vegetables;
fruits and vegetables other than tomatoes;
seeds and other legumes;
nuts and hazelnuts;
marshmallows;
eggs and other cereals;
salt and sugarcane products;
fruit juices;
powdered milk;
milk;
watermelons;
coconut oil;
dairy products.
Beneficiary countries
A list of beneficiary countries is established in an annex to the regulation.
Tariff preferences
The tariff preferences granted to beneficiary countries apply for 2 years after the date of application of a preferential market access arrangement.
Safeguards
The European Commission must ensure that:
the beneficiaries of the tariff preferences are informed of the measures taken to protect them;
the measures taken are proportionate, proportionate and dissuasive;
tariff preferences do not discriminate on the basis of nationality, race, colour, religion, sex, ethnic origin or sexual orientation;
there is no discrimination on the grounds of origin, colour or religion.
Implementation
The Commission must publish a notice in the Official Journal of the European Union and notify the beneficiary country of any temporary withdrawal of tariff preferences under the urgency procedure.
If the Commission finds that the reasons justifying a temporary withdrawal no longer apply, it may adopt delegated acts, in accordance with Article 36 of the regulation, to amend Annex III in order to reinstate the preferential tariff preferences provided under the special incentive arrangement for sustainable development.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/856 has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a wide range of trade policies, including the common foreign and security policy (CFSP).
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
Generalised scheme: a system of trade measures designed to encourage the development of developing countries by providing them with preferential access to the EU market.

Summary 104:
Export of firearms, their parts and essential components and ammunition — export controls and measures
Exporting firearms, parts and necessary components and measures for the export of firearms
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — measures to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and combat the illicit manufacture, importation, export and transit of firearms in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the export, processing and control of firearms and their parts to the EU.
It does not apply to transfers of firearms for civilian use within the EU, while this regulation focuses on measures in respect of export from the customs territory of the acquisition and possession of weapons.
EU countries must ensure that:
the importing country has authorised the shipment of firearms;
the exporting country has notified the exporting country of any objection to the shipment; and
the third countries of transit have given notice in writing or at the latest prior to shipment that they do not object to the transit.
Importers must obtain a European Firearms Pass (EFC Pass) from the competent authorities of the EU country where the exporter is established.
The EFC Pass must be accompanied by the relevant customs importation documents and must be issued by the competent customs authorities of that EU country.
An export authorisation must be granted by the country of origin. The exporter has the power to determine the sending of the item out of the country where it is established and the person responsible for sending it out of that country must have the power for determining the sending out the item.
If an export contract has not been concluded or if the holder of the contract does not act on its own behalf, the person who has the decision-making power must be notified of the decision to send the item to the exporting EU country by means of a written contract or by electronic means.
Exporters may not export firearms which have been rendered permanently unfit for use by deactivation, ensuring that all essential parts of the firearm are rendered permanently inoperable and incapable of removal, replacement or modification that would permit the firearm to be reactivated in any way.
In order to obtain an EFCP, EU countries must take into account all relevant considerations including, where appropriate:
their obligations and commitments as parties to the relevant international export control arrangements or relevant international treaties;
considerations of national foreign and security policy, including those covered by Common Position 2008/944/CFSP;
measures as to intended end use, consignee, identified final recipient and the risk of diversion.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The implementing regulation entered into force on 3 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The UN Firearms Protocol, the purpose of which is to promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among EU countries in order to prevent the illicit production, import and export of weapons and related products, is the basis for the implementation of this regulation.
For more information, see:
The United Nations Firearms Protocol (UN Firearms Protocol).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 447/2017 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 105:
EUROSUR — situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of the EU countries
EurOSUR – situational awareness & reaction capability in order to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration and cross-border crime
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 establishing the European Union External Action Service
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) external border surveillance agency (EurosUR).
It aims to provide EU countries with the infrastructure and tools needed to improve their situational awareness, reaction capability and border control capabilities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ external borders.
It does not apply to the surveillance of air borders as well as to checks at border crossing points if EU countries voluntarily provide such information to EUROSUR.
EU countries are responsible for their own internal border control systems.
EURoSUR is responsible for detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration.
The European situational picture (ESP) is an integrated set of information on terrain and weather conditions at the EU country’s external borders which is used by EU countries to identify and respond to incidents. It includes information on:
incidents and other events contained in the events layer of the national situational picture;
in the operational area of a joint operation, pilot project or rapid intervention coordinated by the agency.
National coordination centre
The national coordination centre coordinates the common application of the ESP and the common pre-frontier intelligence picture.
Monitoring
The ESP operates twenty-four hours a day and seven days a week.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Directives 2014/54/EU and 2014/56/EU have applied since 31 December 2014. Directive 2014/55/EU had to become law in the EU Member States by 31 December 2015.

Summary 106:
Eurodac — fingerprint database for law enforcement and criminal justice purposes (2013-2020)
EuroDac — fingerprints database for legal and criminal proceedings (2013 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — setting up a computerised central database of fingerprint data
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Eurodac database for the purpose of preventing, detecting and investigating terrorist offences or other serious criminal offences.
It aims to ensure that the data collected by EurodAC are used only for law-enforcement purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up a central database for fingerprint data.
The database is operated by the European Agency for the Protection of the Rights of Persons in the European Union (EAPEU).
The European Agency is responsible for the operational management of EuroDac.
EU countries’ designated authorities are responsible for processing fingerprint data for the purposes of law enforcement, criminal justice and criminal judicial proceedings.
Euros are processed by the competent authorities of the EU countries for the following purposes:
to identify suspects, perpetrators or victims of terrorist offences;
to prevent, detect or investigate terrorist offences and other serious crimes;
for the purposes in which Euros are used to identify suspects and perpetrators;
in the context of criminal proceedings; and
to detect, detect and investigate criminal offences and serious crimes.
In addition, EU countries must ensure the security of the data before and during transmission to the Central System as well as the security and confidentiality of data it receives from the central system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has set up a system for the processing of fingerprints in order to prevent and detect terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Euros’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fingerprint database: an electronic database of fingerprints.
ACT

Summary 107:
Protected designations of origin and protected geographical indications
Protected designs of origins and protected geographic indications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1257/1999 on the protection of the names of products linked to a geographical area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to protect the names and symbols of products that are linked to an area of the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure fair returns for producers of these products by:
ensuring uniform protection of their names;
providing clear information on the value-adding attributes of the product to consumers.
KEY POINTS
Protection of designations
The regulation applies to the names, symbols and indications of products which:
are linked to the geographical area of origin of an EU country;
are produced from raw materials or ingredients that are those traditionally used.
It does not apply to products produced in non-EU countries.
The protection of these names is based on a number of factors, including:
the origin of the products;
the characteristics of the raw materials and ingredients used; and
the quality of the finished product.
To register a name, a producer must:
apply for a protected designation of origin or a protected geographical indication;
submit an application to the competent authority of the EU country where the product is produced;
apply to the European Commission for an EU symbol;
use the EU symbol on the labelling of traditional specialities guaranteed which are produced outside the EU.
A name may not be registered if it conflicts with a name of a plant variety or an animal breed and is likely not to be registered as protected designations or geographical indications.
EU symbols
The Commission may adopt implementing acts laying down detailed rules on the form and content of the register.
Labelling
The European Commission publishes a list of the protected names and the EU symbols that can be used on products.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    данска барка  года
It has applied since 1 July 1999 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2001.
BACKGROUND
 
Protected designations and geographical indications (PDOs)
See also:
‘Protected Designations of Origin and Protected Geographical Indications’ on the European Food Safety Authority’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* PROTECTED DESIGNATIONS OF ORIGIN: an EU name or a geographical indication that designates a trans-border geographical area or in the case of a traditional speciality, a geographical feature of a product that is linked to that geographical area.
Protective geographical indication (PGI): an EU geographical feature that is associated with a geographical region or a product of that geographical region.
Traditional specialities: a product which has been traditionally produced in a particular EU country for more than 100 years.

It aims to reduce the safety gap between new and existing ships by upgrading and/or phasing out older ships.

Summary 109:
European System of Financial Supervision (ESFS) — rules for the supervision of the financial sector in the European Union (EU)
European system of financial supervision (EFSM) — checks for the prudential oversight of financial institutions in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1092/2010 on the structure of the European system of supervisory authorities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for supervising financial institutions across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The ESFS is a system of EU-wide supervisory bodies responsible for ensuring that financial institutions are properly supervised and that they do not run afoul of the EU’s financial rules.
It is designed to:
ensure the stability of financial markets;
facilitate cross-border cooperation between EU countries;
provide the European Commission with information on financial institutions;
support the European Banking Authority (EBA), the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Insurance and Reinsurance Board (EIRB) in their work in the field of prudential supervision.
The regulation lays down the tasks of the ESFS:
supervise financial institutions operating within the EU;
monitor and assess systemic risk;
implement technical standards;
assess and respond to micro-prudential trends, potential risks and vulnerabilities;
make recommendations to the ESRB and to the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission;
take decisions on delegated powers;
act as a mediator between the EBA and the other authorities;
draw up a management board;
set up an internal committee to assist in the decision-making process;
report to the management board every 2 years on the activities of the ESCFS.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The ESCFS has applied since 1 January 2011.

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement for workers in the European Union (EU) — Employment and equality of treatment
Freedom of mobility for workers within the EU (EU): Employment and equal treatment
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1612/68 on the freedom of movement of workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that workers who wish to pursue an activity in the territory of an EU country can do so without any discrimination on grounds of nationality.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 1611/68.
KEY POINTS
Employment
Any national of a EU country and any employer pursuing an activity may exchange their applications for and offers of employment, and may conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action in that EU country.
The regulation does not apply to conditions relating to linguistic knowledge required by reason of the nature of the post to be filled.
Employers may not discriminate against workers on medical, vocational or other criteria which are similar to those applied to nationals of the other EU country when pursuing the same activity.
Workers must be provided with information concerning living and working conditions and the state of the labour market as is likely to be of guidance to workers from other EU countries.
An Advisory Committee composed of representatives of the EU countries meets at least twice a year and is chaired by the Commission or by a member of the Commission.
EU countries must ensure that children who are eligible to attend vocational training are able to do so under the best possible conditions.
Children under the age of 18 must have the opportunity to take part in vocational training courses in the EU country in which they wish to work. EU countries must encourage all efforts to enable such children to attend these courses under the same conditions as their parents.
National workers who are employed in another EU country must enjoy all the rights and benefits accorded to national workers in matters of housing, including ownership of the housing he needs.
A worker who is employed in a non-EU country and who wishes to move to that country must also be regularly informed of working conditions in that country and of the conditions under which he or she can exercise his or her rights.
Equality of treatment for workers
The European Commission has set up a Technical Committee and an Advisory Committee to deal with issues relating to the employment and employment rights of workers. The Technical Committee comprises representatives of EU countries and the Advisory Committee consists of representatives from each EU country, and is composed of at least one third of the members. The term of office of the committee is 2 years.
Vacancies and applications for employment
The Commission has the general task of promoting vacancy clearance at EU level.
Each EU country sends to the European Office for Coordinating the Clearance of Vacancies and Applications for Employment vacancies it receives directly to the Commission and informs the Commission of the action taken thereon.
In addition, the Commission must send to the specialist services of each country and to the EU Coordination Office such information as is necessary to ensure the proper functioning of the European labour market.
Measures for controlling the balance of labour market
The EU has adopted a number of measures to control the imbalance of labour markets. These include:
measures to encourage employers to hire more workers;
measures to reduce the number of vacancies and to improve the quality of employment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive 2014/33/EU has applied since 1 January 2014.
Directives 2014/34/EU and 2014/36/EU have applied since 31 December 2014. Directive 2014/35/EU had to become law in the Member States by 31 December 2015.
BACKGROUND
European Union (European Commission).
For more information, see:
‘Workers’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/68 of 30 November 2008 on the right of freedom of mobility of workers within a Member State and repealing Council Decision (EC), Euratom (OJ L 168, 30.11.2008, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Eurat) No 608/68 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
