Summary 111:
Ozone depletion — EU rules on the safe use of ozone-depleting substances
Ozone pollution — EU controls
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 450/2008 — the EU’s rules on controlled substances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a uniform approach to the safe and sustainable use of certain substances to reduce the harmful effects of ultraviolet-B radiation resulting from the depletion of the ozone layer in the atmosphere.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 552/2006, which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2014.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the production, processing, storage, recovery, disposal and disposal of substances, including hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HFCs), which are a constituent of ozone layer ozone depletion (Ozone Layer Protection Protocol).
It does not apply to certain substances, such as chlorinated biphenyls (CPSs) which are used in the manufacture of pharmaceuticals, cosmetics, foodstuffs, cosmetics and food additives, as well as to certain chemicals used as process agents (e.g. pesticides, fertilisers, pesticides, etc.).
It applies to imports, exports, exports and imports of substances and products containing more than 6 kg of HFCs.
Importers and exporters must ensure that:
the substances are checked for leakage at least once every 6 months;
the products and equipment are repaired or repaired at least 3 times a year;
they are destroyed within 45 days of their release for free circulation
the chemicals are destroyed by the most environmentally acceptable destruction technology;
products and equipment containing HFCS are destroyed only by approved technologies listed in Annex VII of the regulation.
The European Commission may amend Annexes to the regulation in order to take new technological developments into account.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/34/EU has applied since 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has to protect human health and the environment by limiting the adverse effects of ozone depletion on human health, the environment and the health of animals and plants.
For more information, see:
‘Ozone’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hydrofluorocarbon: a naturally occurring substance that is a component of the stratospheric ozone layer.
ACT
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/35/EU of 20 January 2014 on the recast and repeal of Regulation (EC, Euratom, EEC, EC, EC), EEC and EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 December 2014 concerning the safe, sustainable and responsible use of controlled substances (OJ L 347, 20.1.2014, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS
Commission Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 of 20 December 2018 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the control of the release of substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses and the placing on the market of substances intended for such uses and repealing Decision (EURatom) 2015/853 of the Commission and Commission Regulation (CEC) 2015-852 (OJR) No 853/EEC) (See amending act(s) below).

Summary 112:
European Environment Agency (EEA) — European information and observation network (EEO-N)
European environment agency (EAE-N)* — European data and information network
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1210/90 — setting up the European Environment Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
    б   —  
It establishes the European Union (EU)’s (now the EU) EEA — a network of environmental information and monitoring centres.
It aims to ensure that the public is properly informed about the state of the environment, and to provide the necessary technical and scientific support.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up the EEA for the purpose of:
providing the public with information and information on the environmental situation in the EU;
collecting and analysing data on the environment;
assisting the European Commission and the European Parliament in their work in this field.
The EEA has a management board and an executive director. The management board is composed of representatives of the EU countries and is responsible for the management of EEA activities. The EEA also has a scientific committee, which delivers opinions on any scientific matter concerning the Agency and may submit its opinion to the management board or the Executive Director.
Each multiannual work programme must be drawn up by the Management Board for a period not exceeding 3 years. The programme may be adjusted in the course of the year by the same procedure. It must become final following final adoption of the general budget of the European Communities.
EEA activities are divided into 3 main areas:
environmental information and communication — information on environmental issues such as air, water, soil, land, air, land and sea;
chemical substances — hazardous substances which are hazardous for the environment and coastal and marine protection.
These activities are carried out by EEA topic centres, which are designated by the Agency for a 3-year period. The topic centres are responsible for collecting, analysing and disseminating information on:
the environmental situation of EU countries;
the environment and energy situation in EU countries, including energy, transport, energy, water and air;
natural resources, such as forests, lakes, rivers and seas;
marine and coastal resources, including marine protected areas;
pollution, air and water pollution, including air pollution, air pollution and water;
human health and safety, including the protection of human health and the environment.
In order to achieve these objectives, EEA programmes are designed to:
give the public the information they need to make informed decisions;
provide the Commission with the information it needs to carry out its work;
contribute to the development of EU policies and programmes;
ensure that EU countries have the necessary resources to implement their environmental policies.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2002.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for setting up EEA-N since 1 January 2001.
For more information, see:
‘EEA’ on the European Agency for the Protection of the Environment's website.

Summary 113:
Environmental management systems for small enterprises
Environmental management system for small organisations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2002 on environmental management systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the environmental management system (EMAS) for small businesses in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EMAS is a voluntary system designed to encourage small businesses to set up their own EMAS systems. It aims to reduce administrative burdens for businesses and to improve their environmental performance.
The regulation sets out the conditions for organisations to register with EMAS.
Organisations must:
ensure that their EMAS system is up-to-date and that they have the necessary resources to meet the requirements of EMAS;
provide an annual report to the competent authorities of the EU country in which they are registered;
have an annual programme of activities to demonstrate that they are meeting the EMAS requirements;
assess the impact of their activities on the environment, human health and the environment in a systematic way;
establish and maintain an internal environmental audit;
check that their systems are in place and that their activities do not have significant environmental impacts;
apply for EMAS accreditation and licence and ensure that their organisations comply with all the requirements.
An EMAS organisation must register with an EMAS competent body. The competent body is responsible for verifying that the organisation complies with the requirements and that it has the resources necessary to meet them.
A competent body ensures that:
the organisation meets all the relevant EMAS obligations;
that the organisation has the appropriate resources to carry out its EMAS activities;
it has the necessary staff to implement EMAS projects;
environmental information is available on the organisation’s website;
the competent body carries out an annual audit of the organisation and checks that it is in compliance with all EMAS objectives;
organisations that do not meet all the required requirements are subject to a derogation from EMAS registration.
If a competent body considers that an organisation does not comply with the relevant requirements, it may disqualify the organisation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    иганска гарациальна кародна  года баркалканфала варски горанки  кодскова мантаннога настица  форнаснаги коншани в харнока дандан грабановка, глассконнаказначках гчангенкаки, клажналнанаташкаафкар герадная гварфовна,  блакнарговсккая  бълховчшшинаа, ‘бхорчна’ гнадка чинстакки (борлшна) гкадстка ‘ххнэкатна ’ and ‘нхлчекамка‘ гудакцка.’
It has applied since 1 January 2003. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2005.

  * KEY TERMS
* Environmental management system: a system that ensures the environmental performance of an organisation and its activities.
* EMAS: an organisation has a system of environmental management that ensures that its environmental performance is maintained and that its activities have a significant impact on its environment, the environment or human health.

Summary 114:
Geographical indications for spirit drinks
Geographical indication for spirits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2010 — rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks and on the protection of geographical indications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out rules on how spirit drinks are defined, described, presented and labelled in the EU.
It aims to ensure that spirit drinks can be sold in the European Union (EU) without compromising their quality, reputation or other characteristics.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks, irrespective of their origin. It does not apply to wine, spirits or spirit drinks that are produced in the territory of a country, or a region or locality in that territory, where a given quality or reputation is essentially attributable to its geographical origin.
The rules apply to all spirits, spirits and all their components, regardless of their composition.
Distribution
Distributors must ensure that the spirit drinks they sell on the market comply with the rules laid down in the regulation. They must also ensure that:
the spirit drink is sold under the name ‘fruit spirit’ or ‘vegetable spirits’, as appropriate;
the product is labelled with the geographical indication ‘agricultural’ and ‘rum’ accompanied by any of the geographical indications of the French Overseas Departments and the Autonomous regions of Madeira as listed in Annex III to the regulation (Annex I);
the name of the country of origin of the spirit drink may be accompanied by a geographical indication of the region of origin;
a description of the method of distillation and of the place of production, if appropriate, and the name and contact address of the applicant.
Labelling
The label of a spirit drink must contain the following information:
an indication of geographical origin, as set out in Annex I of the regulation, in accordance with the Annex II of Annex III of Regulation (EU);
a definition of the category of spirit drink and of its components, as defined in Annexes III and IV of the Regulation;
where the product has undergone blending, the label must include:
Annex V of the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Food Safety and Consumers’ Protection Regulation (EFSPR);
An Annex VI of the EFSPR, as laid down by the Commission, sets out the rules for the labelling and marketing of spirits and their components.
Regulatory procedure
The European Commission adopts the regulation on behalf of the EU and the European Parliament and the Council. It repeals Regulation (EEC) no 1576/89.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2010/852/EU has applied since 20 February 2010 and had to become law in the Member States by 20 February 2011.

BACKGROUND
The spirit drinks sector is one of the most important sectors of the economy and is a key driver of the growth of the single market.
For more information, see:
‘Spirits’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Distilled spirits: spirit drinks produced by flavouring ethyl alcohol of agricultural origin, grain spirit and/or grain distillate with juniper (Juniperus communis L. or Juniperus oxicedrus L.) berries.
* Fermented spirits: spirits produced by distillation of grapes, fruit, vegetables or other natural botanicals in a spirit-making process.

Summary 115:
Safeguarding civil aviation: common rules for airport security and measures to prevent unlawful interference with civil aviation (Safecurity Regulation)
Safefocusing civil aviation
The European Union (EU) has adopted a common set of rules to protect persons and goods within the European Union against acts of unlawful interference that jeopardise the security of civil aviation.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 of 20 November 2008 establishing common rules on airport security
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out common rules to safeguard persons and cargo within the EU by establishing common security standards for airports, operators and entities applying aviation security.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all flights between the EU and non-EU countries. It does not apply to international flights.
It applies to:
passenger and baggage;
vehicles entering a security restricted area;
aircraft departing from an airport;
passengers and their baggage arriving on flights from non-European Union countries.
National civil aviation security programme
Each EU country must set up a national civil aviation safety and security programme. The programme must include internal quality control provisions describing how compliance with these methods and procedures is achieved.
The security programme must be reviewed every 5 years by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2008/53/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 October 2008 establishing a European Union Agency for Civil Aviation Safety and Security (EACAS) and amending Decision 1999/468/EC (see summary) has applied since 1 January 2009.
Directives 2008/52/EC and 2009/54/EC had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND

Aviation security (European Commission).
KEY TERMS

unlawful interference with a civil aircraft;
suspicious behaviour;
non-compliance with security standards.

Summary 116:
Freezing of funds and economic resources to combat serious human rights violations and abuses
Freezing funds and economically related activities
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/852 — freezing of funds, economic resources and assets of natural or legal persons, entities and bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in serious human-rights violations or abuses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision and the regulation (Decision) are part of the EU’s response to the situation in Yemen.
The regulation (Regulation (EU) 2019/853) sets out the list of persons, groups and entities subject to EU financial sanctions and the interactive sanctions map.
KEY POINTS
The decisions and regulations apply to the following persons and entities:
state actors;
other actors exercising effective control or authority over a territory;
non-State actors, subject to Article 1(4) of the Treaty on European Union (TEU);

the European Commission, the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom).
The Council, the Commission and Euratom have the power to impose sanctions on these individuals, entities or bodies in order to:
restrict the movement of funds or economic resources within the EU;
suspend, restrict or restrict the supply of goods or services to these individuals or entities;
ban them from entering or transiting the territory of an EU country;
impose restrictions on their movement within the territory.

the decision to freeze funds and the economic resources of individuals and entities responsible for or providing support for, or facilitating or encouraging, or otherwise engaging in serious violations of human rights or abuses, as well as those associated with the natural and legal persons or entities listed in Annex I of the decision;
decision to freeze the assets of individuals, companies or entities associated with, or providing financial, technical or material support to, or participating in, acts of serious human and/or legal violations, including by planning, directing, ordering, assisting, preparing, facilitating, or encouraging such acts.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE OPERATION?

Summary 118:
European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCG) — Faced with document fraud (FADO) (European Commission) — EBCG (European Parliament and the Council)
European border and coast guard agency — FADO (European Union Agency for the Protection of the Internal Market)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the European Border and Cart Guard Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a system to exchange information on authentic documents and on known methods of falsification between EU countries’ authorities.
It aims to fight against document fraud at the external borders of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The system is an electronic storage system describing possible detection points, both in authentic and false documents, in order to combat document fraud, in particular at the EU’s external borders.
The EU countries and the European Commission must transmit without delay to the EU Agency the information they have on the methods of detecting false documents and their recognition by EU countries, recommendations on effective ways of detecting them.
Agency responsibilities
The agency is responsible for the operation of the system and for ensuring that the information it receives is used for the purpose of preventing and combating document fraud.
In addition, the agency must ensure that the data it receives are used only for the purposes of preventing, detecting and combating fraud, and that it does not use the data to identify any natural person through the system.
Data protection
The Agency must take the necessary steps to pseudonymise all elements of personal data which are not necessary in relation to the purposes for which the data are processed in accordance with the principle of data minimisation.
Access to the system
The Commission and the Agency, to the extent necessary for the performance of their tasks, and the EU country authorities competent in the area of document fraud and other relevant national authorities, have secure access to the facility on a need-to-know basis.
Information on other related official documents
The facility may contain information on documents used in support of applications for official documents, issued by Member States and, where applicable, third parties, such as third countries, territorial entities, international organisations and other entities subject to international law, and on false versions thereof.
Use of the facility
The European Commission may use the facility in a limited manner:
for the protection of the internal market, other than those referred to in paragraph 2;
to prevent, detect and prosecute fraud;
in the event of fraud, for the prevention, detection and prosecution of fraud in the European Union, other EU countries or third countries;
on the basis of a request for information from the Commission or the European Parliament or the Council.
Implementation of the regulation
The regulation repeals and replaces Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
    *   ‘Faced with Document Fraud’ is a common term used by the EU countries to refer to documents that have been forged or counterfeited.
For more information, see:
‘Document fraud’ on the website of the European External Action Service (EEAS).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �   Манска ганца варскова  Васта бальна кантанна (Faced With Document Fraud) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2019, pp. 1-2)

Summary 119:
Access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions in the European Union
Access to and the pursuing of regulated professions within the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2005/59/EC on the regulation of professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries do not restrict the access to or pursuit of certain professions in order to protect the health, safety and welfare of consumers.
It sets out the criteria for assessing the proportionality of EU countries’ measures.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all professions, whether or not they are regulated by the European Commission. It does not apply to professions which are regulated in a non-EU country.
The scope of the directive is limited to the following professions:
physicians;
vocational trainers;
educators;
social workers;
administrative staff.
Regulation
EU countries must ensure that:
the regulation of a given profession does not restrict access to that profession;
it is justified by public interest objectives; and
it does not go beyond what is necessary to attain that objective.
Procedures
The European Commission must carry out an assessment of proportionality before introducing any new, or amending existing, legislative, regulatory or administrative provisions restricting access to professions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directives 2005/58/EC and 2006/36/EU have applied since 1 July 2005.
Directions 2006/35/EU and 2007/34/EU had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2007.
BACKGROUND
The Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU (‘the Charter’) guarantees the freedom to choose an occupation, as well as to conduct a business.
For more information, see:
‘Access to the professions’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Professional: a person who is a professional in a regulated profession, such as a doctor or a lawyer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2005/ 59/EC of 29 June 2005 on the Regulation of professions in certain Member States and repealing Directives 2001/60/EC, 2001/61/EC (Directive 2001/62/EC) and 2001/63/EEC (Regulation of the professions in various Member States)
Successive amendments and corrections to the Directive have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 120:
Management of the North Sea and Baltic Sea stocks of fish
Management of fish stocks of species
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the management of stocks of cod, plaice and sole
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the sustainable management of the stocks of certain species of fish in the north-east Atlantic and Baltic seas.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to stocks of:
cod,
plaice,
bivalve molluscs,
skeletons,
whales,
crab,
dolphin,
gill rays,
seabirds,
marine mammals,
sea turtles and
marine invertebrates.
It does not apply to stocks in the Baltic Sea, the Black Sea or the North-East Atlantic.
Management
The European Commission must draw up a management plan for each of these stocks.
The plan must be based on scientific advice and should indicate, in particular, ranges of FMSY and biomass reference points, i.e. the maximum sustainable yield of fish that can be harvested.
Fisheries
The Commission is responsible for drawing up the management plan. It must be submitted to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the European Union (EEC) every 5 years. The Commission then adopts delegated acts to ensure that the plan is implemented.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2014.

Summary 121:
Fishing for straddling fish stocks in the North-South Waterway (SPRFMO) — management, conservation and control of stocks
Fishing in the north-south Waterway
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — managing, conserving and controlling stocks of fish in the south-south waterway
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules for managing and controlling the stocks of certain species of fish found in the Southwestern Waterway of the North Sea and the South-East Waterway in the framework of the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of an EU country in the SPRFMO Convention Area.
It applies from 19 July 2018 to 19 July 2023.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
From 19 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has signed the UNCLOS convention on the law of the sea, which aims to ensure the conservation and management of the living resources of the oceans.
For more information, see:
‘The UNCLos Convention’ on the UN’s website.
KEY TERMS
Straddling species: fish that are found in two or more different parts of the same waterway.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 of 19 June 2017 laying down rules on managing and conserving stocks of fishes in the southern-southwest waterway (the South-South-south-east Waterway Convention)
WHAT DOES THIS REGULAMENDMENT DO?
This regulation lays down the rules for the management and conservation of fish stocks of the southern and eastern waters of the northern-west Waterway, in the context of the UN CLOS Convention.
The convention was signed by the EU countries on behalf of the European Community (now the EU).
It aims to conserve and manage fish stocks for which there is a high risk of overfishing.
By 15 July 2018, the EU had to submit to the Commission a list of vessels flying its flag that have been authorised to fish in SPRFisheries in the area covered by this regulation.
This list must be sent by 15 September each year to the European Commission, which then forwards it to the Secretariat of the International Commission for the Conservation and Management of Marine Living Resources (ICMNR).
The Commission then transmits the list to the secretariat, which forwards the information to the scientific committee of the ICMNR.

Summary 122:
Controls of cash entering and leaving the EU
Countries must impose controls on the movement of cash in and out of the European Union (EU) to prevent money laundering and terrorist financing.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005/EEC — controls of cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a system of controls with respect to cash entering or leaving the Union to complement the legal framework for the prevention of money laundering, terrorist financing and money-laundering.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to cash in the form of cash on the person’s person, in their luggage or in the means of transport in which they cross the external borders of the EU. It does not apply to cash on their luggage.
It defines ‘cash’ in the following ways:
the amount of cash must be declared to the competent authorities of an EU country;
the value of the cash must not exceed €10,000;
cash must be accompanied by a statement of reasons and must adequately describe the specific factors that have given rise to the action.
The competent authorities must notify the European Financial Intelligence Unit (EFU) of any cash movements suspected to be related to criminal activity.
Controls must be imposed on natural persons entering or departing the EU and must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
National authorities must inform the European Anti-Fraud Office (EFTA) and the European Border and Coast Guard (Eurojust) of the measures they have taken to detect and prevent cash movements.
the competent authorities in the EU countries have the power to impose penalties for infringements of this regulation.
EU countries must ensure that information campaigns are carried out to inform the public about the measures taken to prevent and deter criminals and their associates from circumventing the obligation to declare or disclose cash.
Information campaigns must be carried out in cooperation with the European Commission and the Eurojust network.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Ваксандароднакка барка  бълишкаканкабанскашанцангананна гарна  шфаногацка
It has applied since 26 October 2005 and had to become law in all EU countries by 26 October 2018.
BACKGROUND
The reintroduction of illicit proceeds into the economy and the diversion of money to finance illicit activities create distortions and unfair competitive disadvantages for law-abiding citizens and companies, and are therefore a threat to the functioning of the internal market.
For more information, see:
The fight against money laundering (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cash: a highly-liquid store of value that can be converted into currency while incurring only modest transaction costs.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Бачкалка/2005 / EEC of the Council of 26 September 2005 on controls of the movements of cash (OJ L 183, 26.9.2005, pp. 1-8)

Summary 123:
Energy Union — national energy and climate plans
Energy Union – national energy, climate and transport plans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 517/2014 on the energy union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a framework for the development of national energy policies and strategies to meet the EU’s energy security, energy efficiency, decarbonisation and research, innovation and competitiveness objectives.
It aims to ensure that each EU country has a national energy policy and strategy to meet these objectives and targets by 2020.
KEY POINTS
The energy union covers five dimensions:
energy security;
the internal energy market; energy efficiency; decarbonising;
research and innovation (R&I).
Each EU country must draw up an integrated national energy plan for each of the five dimensions.
The plan must include a breakdown of the relevant factors affecting primary and final energy consumption, including:
the total volume of each type of fuel or energy supplied;
energy efficiency;
carbon capture and storage (CCS);
carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions;
transmission of CO2 to the atmosphere;
climate change.
Each country must also provide the Commission with a report on the progress it has made towards achieving its objectives and climate targets.
National policies and measures
The Commission is responsible for drawing up the national energy plans and for implementing them. It has delegated the power to the European Commission for the period 2014-2020.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The Energy Union is the European Union (EU) policy framework for energy security and energy efficiency.
For more information, see:
‘Energy Union’ on the EU's website.

Summary 124:
Release reduction, minimisation and elimination of persistent organic pollutants (POPs)
Release reduction and minimisation of POPs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — reducing the release of certain substances into the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to reduce the amount of substances that can be released in the environment by reducing their production, storage and use.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 852/2004, which had to become law in the EU countries by 16 July 2021.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all substances listed in Annex III of the Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants (see summary).
It does not apply to substances that are present in mixtures or articles that have been dissolved in water, air or soil.
The list of substances includes:
perfluorooctane sulfonic acid and its derivatives (PFOS) and its metabolites (PFOA, PFOA and PFOB);
piperfluorocarbons (PFCs);
chlorinated biphenyls (PCBs) and their derivatives (PPCBs, PFCs and PPCBs);
polymers (PPBs, PPBs and PPBs;
polymer-based solvents, PPCs, PPPBs and PPPs;
pesticides (PPS, PPSBs, NPPs, NPSs and NPPBs); and
polycyclohexic acid and PPSs.
Annexaluminium chloride (PCL) and PCLs are also listed in the annex to the regulation.
EU countries must set up or maintain, as appropriate, monitoring programmes and mechanisms for the regular provision of comparable monitoring data on the presence of substances as listed in Part A of Annex III.
Monitoring
The European Commission, supported by the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW) and the European Union Agency for the Prevention of Pollution from Chemicals (EAP) are responsible for the monitoring of the presence and release of substances in the environmental environment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
  дародансканка барка  бодка, боралкага гарца and годна   горовка have applied since 16 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Stockholm Convention is one of a series of international agreements that aim to protect human health and the environment against persistent organic pollution.
For more information, see:
‘Living well, within the limits of our planet’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products — reducing plastic waste on beaches and in the environment
Single-used plastic products – reducing plastic wastes on the grounds of human activity
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/883 on the reduction of plastic waste in the seas and on land
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce plastic waste by reducing the amount of plastic on the European Union’s beaches and on the land.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all plastic products which are placed on the market in the EU and which are not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling.
It does not apply to:
food containers with dried food or food that is sold cold requiring further preparation, containers containing food in more than single-serve portions or single-serving portion-sized food containers;
personal care products;
tampon pads and applicators;
pre-wipes and personal care products.
EU countries must ensure that producers of these products cover the costs of cleaning up litter. They must also establish deposit-refund schemes to compensate consumers for their waste.
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts laying down the format for reporting data and information in accordance with points (a) and (b) of paragraph 1 and (c) of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 3 April 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The plastic waste directive is part of a package of measures designed to tackle the problem of plastic pollution on EU beaches.
For more information, see:
‘Plastic waste on the EU’ on the website of the European Commission's Directorate-General for the Environment and Energy.
* KEY TERMS
* Single-use product: a plastic product which is not designed to be reused or recycled and which is intended to be thrown away or thrown away.

Summary 126:
European Union Intellectual Property Office (EU IPO)
European Union IPO
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 179/2008 — establishing a European Union intellectual property office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal framework for the protection of intellectual property rights (IPR) in the European Union (EU) against infringements of the EU trade mark (EU trade mark) by third parties.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC, Euratom, ECSC) No 259/68.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU trade marks, whether they are a trade mark, collective mark, certification mark, guarantee mark or guarantee mark. It does not apply to:
the use of the trade mark in a form differing in elements which do not alter the distinctive character of the mark in the form in which it was registered, regardless of whether or not the mark is also registered in the name of the proprietor;
affixing of the trademark to goods or to the packaging thereof in the EU solely for export purposes;
the packaging of goods or the placing of the goods on the market for the purpose of export purposes.
Implementation
The EU IPO is independent in relation to technical matters and has legal, administrative and financial autonomy. It is responsible for the implementation of the implementing acts of the regulation.
Rights of proprietors
The proprietor of an EU trademark has the right to use the EU trademark in the following ways:
to use the trademark in any way that does not alter its distinctive character;
to attach the mark to the goods or packaging of the products for the purposes for which it is registered, irrespective of whether the mark was used in the original form;
for the purposes of marketing, advertising or promotional purposes; or
to place the mark on the packaging of a product for which the trademark has been applied.
The rights of the licensor
The licensor may bring proceedings against third parties for infringement of the rights of their proprietor if they believe that they have been infringed by a third party. The proprietor may also bring proceedings for infringement if they consider that the third party has infringed the rights conferred on him or her under the licensing contract.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The European Union IP Office has been operating since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The Commission has adopted a number of measures to modernise and modernise the EU’s intellectual property system, including the creation of the European Trade Mark Register (ETR) and the establishment of a European Patent Office.
For more information, see:
European Trade Marks (European Commission).

Summary 127:
Access to published works for persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled
Access to Published Works for persons with disabilities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the implementation of the Marrakesh Treaty
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the accessibility of books and other printed material to persons who have disabilities.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright and related rights for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to books, other printed materials, audio books and radio broadcasts.
Accessible formats include, for example, Braille, large print, adapted e-books and audio books.
Authorised entities
An authorised entity established in an EU country may distribute, communicate or make available to beneficiary persons or to an its primary activities, institutional obligations or as part of its public-interest missions for non-commercial purposes.
The authorised entity may use the personal data of beneficiary persons referred to in Article 6.
Exceptions or limitations
The exemption or limitation to copyright or related rights may be limited to:
the content of the work;
the authorisation of the author; or
the quality of the content.
Export of accessible format copies to non-EU countries
EU countries may also allow for the importation of, and access to, accessible formats made in accordance with the regulation, from a third country, by beneficiary persons in the EU and authorised entities established in a EU country, for noncommercial purposes for the benefit of persons who do not have the capacity to read printed works.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    барканска гаранка  года  
It has applied since 11 October 2017.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Authorised entity: a public institution or non-profit organisation that provides the same services as beneficiary persons as one of their primary activities or institutional obligations.
* Accessible format: a format that allows a person with disabilities to read a printed work without the use of their eyes to the extent that would normally be normally acceptable for reading, insofar as, as a result of such impairments or disabilities, those persons are unable to read the printed works to substantially the same degree as persons without such disabilities, or to the same extent as persons with the ability to hear the printed work.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EU), of 11 September 2017, on the application of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) to the implementation, on behalf of the Member States, of Article 6(1) (ex Article 6 TFEU) (OJ L 140, 11.9.2017, pp. 1-5)

Summary 128:
Enforcement of EU laws that protect consumers’ interests — mutual assistance mechanism
En enforcement of EU law that protect citizens’ rights — mutual aid mechanism
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — mutual assist mechanism
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities, together with the European Commission, cooperate effectively in the enforcement of the EU’s consumer protection laws.
It lays down the rules for mutual assistance between EU countries and the Commission.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that the competent authorities have the means and powers to cooperate effectively with the other public authorities in the application of this regulation.
The regulation sets out the powers of competent authorities and the single liaison offices (SOLOs).
The SOLOs are responsible for:
collecting and analysing documents, data or information on suspected infringements of EU consumer protection law;
providing information to the other EU countries, the Commission and the European Consumer Centres, consumer organisations and associations;
ensuring that the information is protected by the protection of individuals’ personal data;
coordinating the actions of EU countries to address infringements within their jurisdiction;
assessing the impact of the infringement on the EU market and ensuring that the penalties for infringements are proportionate.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices — EU rules for their design and certification
In vitro diagnostics — EU guidelines for their development and manufacture
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules for the design, certification and marketing of in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union has a uniform regulatory framework for the development, certification, marketing and sale of medical devices.
It sets out the rules for designations, certificates, performance studies, vigilance and market surveillance.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medical devices designed and manufactured in the EU. It does not apply to medical devices intended to diagnose, treat, diagnose or cure disease.
The rules apply to:
medical devices intended for use in the laboratory, in the home or in the workplace;
diagnostic devices used for the diagnosis, treatment or prevention of disease;
in vitro diagnostic diagnostic devices used to diagnose diseases;
electromagnetic interference devices used in medical devices;
therapeutic devices for the treatment, diagnosis and prevention of diseases.
Risk classification, conformity assessment and notification
The European Commission decides on a case-by-case basis whether or not a specific product, category or group of products falls within the scope of the regulation.
Designation and notification procedure
Manufacturers must designate a designated body to carry out conformity assessments and notify the European Commission of the results of the assessments.
A notified body must draw up its final assessment report which includes:
the result of the assessment,
the corrective and preventive actions taken and,
any remaining diverging opinion with the joint assessment team and
the recommended scope of designation.
Notified bodies must make publicly available a list of their subsidiaries.
Monitoring
The Commission must carry out market surveillance to ensure the safety and effectiveness of the devices and equipment covered by the regulation, including:
monitoring the use of the products and equipment;
checking that the devices comply with the relevant safety and health protection measures;
assessing whether the devices are compatible with other devices and/or general purpose equipment.
In addition, the Commission must keep a register of authorised distributors and authorised medical device distributors.
Quality management
The competent authorities of the EU countries must ensure that manufacturers and distributors carry out their conformity assessment activities with the highest degree of professional integrity and the requisite technical and scientific competence in the specific field and that they are free from all pressures and inducements, particularly financial, which might influence their judgement or results of their activities, especially as regards persons or groups with an interest in those activities.
EU countries must also ensure that competent authorities have the necessary resources to monitor and monitor the market for the products covered by this regulation, and that the competent authorities carry out the necessary checks.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    данска ванта грашка  бакова  
It entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
In 2015, the European Parliament and the Council adopted the European Medicines Agency’s (EMA) In vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices Directive.
For more information, see:
‘In vitro medical devices’ on the EMA's website.

SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the procedures and procedures for assessing the conformity of electrical and electronic products in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the sale of:
electromagnetic compatibility products;
electrical compatibility products.
It does not apply to:
gas appliances;
gas heaters;
air conditioners.
The manufacturer, having detailed knowledge of the design and production process, is best placed to carry out the conformity assessments.
Importers must ensure that:
the appliance or fitting meets all the requirements of the regulation;
the manufacturer has carried out a conformity assessment and notified the competent authorities of the EU country concerned;
all the necessary documentation has been provided by the manufacturer or the importer.
Manufacturers must notify EU countries of the results of their conformity assessments and notify them of any non-compliance with the regulation.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any measures taken by them to ensure that non-conformity is withdrawn from their market and inform the Commission accordingly.

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash by road
Transport of euro banknotes by road (CIT)
Transportation of euro notes and euro coins
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 on the cross-border cross-Border transport of cash-in-transit
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that professional cash handlers can transport euro notes by road across the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules for the professional transport of euros by road between participating EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
banknote transport;
euro coins by road;
professional cash handlers (i.e. cash handlers who are employed by a company established in another EU country to fulfil the national requirements for obtaining a professional weapons licence or authorisation);
cash deliveries/pick-ups that a CIT vehicle may carry out in a host EU country during the same day.
It does not apply to the transport of other types of cash, such as:
currency notes;
currency coins;
banking notes; and
euro banknotes that have been withdrawn from the euro area’s national central banks.
Rules for cross-banking
EU countries must ensure that the rules laid down in the regulation are complied with, including via random inspections without prior notification to the CIT company.
Companies holding a licence may transport euro banknote transport by road using an unarmoured vehicle equipped with IBNS, provided that the following conditions are met:
the vehicle is of ordinary appearance;
there are at least 2 CIT security staff per vehicle;
the CIT vehicles are equipped with a global navigation system;
it is driven by a driver with a valid driving licence;
its driver is a national of the host country.
The company must also have a licence issued by the national authorities of the EU country in which it is established.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?

Summary 133:
Issuing of euro coins and collector coins
Issuing and circulationisation of euros
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuing and circulationization of coins in the euro area.
KEY POINTS
EU countries may issue two types of euro coin: circulation coins and collectors coins.
The circulation coins are legal tender in all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
Collector coins are not legal tender, but may be put on the market above face value if justified by the special quality of the coin, a special packaging or any additional services provided.
Issuance of circulation coins
Each EU country may issue 2-euro commemorative coins to celebrate specific subjects, subject to limits set per year and per issuing EU country for the number of issues of such coins. The issuances of collector coins must be accounted for on an aggregated basis in the volume of coin issuance to be approved by the European Central Bank.
In order to avoid that euro collector coins are destroyed by one EU country while there may be a need for such coins in another, EU countries should consult each other prior to the destruction of them.
Decision whether to issue commemorative or circulation coins with a common design collectively issued by all the EU countries must be taken by the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
  
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘The euro and the euro’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — investment priorities and operational programmes (Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013)
European Social Funds (ESFs) — investments priorities and operating programmes (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 1081/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the mission and scope of the EU’s social investment fund, known as ESF, as well as the investment priorities it supports.
KEY POINTS
The ESF is the main instrument for the European Union (EU), the European Economic Area (EEA) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) to promote social inclusion, combating poverty and any discrimination in the EU.
It is funded by the European Investment Fund (EIF), the EAF, the EEA and the EFTA.
The objectives of the ESF are to:
promote social inclusion;
combat poverty;
improve the quality of life of disadvantaged people;
encourage the development of sustainable and inclusive communities;
contribute to social innovation and transnational cooperation.
Funding
The EIF is financed by the EU countries’ national governments, the European Development Fund (EDF) and other financial instruments. It is managed by the managing authority of the EIF.
Eligible beneficiaries
The EU countries may decide to allocate a limited amount not exceeding 10 % of the funds under the Youth Employment Initiative (YEI) to young persons residing in sub-regions which experience high youth unemployment levels and which are outside the eligible NUTS level 2 regions.
Operational programmes
The operational programmes are designed to support a number of thematic objectives, including:
the thematic objective ‘promoting social inclusion’;
the investment priorities ‘community-led local development’ and ‘transnational cooperation’.
In addition, operational programmes may set out priority axes for the implementation of social innovation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
European social fund (European Commission).

Summary 135:

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the production, production and dissemination of European statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out how EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the population and on their vital events for the previous year.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must send the Commission data every year on their usually resident population and their vital event statistics to Eurostat.
Each year, EU countries provide Eurostat with the following data:
live births by sex, month of occurrence, live birth-order and mother’s age;
deaths by age, sex, year of birth, region of residence, country of birth and country of citizenship;
other vital events, such as deaths by death, business, medical treatment or religious pilgrimage.
The data must be transmitted to the Commission by the end of the reference year (midnight of 31 December of the previous reference year).
The Commission must keep the European Statistical System Committee (ESSC) informed of any changes to the data sources, definitions and estimation methods used for the first reference year, and EU countries keep Eurostat informed on any changes thereto.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 31 August 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for producing and disseminating European statistics for the EU countries since 1 January 2013.
For more information, see:
European statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* EU countries:
countries, regions, cities, towns, villages, rural regions, mountain regions and mountain and island regions.

Summary 136:
Textile products — labelling and labelling of certain textile products
Textile Products — labeling and marking of certain textiles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 — textile products — rules for the labelling, labelling or marking of textile products and their packaging
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the laws of the Member States of the European Union (EU) relating to textile products.
It repeals and replaces the previous acts on textile products which had been amended several times.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all textile products, including:
fleece wool or virgin wool products;
wool or virgin cotton products; and
wadding.
The textiles covered by the regulation must bear the following information:
the name of the producer or importer of the product;
the number of fibres and the weight of the fibres, followed by their total percentage by weight.
Labelling
The textile products covered by this regulation must be labelled in accordance with the following rules:
inclusive labelling: the textiles must bear a number indicating the value of ‘d’, ‘e’ or ‘m’ in the case of wool, and ‘c’ for cotton.
the textiles may contain non-fibrous matter, such as fats, waxes or dressings, or water-soluble matter, either occurring naturally or added to facilitate processing.
labelling and marking
the textile products must be accompanied by the following:
annexes to the regulation:
a list of the names of the producers or importers of the textile products referred to in Annexes I and II;
a description of the products covered in Annex III of the regulation;
instructions for the production, processing, storage, transport and disposal of the finished textiles;
information for consumers concerning the quality and safety of the textile products covered under Annexes III and IV of the legislation;
exchanges of information between the producers, importers and retailers concerning the products listed in Annex I and Annex III;
contact details of the importers, distributors and retailers for the manufacture, storage and transport of the final textiles.
Annexes V, VI and VII of the Regulation (EC, EC, EC and EC) provide for the use of special methods to test the quality of textiles and for their packaging. These methods are based on the following methods:
methods for the analysis of fibre mixtures;
method for the measurement of the fibre content of the various fibres of the different fibres used in the manufacture of the same textile;
technological methods for the determination of the percentage of fibre by weight of each fibre in the final textile.
Importers must ensure that textiles are labelled with the information ‘textile products’ on the label of the packaging.
Manufacturers must also ensure that the label on the packaging of each textile product is clearly visible and clearly visible.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Textiles — labels (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Textile products: fibres are fibres that have been separated from other fibres to produce a single product.
* Wadding: a material that is attached to a textile by means of a wadding system.

Summary 137:
Small-scale coastal fishing — European Maritime Fisheries Fund (EMFF)
Small-sized coastal fishing – European Mediterranean Fisheries Fund(EMFF)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the European Maritime fisheries fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the EU’s common maritime fisheries fund, known as EMFF.
It aims to support the development of small-scale fishing in coastal areas of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The EMFF is the main source of financial support for small coastal fishing in the EU.
EMFF is managed by the European Commission, which is responsible for implementing the regulation.
The European Commission manages the EMFF and the EU countries that have a significant coastal fishing segment.
EU countries must set up an EMFF operational programme for each of their coastal areas.
Each EU country must draw up an action plan for the development, competitiveness and sustainability of coastal fishing.
In order to achieve this, EU countries must:
ensure that their operational programmes include measures to:
promote the sustainable development of coastal fisheries;
support the sustainable use of coastal resources;
encourage the sustainable utilisation of coastal waters;
provide information to the public on coastal fishing;
develop sustainable fishing techniques;
ensuring the sustainable management of coastal areas;
establish a network of fishery management organisations.
An EMFF management authority is appointed by each EU country to manage EMFF projects.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    б  
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle the impact of climate change on coastal fisheries.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable coastal fishing’ on the EU's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Small coastal fishing: small fishing is defined as fishing with fewer than 50 fish per square kilometre of coastal area.
Small coastal fishing means fishing with less than 50 fishing vessels.

Summary 138:
Insurance-based investment products for retail investors — key information document (PRIIP)
Insurance–based investment plans for retail consumers — KEY INFORMATION DOCUMENT
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 on retail investment products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the format and content of the key information documents (KIDs) that retail investors must receive before buying an insurance-backed investment product.
KEY POINTS
The KIDs are designed to enable retail investors to understand and compare the key features and risks of an investment product, such as the investment objectives, the investment targets and the financial instruments used to achieve them. They must be accurate, fair, clear and not misleading.
They must be provided by the PRIIP manufacturer to retail investors by means of either:
by means of a KID;
by a durable medium other than paper, where the conditions laid down in paragraph 4 are met; or
by way of a website where they are met.
Manufacturers must ensure that the KID is written in clear, understandable and comprehensible language that is easily understood by retail investors and that it is accompanied by a description of how the investment target is achieved, including the description of the financial instrument used, avoids financial jargon and terminology which is not immediately clear to retail investor.
The key information must be accompanied by:
a copy of the investment policies and its objectives;
a summary of the product’s risks;
an explanation of the risks associated with the product;
the description of investment objectives and investment targets;
information on how the product is designed to achieve those objectives and how they are achieved.
PRIIPs must also provide retail investors with information on the risks involved in investing in PRIIPs.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report on the implementation of the regulation in 2014.
For more information, see:
‘Investment products’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 139:
Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM) for financial services — rules for the resolution of failing banks
Single resolution Mechanism for financialservices — rules concerning the resolution and supervision of failed banks
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2013 establishing a single resolution mechanism for the financial services sector
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules and procedures for resolving failing banks in the European Union (EU).
It establishes the Single Resolution Board (SRB), a body composed of representatives of the EU countries, the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority (EIOPA).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out:
the tasks of the national resolution authorities of the participating EU countries;
the responsibilities of the ECB and the EIOPA;
rules for the establishment of the single resolution fund (SRF);
the procedures for the operation of the ESRB;
measures to be taken by the ECB to ensure that the resolution process is transparent and efficient.
Resolution procedures
National resolution authorities must draw up a resolution plan for a failing bank.
The resolution plan must include the objectives and principles of the resolution objectives, including:
restructuring the business;
promotion of the company’s long-term prospects;
improvement of its financial health;
reduction of the risk profile of the business.
National authorities must adopt a resolution decision within 3 months of receiving the resolution plan, unless they assess, taking into account the circumstances of the case, that they could achieve more effectively by taking actions which are not provided for in the resolution plans.
If the national authorities do not adopt the resolution decision, they must inform the ECB of the reason and inform the EIB of their decision.
When a national resolution authority decides to take control of a failing financial institution, it must notify the European Investment Bank (EIB).
The EIB is responsible for the management of the assets and liabilities of the failed financial institution and for ensuring that they are transferred to a bridge institution that meets the minimum requirements for own funds and eligible liabilities. The EIB also provides the necessary resources and expertise to manage the bridge institution.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/59/EU has applied since 1 January 2014.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) No 1094/2010 and (EU), Euratom have applied since 31 December 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures to strengthen the stability of the financial sector in the EU.
For more information, see:
European banking crisis (European Commission).

Act

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at EU airports
Noise - related to aviation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/852 on the introduction of rules and procedures for noise-related restrictions at European airports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to reduce the impact of air traffic on human health and the environment by limiting the number of flights to and from airports in the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals Directive 2002/30/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all aircraft engaged in civil aviation. It does not apply to aircraft operating in the airspace of the European Economic Area (EEA), the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) or the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA).
The rules apply to:
aircraft flying within the EU;
passenger aircraft flying between non-EU countries;
airport-operated aircraft flying from non-EFTA countries to non-EEA countries.
It applies to aircraft flying within EU airspace.
Rules on noise management
Air traffic noise impact will be described, at least, in terms of noise indicators Lden and Lnight which are defined and calculated in accordance with Annex I to Directive 2001/49/EC (see summary).
Airport operators and air navigation service providers must provide aircraft operators with aircraft noise and performance information for noise modelling purposes.
The European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts to adopt noise abatement operational measures.
Operational measures must be non-discriminatory in nature and must not discriminate on grounds of nationality, identity, race, religion, gender, age, sexual orientation or sexual orientation.
Regulatory measures
The Commission must notify the relevant competent authority if it finds that the process followed for the imposition of a noise restriction does not meet the requirements of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 13 June 2016 and had to become law in the EU countries by 13 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce air traffic noise in the area of the EU’s airports.
For more information, see:
‘Air traffic management’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Air traffic noise: the noise emitted by aircraft flying in the air during the flight from an airport to an airport in the territory of an EU country and the noise produced by those flights.

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings — rules for the opening, conduct and closure of insolvencies (Regulation (EC) No 552/2001)
Cross-Border insolveny proceedings — ways for the closing of insolvent companies (Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 652/2002 and (EU) 2017/852)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EC) no 552 / 2001 — rules governing cross-border proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that insolvenic proceedings can be opened in any EU country, irrespective of the country in which the debtor is resident.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all types of legal proceedings, including:
proceedings for the liquidation of a company;
prosecutions for the transfer of assets;
actions deriving directly from or closely linked with the proceedings.
It does not apply to:
contracts relating to immoveable property, such as patents, copyrights, trademarks, etc.;
other types of contracts relating to the sale, transfer or disposal of assets.
Decision to open proceedings
The decision to open insolvent proceedings must be made by the court of the EU country where the debtor has the centre of its main interests.
Procedure for lodging claims
Creditors are required to lodge their claims in order to have their claims taken into account in the proceedings, except in cases where they are not required to do so.
Right to give an undertaking
The creditor has the right to make an undertaking to avoid the proceedings if they wish to avoid them.
The undertaking is valid for a period of 5 years. It may be renewed or renewed for similar periods.
Cooperation and communication between courts
The court must cooperate and communicate with each other on the operation of the proceedings and with other interested parties.
Jurisdiction
A court has jurisdiction to decide on the jurisdiction of the court in which proceedings are opened. The court has the power to rule on the application of the law of an EU country.
If the court does not have jurisdiction, the court has to decide whether the court should have jurisdiction.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Directive 2001/46/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2001 on the establishment of a European Union insolvent company (OJ L 183, 28.10.2001, pp. 1-2)
Directives 2001/45/EC and 2001/48/EC have been repealed and replaced by Directive (EU), Euratom and Regulation (EU).
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report in 2014 on the implementation of the regulation. It concluded that the regulation is functioning well in general but that it would be desirable to improve its application of certain of its provisions.
For more information, see:
‘Insolvency’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
    ‘Regulation [EC] No 553/2001 of the Parliament and the Council on the rules governing the opening and conduct of insolvents’ (recast)

Summary 142:
Carriage of passengers by sea — liability rules for damage caused to passengers
Carriage by sea – liability rules to compensate passengers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on liability rules relating to the carriage of passengers on board ships
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down liability rules on the liability of the European Union (EU) Member States for passengers who are injured or killed in the event of a maritime accident.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the liability rules that the Member States must apply when carrying out their obligations under the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) Athens Convention for the Community (see summary).
The liability rules apply to all persons who are harmed or killed on board a ship under the jurisdiction of the Member State in which the accident took place.
Carriers are liable for the loss of or damage to passengers, unless the carrier proves that the incident which caused the loss occurred without the fault or neglect of the carrier. The burden of proving fault or negligence lies with the claimant.
The carrier is also liable for death and personal injury.
Insurers and guarantors are responsible for protecting passengers against damage caused by the carrier or the performing carrier.
Limits of liability
The carriers and the performing carriers are not liable for:
the loss suffered as a result of the loss or damage of cabin luggage;
the damage suffered by passengers in relation to the damage to luggage other than cabin luggage, but the carrier is liable for that loss;
damage caused by damage to the cabin luggage of a passenger who is not the person entitled to damages.
Liability of the insurer
The insurer and the guarantor must guarantee that passengers are entitled to a minimum amount of compensation.
Unit of account and conversion
Each passenger must have an account of at least 250,000 units of account per passenger on each distinct occasion.
A certificate must be issued in the official language or language of the issuing State.
An insurance policy must bear the name and address of the insurance company or guarantor.
If an insurance policy does not bear this name or address, the insurer must provide the claimant with a copy of the policy.
Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/852 amends Regulation (EC, Eurat) No 550/2008 and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 648/2008.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    данска бакова ганца
It has applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Liability rules’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person who is injured, killed or seriously injured during the operation of a ship or on board it.

Summary 143:
European rail network for competitive freight — management and use of railway infrastructure (European rail networks for competitive transport)
European rail system for competitive delivery of goods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2001 — management of railway infrastructures for the development of European rail networks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a European rail network that is competitive for freight transport.
It sets out the management and management procedures for the management of the infrastructure for this purpose.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the management of rail infrastructure for freight in the EU;
the development of the EU’s internal rail market;
traffic management of freight infrastructure;
management of freight corridors.
Management procedures
Each EU country must designate a management board responsible for defining the general objectives of the freight corridor, supervising and taking the measures to implement those objectives.
The management board for a freight corridor must designate or set up a joint body for applicants to request and to receive answers regarding infrastructure capacity for freight trains crossing at least one border along the rail corridor (hereinafter referred to as a ‘one-stop shop’).
A management board must draw up a programme of measures to improve the performance of the rail network, including bottlenecks, including the essential elements of the study and the programme of actions necessary for creating the corridor, and the objectives for the infrastructure.
An executive board is responsible for setting up the management board.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
The European Commission has the power to adopt and implement the regulation.

Summary 144:
Road transport operators’ professional qualifications
Road transport professionals’ personal data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 on the professional qualifications of road transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
    бакованска грашка — the European Union (EU) sets out the requirements for road transport professionals.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the conditions to be met by undertakings and transport managers in order to satisfy the requirement of good repute.
To meet the requirements, undertakings must:
have at least a minimum level of professional qualification;
have a minimum number of years of experience in the transport sector;
be familiar with road transport operations;
provide the competent authority with all relevant documents relating to each transport operation, and in particular those relating to the vehicle, the driver, the goods and luggage;
keep the relevant documents on the premises of the transport undertaking;
notify the competent authorities within 28 days or less of any changes to the data contained in the national electronic register.
The competent authorities must ensure that:
the competent authorities of the EU country in which the undertaking or the transport manager is located have the necessary documents to carry out their tasks;
the relevant authorities have access to the relevant national electronic registers;
all relevant documents are kept in a secure place.
EU countries must also ensure that competent authorities carry out the necessary checks to ensure that the documents covered by the regulation are in compliance with the relevant legislation.
In addition, EU countries must establish a system of checks to check that undertakings comply with the requirements of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has set up the European Transport Agency (ETA).
For more information, see:
‘Road transport’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 145:
Social security — mutual recognition of benefits and access to benefits in different EU countries (SARS)
Social Security — Mutual recognition of beneficiaries in different European Union (EU) countries (Social Security)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the mutual recognition and access of social security benefits and contributions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that people who have been victims of an accident or disease in one EU country are entitled to receive benefits in kind in another EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for mutual recognition* of benefits for victims of accidents and diseases in the EU.
Beneficiaries are eligible for benefits in all EU countries, irrespective of the EU country to which they are resident.
It does not apply to:
the EU countries where the person is resident;
the countries to which the person was resident before the death of their spouse or partner;
individuals who have left the EU for a period of more than 2 years;
other EU countries to whom the person has been resident for more than 3 years.
The social security system in each EU country is governed by national legislation.
A person who has been a victim of a serious accident or a disease is entitled to benefits under the national legislation of the country in which he or she is resident. If a person has received social welfare assistance in one Member State during a period in which they were entitled to benefit under the legislation of another Member State, the body which provided the assistance may, if it is legally entitled to reclaim the benefits due to the person.
In the event of a person who is no longer a resident in the Member State in which provided that assistance, the person concerned may be entitled to claim the benefits of the other Member State.
An EU country must notify the national authority of the person who was the victim of the accident and the institution responsible for paying the benefits to that person. The person concerned must provide the relevant information to the relevant national authority. The national authority must then inform the contact institution of the last Member State to whose legislation the claimant was last compulsorily subject before that period.
If the person does not provide the information, the national authorities of the two Member States must inform the institution of which he/she is a resident and inform it of the reasons for the refusal to do so. If the person fails to provide information, this Article applies at the initiative of the institution designated by the competent authority of that Member State of residence as soon as it is appraised of that person’s situation, possibly via another institution concerned.
Disputes concerning the occupational nature of the injury or disease may be settled by the body providing the assistance or the body that paid the benefits.
When a dispute arises between the two parties, the competent authorities of both parties must take the necessary actions to resolve the dispute.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
    и  баранскаганка гарка  года  
It has applied since 30 June 2004 and had to become law in the European Union countries by 30 June 2005.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Mutual recognition: when a person receives benefits in more than one country for the same period of time.
* Social security contributions: the amount of money that a person contributes to the system of benefits to cover the costs of living, working and social security.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �  ъ  таршак  банна карг горака
